The Red Page
Cocky Verses
“Billjim, and his mate from Maoriland
....” This extract comes from a “Plain
English” (13/1/T6). The name “Billjim,”
with its variant, “Jimbill,” is generally
taken to represent the typical Australian, or
in a more specialised sense, the typical
soldier Australian. But Billjim cannot
imply the Australasian; he does not include
the Maorilander —and this page has a sus-
picion that the Maorilander would not par-
ticularly care to be lumped even with Bill-
jini. Incidentally, the name “Billzac”
would include the inhabitants of the Narrow
Isles; but then there is again the possible
resentment of the Maorilander against thus
sinking his separate nationality. Well, it’s
up to the Maorilander to christen himself.
Hasn’t he got a name of his own? This
page throws out, as a bone for Billjim’s mate
to worry over, the name, “Beginaldsandy.”
Somehow, from this distance, he looks rather
like a “Beginaldsandy.” The subject is now
open for discussion and insults.
Vol. Ill, part 8, of J. H. Maiden’s Criti-
cal Revision of the Genus Eucalyptus (2s.
6d.), like the war, shows not the remotest
sign yet of coming to an end.
JIM AN' ME.
I! There was a durnce at Thompson’s place
The other night.
I was invited. So teas Grace.
(Oh! That’s all right.)
There was a darnce at Thompson's place,
An’ me an’ Jim, an’ Em an’ Grace
IVttS there. 1 thought it was a case:
An’ ivell I might.
I used, to think Jim was my friend,
An' so would you.
Wen hoik of us got on a bend
(As coves ’ll do) —
If each had got a pound to spend
An’ one run short, the ’tothcr’d lend,
An’ plank it for his pal, no end —
An’ quite right, too.
I darnced with Grace, an’ Em with Jim,
Most of the night.
I didn’t find no fault with him,
Though some coves might.
An’ w’en the moon was gettin' dim
We sat upon a leanin’ limb,
An’....I kissed Grace an’ Em kissed Jim,
Well, that's all right.
But me an’ Em’s engaged, yon know;
We was last year.
An’ Jim an’ me is pals — an’ so —-
Well, yes. It’s queer!
I ain’t a cove as likes to blow....
Em darnced with Jim. ’E ain’t too slow...
I iconder —? Blarm it! I don’t know!
('Eve's better beer!)
WEN THE WHEAT TURNS BROWN.
Sometimes I sits and wonders, like,
W’at time o’ year’s the best.
Autumn is good, some kind o’ ways:
Nothin’ to do but rest.
Falterin' comes — an’ seedin’, too,
Later on in the year.
Good limes, them is, with lots o’ work:
Stump-jumpin’ ploughs to steer.
Drivin’ a drill from morn to night —
Later on, rollin’ down.
But there’s a time as is .better still —
Better than drivin’ the plough or drill—
W’en you cun sit on the old sand-ill
Wat chin’ the w’eat turn brownl
Winter ain’t bad, to’en rains is on,
Bringin’ the crops along.
Talk about gramophones an’ thingsl
liain sings the farmer’s song!
Listenin’ to the rain all night!
Just think o’* that for fun!
Nothin’ to do but just lay loio,
’Avin’ your work all done!
Money a-growin’ w’ile you sleep:
Never no need to frown!
Still there’s a time as is “better beer”:
Just the best time in the bloomin’ year,
W’en you can sit do ion and ’are no fear,
turn_ hraijjnL __
Spring’s all right w’en the crop’s knee-high:
Most if it in th’ “boot,”
Wavin’ the “flag” in the moist west wind:
Stoolin’ well at the root.
Growin ’ an’ fillin’ out, day by day —
Promisin’ full “three bags”
(S’posin’ the “smut” and “rust” don’t come
Knockin’ it into rags!)
But summer’s the time you wouldn’t swap,
Not for a golden croicn!
That’s w’en you’re ’ummin' the ’arvest-
song,
’Appy as Larry the ’ole day long!
That’s w’en you know as you can’t go
wrong!
W r at chin’ the w’eat turn brown!
C. H. Souter, in “To Many Ladies (and
Others).”
0. H. Souter writes other than Cocky verses
in To Mann Ladies (and Others ) (Hassell,
Adelaide) ; he writes chanteys; he writes
about ’cellos and Chopin; he writes about
Billjim; bnt his most characteristic work is
done on the land. It is light stuff, done with
a delicate and fragile grace and with a
pretty wit. He is most at home in his
kisses, and his emotion seldom goes deeper
than a kiss. Many ladies (and others) will
be delighted with it.
Australian
The eighth volume of Fredk. Watson's
Historical Records of Australia, dealing
with the period July, 181d —Dec., 1815, in-
troduces us to Governor Lachlan Macquarie.
He was a military officer with an excellent
record, which included administrative work.
He was employed as a lieutenant in New
York and' Charleston on garrison duty until
the close of the war with the American
colonies. Placed upon the half-pay list, he
returned to Scotland, where later he was
offered the appointment of eldest lieutenant
in a regiment for service- in India. The
usual condition was attached to this prefer-
ment : he had to raise a quota of 15 recruits
for the regiment. Macquarie failed utterly
to get his recruits from the Highlands, and
had to travel to Glasgow and Edinburgh
before he got his 15 heroes. The servant
he enlisted for himself had all the necessary
military qualifications: he “dressed hair re-
markably well, waited table, and played very
well upon the fiddle.” Fredk. Watson
acknowledges that many of these curious
facts are derived from journals preserved in
the Mitchell Library. During his military
career Macquarie saw much service in India
and made himself a person of importance in
London Court circles. His appointment
as Governor of N.S.W. was due to the unex-
pected death of Brigadier-General Nightin-
gall, to whom Macquarie was second in com-
mand, as military officer in charge of the
73rd Regiment, sent out to relieve the notori-
ous N.S.W. Corps. Macquarie’s adminis-
tration was vigorous, but marred by an ill-
regulated judgment. “He created the first
Australian ‘boom’ by the lavish expenditure
of public money, which was followed by the
inevitable reaction after his departure.”
His energy showed itself in the many excel-
lent roads lie laid out, making the western
districts accessible by the famous x*oad over
the Mountains, though he withheld due
acknowledgment to Blaxland, Lawson and
Wentworth for their discoveries. He also
laid out towns on a lavish scale: he had an
itch for building and apparently rivalled
Kink O’Malley in his partiality for conspicu-
ously inscribing his name upon the works
constructed to his orders. “It is certain
that . Macquarie wasted a large amount of
convict labor, on these buildings, instead of
employing it in the formation of agricultural
settlements, which would have conferred a
permanent and lasting benefit.” Though
Macquarie, justly recognised the claims of
the emancipists and expirees to positions of
authority in the settlement, he acted so
arbitrarily that society refused to accept
some of his ex-convict proteges. He was an
honest, punctilious, intolerant, ostentatious,
pompous, intelligent sort of bloke.
The most important part of George A.
Taylor’s big-travel volume, There! A Pil-
grimage of Pleasure (Building, Ltd., Syd-
ney), is the title-page addendum, “with 306
pictures.” The letterpress is chockfull of
facts and platitudes; and all through one
feels that George A. Taylor is being
smart. But he is genuinely interested in
architecture and all civic ideals; and in the
United States he found much that interests
the average reader. And the photographs of
American buildings and the civic and archi-
tectural details that he gathered clearly
show how horribly backward Australia is in
the ferroconcrete of civilisation.
There has been some talk about what
Billzac reads. But Pastor Bampton, Stan-
ley, Tas., patriotically prints what Billzac
ought to read. Soldiers’ Battle Hymn and
Words of Comfort for the Amadous, Weak
and Dying (6d., or £5 per 1000) will cer-
tainly appeal to the emotions and the lan-
guage of every anxious, weak or dying Bill-
zac. There is an assortment of “helpful
texts and verses for the weak” —and the
Hun knows how weak Wm.-Jas. usually is.
There are also “helpful thoughts for the
anxious,” and “helpful verses for the dying.”
But these have not the personal element so
beautifully expressed in Bampton’s “Message
from the Soldiers in Camp on the Way to
those at the Front.” It begins thus and
goes on thus: —
Hold on, lads, for we are coming,
Coming, slow but sure,
Keep the good old banner waving,
We our help assure.
Hold the fort a little longer,
Till we all get there;
Then we’ll join you in the conflict,
And your burdens share.
Evidently Bampton is on the way. But,
no, there follows a “Letter of Appreciation
to the Brave Men of the Battlefield,” in
which Bampton says: “I can assure you
that your movements are watched with eager
expectation, and you are followed into
battle (in thought) by many an anxious
heart, with many a sigh and with many a
tearful prayer for your protection and safety.
Meanwhile we leave you in the Master’s
care, and exhort you to go forward with
full confidence in God.” Yea, Bampton is
still pastoring in Tassy; he has enlisted only
in thought; and he prays and exhorts Bill-
zac from some thousands of miles beyond
shell-fire. Upon the cover Bampton asks.
“Have you sent one to your soldier friend
jet?" Well, lmve you? And has your
soldier friend told you what he thinks of it?

At last Australia possesses a complete,
and in its broad respects, a iinal piscatorial
Who's Who. Fishes of Australia and Their
Technology, bj - T. C. Ilougliley, Economic
Zoologist for the N.S.W. Government, is not
only a notable contribution to our know-
ledge of the wealth of food in Australian
waters, but a valuable advertisement for the
N.SA\ . Government Printer. It will come
as a surprise to the casual Australian that so
manj r full page plates are necessary to illus-
trate the species of Australian edible fish.
These plates are admirably rendered in color,
and the volume has been produced with a taste
that this page was beginning to despair of in
N.S.W. technical publications. There was
the added difficulty in the work of the artist
that fishes so soon fade. Sometimes colored
sketches had to be made of the live fish imme-
diately after capture, while in other cases
the fish were frozen in ice in order to pre-
serve their complexion for the artist. The
lavish use of these color-plates gives the
book a practical value to amateur and pro-
fessional fishermen, while for the scientific
reader full technical details and much in-
formation about methods of capture, food-
value, supply and habits is available. The
author frankly states that the purpose of his
work is to aid in the development of the Aus-
tralian fish industry, particulars of which
form the subject of supplementary chapters.
He even depicts, in color illustrations, the
slight but artistic use that has hitherto been
made of the Australian fish in decorative
art.
B. C. Buley, whose Glorious Deeds of
Australasians was apt and admirable, now
furnishes A Child’s History of Ansae
(Hqdder and Stoughton), simply and
lucidly written. This work should find a
patriotic welcome as a supplementary read-
ing book for our schools.
Henry Fletcher suggests a scheme for the
Australian play: Australia having learned
to make its own bricks, boots 'and blacking,
is due to write, say, one-third of its plays
and have an oversea demand for the suc-
cesses. These appear the conditions of such
successes: Local color, dramatic construc-
tion, characterisation and a motive pleasing
to the average woman—for she is the chief
patron of the modern play, and decides its
fate. The opening is not for the physiolo-
gical, society or philosophic play; but for
the plain domestic drama. Required a group
of writers mutually assisting each other in
constructing such plays, aided by the criti-
cism of expert producers; and a volunteer
staff of actors and actresses to'be paid such
salaries as returns allow; to give single
matinge performances of these plays to the
public, managers, or those who might pro-
duce. This in a borrowed theatre with
loaned props. If we can get qualified winters,
critics and actors on the lines suggested,
there is hope for a series of plays, none very
bad and some a great success. But we must
first learn our business, as the essentially
Australian play has yet to be written. We
have to adapt the old mechanism and motives
to local, conditions. There must be a story
to tell in four acts, with as few scenes as
possible, and. covering, say, 120 typed letter
pages. —that is, a play to fill out the evening.
The ingredients of emotion to be measured
out as you make a Christmas pudding. Cur-
tains, situations and all the old bag of tricks,
and this, if possible, without staginess or
melodrama.
Mrs. G. It. Coxhead experiences “much
trepidation” iiv- placing her Lost, a Conti-
nent, Heroes of Anzac and O.V. (Modern
Printing Go., Elizabeth-street, Melb.),
before her reader. She admits that she has
not made any endeavor to soar to heights
above her powers; and she is entitled to that
somewhat rare merit in verse-writers. The
author writes a pleasant prose in metrical
form. And she has poems for every occa-
sion, shipwrecks preferred. She is more at
home, however, in her poems of royalty.
Here is “Victoria the Good”:—
The never-fading crown of life will be upon thy brow,
Before the King of Kings thou wilt in adoration bow;
The changing vanities of time are well exchanged
by .thee,
For union with the loved ones thou’st mourned so
faithfully.
There is, of course, “Royal Landing Day,”
with its loyal wish :—•
God bless our future King and Queen 1
On every lip we hear ;...,
And the inevitable In Memoriam on “our
King IferS many
appropriate “lines”: Lines on Hearing of
the Queen’s Illness, Lines on the American
Fleet,. Lines on the Coronation of King
George, Lines to an Aged Christian, Lines to
a Sick Friend, Lines Written in Answer to
an Eulogistic Poem by “Euroclydon”....
And there is even a poem “On the Opening
of the First Federal Parliament of Aus-
tralia,” in which the poet makes this hope-
less request to God
Oh, with true wisdom, now, our statesmen bless—
Such wisdom as canst Thou alone supply
To guide through sunshine or adversity.
Butters, beware!
TO OUR DEAD.
Duntroon, once bare of hero-deeds as dawn
Of stars, now sadly radiant, bows her
head,
Drooping a silent sword, with pride new-
born,
In homage to the dead.
Where dream mists glide on gaunt Galli-
poli,
And reverent memories their vigil keep
by shattered fanes of France, beyond the
sea,
They lie in splendid sleep.
Stalwart, with lunching eyes and parting
cheer,
Their hearts afire with martial pride, they
passed
Jo speed where Honor's call rang true and
clear
Its challenge to stand fast.
Bravely they fell—with just a rebel flush
Of tears for sea-barred home and love and
life.
A silent bracing to that solemn hush —
Serene repose from strife.
Loyal in death, they call us each by name
Vo do the simple thing that men should
do —
Shelter the home and shield the ting from
shame,
Or die for freedom, too.
One day a Napier shall, with pen aflame,
In eager, glowing phrase, not bare of pride,
Set down the glory of Australia’s fame
In her full warrior stride.
Radiant within that throbbing human chart,
Oar sons shall rc'ad the record of the price
We paid-—names Iona enrolled on every
h cart,
Dear stars of sacrifice.
Dear patriot names, imperishably twined
About sonic deed of daring gladly dared
lor home and homeland- -we had not divined
How much they loved and cared.
Duntroon, once bare of memories as dawn
Of stars, now richly dowered, hears the
tread
Of vanished footsteps, and with sword up-
borne,
Proudly salutes the dead.
—iu The Journal of the Royal
Military College of
AUSTRALIA.
Another Australian Novelist
A. lan Macleod must be congratulated
upon her ability to write the conventional
novel in a genuine Australian setting.
Hacks Brat (Hodder and Stoughton, per
Dymock’s Library), -apparently a first novel,
fey;• symptoms of crudity, though,
ttuTyusjy enough, it lacks a frequent char-
acteristic of a first effort—strength. Macleod
takes a conventional theme and develops it
with great naturalness. A mining assayer,
sent to a gold rush, becomes the unwilling
foster parent of a dirty little untamed bush
child, _ whose language is lurid and whose
behavior is rampageous. The Scotehaustra-
lian assayer is a reserved bloke; he tries
to tame his ward without attempting to
evoke her love. But he discovers that this
girl—-of course, she is a girl—-possesses the
makings of a Melba voice, and determines to
get her voice, and incidentally her, edu-
cated. A Sydney boarding-school mistress
manages where he had' failed. Naturally
he has no interest in the girl: all she is to
him is a Voice. So when she is ill all he
is concerned about is the possibility of her
losing her top-note. The hardened fiction
reader guesses that she will grow up into
a charming woman, and that, after the usual
false alarms, the man will discover that the
woman is more than the Voice. But the
situation between the two is ably worked
out; though the author suffers from the
commonest delusion in fiction, the blindness
that seems to strike a hero and a heroine
when they _ fall in love with each
other —and it is necessary to bump the
book out another five chapters. Bach
believes that the other does not reciprocate.
A. lan Macleod wastes many pages in her
attempt to keep up this impossible situation;
though when the man makes his disclosure
the girl instantly becomes natural. “Did
you say—oh, will you say—some of that
again?” she whispers unsteadily, and then,
after she has bui'rowed her face into his
shoulder, “John, aren’t you ever going to
ask me to —to marry you?”
The life in tire mining camp is admirably
drawn ; the pub-keeper’s daughter has more
red blood in her than is usually permitted
in fiction; she is more real in her love-
affairs than the heroine, whose transforma-
tion from the Brat to the Lady is not suffi-
ciently explained. The author has also
unnecessarily thought it necessary to ex-
plain the mystery of the Brat’s parentage,
and has complicated a simple story by much
extraneous plot. But her reserved hero and
the mining men with whom he has so much
to do are genuine individuals; arid the novel
has a wholesome Australian flavor. A. lan
Macleod ought to keep on.
“Dominie Sampson” belatedly suggests a
neat recasting of one of our toasts: “Aus-
tralia ! May her midwives never be idle!”
He proffers, “Australia! May she be kept
off the rock of disaster by the rock of the
cradle t 5 * *

The BENNETT is a practical Standard Keyboard Typewriter, offered at a price within the reach
of all. It is Compact, Portable and Strong’, built for hard usage, to last a lifetime.
The BENNETT does nearly everything most expensive machines do, and does it easily and
neatly. Kase of operation is one of its greatest recommendations.
The BENNETT Typewriter will do beautiful, clean-looking work, which cannot be distinguished
from that of the large machines. It will write plainly and legibly the work of authors, the
sermons of preachers, the briefs of lawyers, and the regular correspondence of a business man.
The BENNETT Tv pewriter may be operated by all the fingers of both hands, and takes all si/.es
of paper up to 84 inches wide.
No Course of Instruction, no long hours of practice are necessary to master the simple features
of the BENNETT. The novice quickly learns the universal keyboard.'
The BENNETT Typew liter, packed in a neat Leatherette Case, is 10 inches long, 5 inches wide,
and 2 inches high, and weighs 5 pounds.
Lverv part of the BENNETT Typewriter is made of the best hardened steel, and the most skilled
labor furnishes the workmanship. The Type face is identical with the larger machines. The
adjustment is accurate, and the type alignment is safeguarded by a special device.
A Manifesto to Our Friends.
-♦
TOP QUALITY AT BOTTOM PRICE is our constant aim and
endeavor, hut under no circumstances do we sacrifice the former
for the latter, as we guarantee to supply the best that money can
buy. Our usual Catalogue is imprinted “Subject to market fluctua-
tions',” but this does not apply to the Sale List, for Ave undertake that
the goods are available at the prices quoted subject to their being in
stock at the time of the receipt of orders, and notwithstanding any
increase in the Federal Tariff during the currency of our Sale.
Our financial facilities enable us to adopt a system of paying Spot
Cash for everything we buy, and the advantage of the loAver rates that
result therefrom are passed on to all who honor us Avith their custom.
Still, it is undeniable that all manufactured goods must be bought on a
rising market, and Ave have every reason to believe the prices quoted to-
day will be materially advanced in a year’s time. By the last mail Ave
received the following advice: —
“ Everything has to be placed on one side but War
work; most of the manufacturers will have to
cease export trade entirely, as all men not doing
munition work are called up for service. ”
Therefore, Ave advise our customers Avithout hesitation to buy while
the Sale lasts, as, although it is contrary to our permanent principle of
trading to raise prices until we are compelled, it is only a matter of time
until the increased cost of freight, Avar risk insurance and dearer Home
markets, must, to a much greater extent, make themselves felt in Aus-
tralia.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free, 1/3. Free sample posted.

A Natural Remedy
Time was when disease was thought to be due to the
direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.
Be prepared for emergencies by always keeping a
bottle in the house.

A Scientific Cure.
There is only one real scientific method of
curing kidney disease, and that is to wash
away the uric acid (the poison) right out of
the system.
The proper way for this to be done is to
drink from three to four pints of pure water
per day, and with each glass of water should
be taken some mild yet penetrating antisep-
tic. The proper one to take is contained
in De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills.
It has the wonderful property of penetra-
ting every crevice of the kidneys and blad-
der, and wherever it touches it washes away
all sediment and bad secretions, and makes
the kidneys and bladder perfectly healthy.

self by getting a box of De Witt’s Kidney
and Bladder Pills at his chemist’s —a4l chem-
ists sell them —and you will, within a very

The quick relief which these wonderful
little pills bring is, so our readers say, noth-

to rheumatism, gout, gravel, backache, tired
feeling, dropsy, Bright’s disease, constipa-
tion, cloudy urine, inflammation of the blad-
der, or other symptoms of urinary trouble.
To cure rheumatism or any other form of
kidney trouble you must positively get rid of
the cause—the poisonous uric acid. Re-

through the kidneys and the bladder, and not
through the bowels, as most kidney pills do.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Scrollers.
I strayed into a place of trees
Where birds made morning melodies,
And youths and maidens in a ring
Linked arms and played at wmodland games,
While round about grey sires and dames
Clapped hands to speed their revelling.
But there was one within whose heart
There stirred no sylvan gaiety;
With thinking brows he stood apart,
And carved his name upon a tree.
“O Fool,” I said, “perchance to-night
That tree shall fall whereon you write,
And all the labors of your day
Pass into dust upon the winds!
Laugh, then, with yonder merry hinds,
And fling the fiction Fame away !”
Another time upon a beach,
Made white by salty seas that bleach,
And smooth by breezes soft nnd fair,
I saw folk splashing to and fro,
Their eyes alight, their cheeks aglow,
And joy resplendent everywhere.
But one with unresponsive heart
Stood heedless to the hour’s demands;
With thinking brows he moved apart,
And wrote his name upon the sands.
“O Fool,” I said, “within an hour
The waves above your name shall tower
And break, like sportive days at play!
Go, join the golden-hearted throng—
Nor love, nor life, last overlong—
And fling the fiction Fame away!”
Anon 1 walked a stately place
Where Beauty, arm in arm with Grace,
Passed by in love and amity;
And there I saw fair damsels trip,
And there I saw good fellowship,
And life contented just to be.
But theje was one within whose heart
There burnt no kindly human flame;
With thinking brows he stood apart
And on cold granite carved his name.
“O Fool,” 1 said, “dire earthquake shocks
That break the strength of fire-born rocks
In blown grey dust your work shall lay !
Scroll, stone and all alike must go.
Conte, then, and join the passing show,
And throw the fiction Fame away
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Little Girls’ Hands.
When little girls hold out their flower-like
hands
I gather them and press them, innocent
Of all unmanly thought, knowing them sent
As sruides to lead myself to lofty lands.
Then do I feel an aching sadness, blent
With sturdy joy; and hug such brave intent
As he who is mere dreamer countermands,
And quakes when his Ideal mocking stands!
For these white, fragile hands are more
than dreams,
Which hem the magic realm of Poesy,
Dim ’mid its purple woods and buddin
themes.
And so I count these hands a queenly loan
To hold until rich Love will bring to me
Hands I can kiss and call my very own !
MAOISILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
Apollo in Sydney Gardens.
Sweet god of oracles ! Spring-hour of life!
Above the shriek of our impassioned strife
Still rings your voice, like running rills of
wine,
And poets meet and mingle at your shrine,
As when the pious priestess from your
breath
Epon the waiting throngs spoke peace or
death
From that deep-fissured rock whose vapors
flung
The wisdom of your being to her tongue.
All thoughts divine are yours. 0 rhythmic
host!
Lord of the flaming noon, whose features
boast
The princely symmetry of untold grace;
Leto in silence marvelled at your face
As drave by torments out of Arcady
Zeus chained the floating Delos to the sea.
And 'neath the shade of laurel leaf and palm
And moon and sun slept in her circling arm.
The serpent of the dark faints in your sight.
Stung by the arrows of immortal light.
Mind ne’er conceived a thing more fair to
see;
Your very faults become a victory ;
For in the morn of your tremendous love,
W hen Daphne fled, its falcon like a dove,
Her shroud of laurel tripped its flight of
fire
To crown some victor, in his keen desire.
O -radiant gift of gods to mortal man!
While yet the Spring rides in her airy van,
Decking the dawn with many a golden rose
From your pure font the fount of beauty
flows.
Poems still patter through the pleasant rain,
Music is with the lark in every lane.
And though men offer you their battle pall
Triumphant rings your pman over all.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
Between Ourselves.
Let us be joyous for a little space;
Emptied the cup of earth of its last tear:
While Summer clasps a scarf of laughter-
lace
With a red rose beneath a sun-kissed ear.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.

Your car pulls better
at night time. Why?
Before petrol vapor can ignite, it must be
mixed with oxygen, of which ordinary air
contains a large proportion. The function
of the carburetor is to mix the petrol vapor
with air in the proportions which will give
the best result. The carburetor can take
in only a certain volume of air, and as the
night air contains more oxygen than air in
the daytime—a more powerful mixture is
always made at night, and your car pulls
better in consequence.

will be regarded as strictly confidential.

without stamps for return it will be understood that

sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not

THURSDAY, JANUARY 25, 1917.
AUSTRALIA AND THE LONDON
CONFERENCE.
To be told that Britain is anxious to have
the Prime Ministers in London for the pur-
pose of securing their personal assistance in
winning the Avar and deciding upon the terms
of peace merely provokes suspicion. Who
believes it? Winning the war is the job of the
Allied armies and navies, and the terms of
peace will be decided by the course of mili-
tary and naval events. So far as the imme-
diate interests of the oversea British com-
munities are concerned, their anxieties are
os well knoAvn in London as they are in Mel-
bourne and Sydney. Representatives of
these communities have been in England
since the war began. Most of them have
talked about war and peace at the top of
their voices. For none of them is there
anything left to say on either subject. If
there was, the mail and cable services would
be available. The men asked to attend the
Conference have definite work to do in their
own countries. While they are in London
this work Avill be left in the ice-chest. But
out of the confabulations there Avill emerge
something that is likely to make a lot of the
postponed work seem very small —something
that will raise issues going to the very root
of our national being. Imperial Federa-
tion looked like an idle dream in the long
years of peace and slouch. Many Austra-
lians indeed imagined that the ideal of the
Avhole-hoggers had been abandoned except
for purposes of academic discussion. They
never made a greater mistake. The force
behind this movement is greater than ever.
The Avar has stimulated it and incidentally
changed many of the problems surrounding
the relationships of British countries.
* * * *
It is easy to see what the sequence of
events beginning with the London Confer-
ence Avill be. There is the invitation to
join in “deliberations of an Imperial charac-
ter.” There are the assertions that the
dignity and responsibilities of the “Do-
minions” are at length being recognised and
they are “taking their rightful place at last
beside the Mother Country as arbiters of
their destiny and hers.” There are the
speeches made in England by representative
men from beyond the sea —-by men like
Fisher and Hughes and Massey and the
lately-dead Bernhard Wise and dozens of
others; there are appeals from India ; there
are even the recorded utterances of the
French-Canadian Nationalist llenhi Bouß-
assa—all insisting that the British conn-
tries should have a voice in the foreign
policy of Britain, that they must be ad-
mitted to a partnership, that it is a mockery
of their local sovereignty if war can be made
upon them without responsibility or choice
on their part. This Conference is the response
to all that: it is the first step towards pre-
paring for permanent political representa-
tion at Westminster—for reconstruction of a
water-logged and overweighted British Par-
liament on the basis of devolution in Britain
and Ireland and the creation of an assem-
blage charged with the complex job of hand-
ling the problems common to all. That
there are such problems it is no use denying.
The question whether they can be met in the
way suggested is another matter. But the
point just now is not whether some scheme
that is merely floating in the air is practical.
It is that this Conference is to be the body
• from which is expected to come something
that will serve as the foundation of an
organic Imperial system.
* * * *
All the indications, at any rate, point in
that direction, and are a warning to Aus-
tralia that whoever the nation’s representa-
tive at this Conference may be, he must be a
serious, responsible incarnation of Aus-
tralian ideals. The consequences of the
Commonwealth being spoken for by a man
of reckless tongue would be deplorable.
Hitherto Imperial Federation has attracted
few Australians, and the objections they
have had in the past to government by ex-
ternal authority—to being dragged into
obligations and enterprises with which they
have no concern—have not been modified by
recent events. The doctrine that their job is
here in Australia, and that it is nobody
else’s job, is firmly held; and this paper
knows of no reason, contemporary or his-
torical, to make it believe that future gener-
ations of Australian-born will modify that
view. The people of to-day have grown up
in an atmosphere of Nationalism. Though
there have been illusions about their inde-
pendence, it has been their governing senti-
ment ever since autonomous government
became a fact. Some of these illusions have
been violently banished; but we are not
yet ready to remedy the proved defects by
abandoning our visions and ambitions and
rushing into foreign entanglements. This,
however, means tremendously serious respon-
sibilities. The Bulletin is under no delu-
sions about everything being the same after
the war as it was before, or that the same
old rut Ayill be at the ancient address. The
Avorld is in flames. When these are stamped
out there will be a lot more to do for the
national future than to chatter about it.
Nationalist ambitions will not be realised
by talk or by bowing down to a joss, no
matter how good a joss it may be. Neither
economically nor territorially can Australia
be safe unless the people show the courage
and the enterprise such a contract as they
have on hand demands. One of our greatest
perils is that the habit of shirking Avith which
the Australian Parliaments have been
afflicted during the last feAV years may be a
sinister indication of the condition of mind
of the people. If this is so, the Common-
wealth may soon be directed on to entirely
new and entirely wrong lines. The pressure
to send it there will without a doubt be
considerable. For Britain is being put to" a
strain that is shakintr her to the boots; and
to strengthen herself she will surely turn to
those countries abroad inhabited by British
people who Avithin a generation or two will
have a nopulation exceeding her own. She
will be moved by no spirit of mild inquiry,
but by the vital considerations of self-
defence. Australia must, therefore, look
soon for questions that Avill be a searching
test of her strength and vision.
TRAINING IN THE DESERT.
Moses, the dim mixture of prophet and
statesman who stands at the back of Israel-
itish tradition, led a million people out into
the desert where the Australian Light Horse
are now adding something to history. For 40
years he kept his crowd marching to and fro
in the comparatively narrow area of hot
rock and sand which lies between Suez and
the Dead Sea. It was severe training, but
it seems to have been effective; for the slave
nation, which left Egypt with a feeble han-
kering after fleshpots it had not the pluck
to plunder, broke into Palestine as a fierce,
fighting army which could bear down the
weightiest opposition. The desert did it.
Hunger, thirst, march and counter-march
combined with heat and cold to harden up
the children of slaves, and turn them into
fighting men. The Jew has passed through
a lot of trials and tribulations since; but
the 40 years in the wilderness stands to him
still. At the outset of the present war, the
British Imperial authorities took a leaf
from the book of Moses. Whether the long
training of Australian troops in the ancient
deserts around the* Pyramids and ruins of
forgotten cities was necessary or merely
ornamental it is impossible to say. Certainly
Gaba Tepe and Lonesome Pine did not hint
that the sand had harmed the soldiers of
Australia. That they might have done equally
well without it is possible; that they could
have been trained more comfortably and
quite as efficiently nearer home is certain.
Had Australia possessed a better military
organisation, our soldiers might have gone
directly from Liverpool and Broadmeadows to
take the Turk by the beard and argue with
him about the road- to Constantinople. But
the training in the desert did not entirely
miscarry; and it may even have been the
best thing under the mixed and incoherent
circumstances.
Wanderings in the wilderness and train-
ing in the desert have gone to the making
.of some of the world’s biggest things.
Through deserts of persecution and ages of
proscription the early Christians won their
wav to the spiritual conquest of Europe.
Islam had a- somewhat similar experience of
briefer duration in the deserts of Asia. Out
of the wastes of serfdom, injustice and
bitter contempt the French people rose to
the height of democracy, In fact, almost
everything in the past which looks wortn
while came out of a wilderness of some
sort • and not one movement of permanent
worth had its birth in a nightingale-haunted
rose-garden or a palace of age-long delights.
From the rugged, strenuous, hungry time
came the force that made for prosperity,
kindness and real human progress. Whether
this is a law of life or a mere accident
remains to -be proved. In the ordinary evolu-
tion of life there should come a time when
the bitter of history fails to repeat itself;
and such a period seemed to have been
reached when Australia combined to take
on the responsibilities of nationhood. True,
Australian pioneers had been to some extent
trained in the desert. They accepted hard-
ships and faced the difficulties of loneliness
and incessant toil in a new- land. But it
was a good new land, and the pastures of
promise were never far distant. In the
great peace which succeeded the fall of
Napoleon, Australian settlement shaped
the future States and laid the foundations
of the Commonwealth Of the future. Except
for the few' Who came aS convicts to the
Nark State and Tasmania, the men and
Women who peopled Australia went, not
only voluntarily, but even gladly into the
new Land of Promise. They were not forced
out from older lands by persecution or
hatred; and their choice of a new home
was a deliberate one. Bloodless, free and
ever moving towards still greater freedom,
Australia saw the placid years of her in-
fancy glide by. They were years of plenty,
and seemed to be the prelude to a future of
even greater bounty. There was no desert
training in the background. Of all nations,
earth’s last and newest alone seemed to be
born pangless.
* * * *
But, looking back over the years during
w-hich Australia has regarded herself as a
nation, a cloud of doubts arises to set us
wondering and guessing. The promise of
nationhood has not been fulfilled; the
earnest purpose which was supposed to give
life and reality to Australian unity has not
proved itself. ' Is it possible that we, too,
must yet take our turn in the wilderness?
The one thing quite clear is that we are
heading that way. Already the confusion of
our politics, the inability of our leaders to
understand grave issues and the unwilling-
ness of our democracy to carry its own
burdens have brought us to the sun-scorched
verge. The plenty which Australia has
hither.to enjoyed has been easily won, or
more easily borrowed, and has led to an
extraordinary belief that our wealth is in-
exhaustible. On the one hand, the poli-
tician, taxing and borrowing and squander-
ing, professes to believe that debts ahd re-
sponsibilities of any magnitude can be met
by the people whenever they fall due. On
the other, the majority of the,, people, as
represented by the trade Unions, regard the
capital of the country as so much cash
locked in the banks from which higher and
higher wages may be drawn if only the work
of the nation is stopped often enough by
strikes or thrown into confusion by threats
of worse things to follow. As a plain fact,
Australia’s wealth is not inexhaustible. Our
national capital represents no more than
five years of national labor and production;
and. between the claims for interest on loan
moneys ill expended and the .demand for
wages unearned, we are being forced out
into a desert as bitter and bleak and hungry
as any of those in which other nations
perished or had their training. It may be
necessary for us. It may give us strength
and sincerity and the honest outlook upon
the future which we appear to lack. Yet,
on the face of it, it is poor business to throw
away the starting advantage which we
undoubtedly possessed. Moses led Israel
into ‘ the w-ilderness because there was no
other place to lead them; but it would
be a nation of deep philosophers and
ardent zealots that deliberately left the
fleshpots and chose hunger and suffer-
ing. Australia does not look like a
nation of philosophers and zealots.. It seems
more like a ship, carrying six free fights
and seven drunken arguments by way of
cargo, which is rapidly drifting to a broad
sandbank marking the outer limit of the
great desert of despair. The hope of the
future may lie in that desert. But, again, it
may not.
YELLOW JOURNALISM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was trying to decipher the tablets in a
fine old deserted Confucian temple in the
native city when he materialised alongside
of me. He seemed to have plenty of leisure
time. His queue had gone in the popular
revolt against Manchu rule, and his hair
had not yet decided on its final shape. He
had wild eyes but an engaging manner, and
was dressed in native costume. He told me
he was an editor.
An editor! This was a discovery. I fol-
lowed him along a maze of narrow lanes to
his house, where he. gave me tea, introduced
me to his pretty wife, whose feet were not
bound, and oromised to show me over the
offices of his paper. The offices of the Shun
Pao were not in the native city. The atmo-
sphere of Chinese territory is not good
breathing for critics of the Government.
Stringent press laws and official squeeze can
be avoided by registering newspapers in the
foreign settlements; and that is where most
of the new journalism finds a haven.
The office of tlm Shun Pao, like this
editor, was of semi-Chinese architecture. It
had been a pioneer in* Chinese journalism,
and there was the glad smell of papt" and
printer’s ink natural to all newsr aper
offices —the same uproar of machinery, the
same aspect of untidiness as if the flood of
yesterday’s events had left its rubbish on
shelves and tables. The first pages of the
morrow morning’s issue, to go out to its
24,000 subscribers at three cents a head,
were already in the press. Three parts of
it consisted of advertisements at 25 cents
(Gd.) per inch of column. It used six sorts
of type, and read from right to left and
frotn the back page to the front. A young-
ster was cutting wood engravings' with
native skill. The staff numbered over a
hundred and were all editors; six of them,
at any rate, introduced themselves as such'
The editors lived on the premises and
messed together. Reuter’s, for $2OO per
month, supplied them with an excellent ser-
vice of news from the provinces, and their
foreign news they translated from the Eng-
lish newspapers, and thus published them a
day late. The editorial, managerial and
sub-editorial offices looked business-like
and were replete with typewriters and
other labor-saving devices. The type-
writers were all English ; a Chinese student
has patented a Chinese typewriter with a
capacity of 5000 characters, but it is still in
the experimental stage.
Nor were there any linotypes in the type-
setting rooms. The nearest approach to an
alphabet that the Chinese language has is
the 214 radicals. There are over 40,000
characters in the standard Chinese diction-
ary, and at least 10,000 are essential to the
modern journalistic style. There are no lino-
types, but the Chinese compositor faces the
appalling complexitv of his language with
naked fingers undismayed, moving about
amongst the type-boxes with uncanny ease
and quickness. Occasionally he takes a long
trip down the room in quest of a remote
character.
The Chinese equivalent of a printer’s
devil came in now and then with fresh
copy, or drew proofs and carried them off.
One room was devoted to the making of dies
for new- characters', which the rapid infusion
of foreign ideas into Chinese thought makes
a constant necessity.
From another press a now illustrated
monthly, with a circulation of 10,000 was
being issued. From the windows of the
Shun Pao I could see the signs of half a
dozen other newspapers. Along the s: -ot
were the fine premises of the Comm ■ ini
Press, turning out millions of copies j uly
of the best ancient and modern thought of
Europe— Siiakspeare, Darwin, Huxley,
Mill, Montesquieu, Rousseau, and Kidd
—and employing over 1000 native workers,
who share in the profits, are provided with
schools for their children, and sanitary sur-
roundings on the best Western models. This
is the Fleet-street of Asia, and down the
middle of it flowed the never-ceasing stream
of Chinese coolie traffic, staggering under its
enormous burdens and singing, instead of
the Marseillaise, its endless chant of labor.
The oldest newspaper in the world is the
Peking Gazette. Until 40 years ago it had
no rivals. Even 20 years ago the number of
newspapers i» China could be counted on
the fingers. But the war with Japan, with
its revelation of China’s weakness, gave a
tremendous impetus to progressive ideas.
Newspapers sprang up in every city. The
Government was at first delighted at the
frank criticisms of distant officials which it
Was able to read in the provincial press.
But when this new weapon was turned
against itself it awoke to the danger, and
the futile game, as old as monarchies and as
new as government by caucus, was began
of trying to stifle public opinion by sitting
on it. The Radical papers were driven over
the border where, -under the protection of
the British flag, they grew in power and
vituperative capacity. The first scalps that
the new journalism garnered in were those
of the Manchus. Yuan Sjiih-Iv’ai’s fol-
lowed, so soon as his ambitious hand aspired
to put the crown on it. But the Chinese
press is not entirely destructive. The
scholarly essays of Liang Ciii-ciiiao, a
far-seeing patriot, are 'unsurpassed in any
language. The best papers are trying to
leaven the inert mass of the people with the
elements of science, medicine and hygiene,
to combat the crude theology of Western
missionaries and to obtain the reform of the
barbarous penal code, the mandarinate and
national finance. There is a strong section
which advocates compulsory military ser-
vice, a big navy and efficient defence.
The Chinese Nortiicliffe, when he
aj'rives, w T ill command forces for good or
evil that would overwhelm his English pro-
totype by their terrific potentialities. In
China 400,000.000 await his coming. Add
to these the lesser millions of Cochin China
and Japan who use the Chinese character,
the growing colonies of Chinese which fringe
the whole Pacific shore from Tierra del
Fuego to the Gulf of Carpentaria, and you
have a public to which the English-reading
public is a handful. And from the dawn
of history these millions have revered the
printed word above their gods. In the next
world a sea of knives awaits the thoughtless
butcher who wraps up the morning sausage
in the morning newspaper. The whole
democratic system of Chinese government
depends on a competitive literary examina-
tion in which the lowest coolie can take part.
The highest honors in the Empire are handed
to the successful turner of delicate phrases.
Great masters of the Chinese pen (or brush)
have been deified, and the characters they
drew with such artistic consciousness have
stepped from the page and lived. Here then
is a field for the journalistic sower, rich and
ready for the sowing.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Democracy and its Leader!
“I ASK all Australians to join me in help-
ing to win the war—to do their utmost to
defeat the common enemy.” Thus Hughes.
And it sounds very well. But it is a fair
thing to ask what 'Hughes is doing to win
the war —not the war between himself and
Frank Anstey, but the nation’s war. The
latter ; s not going to be won by anything but
bayonets; and if Hughes is taking any
serious part in securing reinforcements for
the Australian regiments, no one except
himself knows anything about it. All that
the public hears about Hughes from day to
day is that he “w r ent to the seaside,” or "re-
turned from the country,” or “saw Hr.
Cook.” or something like that. It is the
picture of a furtive figure flitting surrep-
titiously to and fro in an atmosphere ot
mystery, hopping from wire to wire, spring-
ing from bough to bough, bamboozling him-
self and trying to humbu., everybody else.


There may be differences of opinion about
the proprietv of this. To some people it
m ay seem even decent. To The Bulletin
this spectacle of the Prime Minister of the
Australian Commonwealth occasionally
poking his head out of a hole to make a few
remarks and promptly vanishing into
obscurity is almost ghastly. In normal times
it might have a humorous aspect. There is
no humor about it to-day. No other country
engaged in the war is burdened with a load
like this. In every one of them the Prime
Minister at least behaves as if he had
reasonable claims to be regarded as a
national leader. In none of them is the
political machine so clogged with the rust
of ages that it cannot respond to the needs
of the hour —so open to abuse that it can be
captured for the purposes of party vaude-
ville.
The Hypocrisy of the Defence Depart-
ment,
There is alleged to be wasteful administra-
tion at Australian military headquarters in
London; and, though an inquiry is pro-
mised, Defence-Minister Pearce more or
less prejudges the matter, declaring that lie
doesn’t think there can be waste because the
papers have spoken well of the administra-
tion. Pearce ought to know what would
happen if the papers said that the ad-
ministration was wasteful and proceeded to
prove it. If he doesn’t, The Bulletin will
tell him. A month ago, tired of making
general charges concerning the inefficiency
under his own nose in Melbourne, The
Bulletin cited one specific case. Dealing
with the matter circumstantially, so that
there should be no doubt about it, The
Bulletin showed that for the better part
of a year a comic-opera correspondence had
been occupying the attention of officials,
from generals downwards, as to whether a
junior clerk should be given a rise of a
few shillings a week. The State Command-
ant had recommended it on the grounds (1)
that the clerk was doing work entitling him
to the rise, and (2) that he had qualified for
a higher position. The correspondence,
which began with the State Command-
ant, wandered on interminably, and after a
vast expenditure of red-tape ended nowhere.
And how did the Department meet this
proof that time and money were being
wasted? By raiding The Bulletin office
with a formal demand under the War Pre-
cautions Act for all documents and informa-
tion that would help it to trace the person
who was responsible for the exposure of
this scandal! For a newspaper to expose
the incompetence of the Department’s
methods in this way was alleged to be aid-
ing and abetting in a crime, and the crime
of the aider and abettor is punishable with
six months’ gaol and any number of fines of
£lOO each. Now the Minister whose De-
partment takes such measures to prevent the
only sort of criticism that really counts—
the criticism that can go into details—
tells the public that he doesn’t think there
can be anything wrong because the news-
papers haven’t said anything about it! .The
hypocrisy of it!

The Profits of the Strike.
Wiiat astonishingly profitable business these
strikes are for the rugged, stubborn toilers
who in defence of high principle—which does
not always include the keeping of honorable
treaties —hold up the business of the coun-
try ! The moulders, who dropped their tools
last September and left them lying where
they fell for 15 weeks, were fighting against
a decision of the Wages Board which ■ re-
duced their earnings from Is. 9d. to Is. 6d. per
hour. They went back to work —if there was '
any work for them—at Is. 74d., which
amounted to an increase of six shillings per
week on the award they repudiated. In the
meantime each striker had lost about £54 in
wages—a sum which he may make good by
sticking steadily at toil for 34 years with-
out strike or other disturbance. But what
about the other losses? Contingent trades
and workers whose daily toil linked into that
of tbe moulders lost cash also. In 50 direc-
tions the work of the country was delayed
and time was wasted which in a real sense
can never be made good. If the whole bill
against each individual striker could be pre-
sented, it w 7 ould amount to a sum that
might stagger some of the believers in crude
and antiquated methods of labor warfare.
And not only does the loss of wages in the
strike itself destroy the benefit of any in-
creases obtained, but by continual strikes
the national capital, which is the real basis
of every pay-sheet, is frittered away. A
nation at work can afford to pay a lot to its
workers; a nation that is half the time out
of a job through the workers’ own act can’t.
The moral is: Keep at work and fight with
saner and subtler weapons—a lesson the
whole country was supposed to have learned
pretty thoroughly a generation ago.

The Leak in the Boom Bladder.
Australia’s friend Bloat is _ beginning
once more to explain what a disreputable
old fraud he is. Deputations pursuing
N. S. Wales Ministers for the expenditure
of more money are told that the cashbox is
mostly filled with emptiness, and there is
great tribulation about men being thrown
out of employment. Then follow wild lan-
guage and denunciation of the Ministry for
not continuing the borrowing policy on
which the State has climbed to a great
“prosperity”; and threats are made that
unless Ministers make a deal with Cohen
and restore confidence an army of unem-
ployed will be marshalled in Sydney to insist
upon another loan, because if there is no
loan there can be no wages. The fact, of
course, is that the Government has been
spending millions as they were never spent
before, and this outlay, so far from doing
the people directly employed any good, has
simply manufactured disaster for them. Bloat
leads directly to unemployment and nowhere
else. Borrowing has always been the lazy
politician’s stall. It has saved him the
trouble of thought and action. And the
great tragedy to Australia has been that the
men who have spent most time mouthing
about their anxiety to protect the workers
and build un the country’s industries are the
men who have been the most profligate in
'their borrowings—who have done least to
stabilise employment. Though millions are
being spent by the Federal Government,
though thousands of men are out of the
country, the biggest harvest the State has
known with one exception being bagged, and
a cataract of imported goods pouring into
Australian ports, the Holman Government
squandered £6,000,000 of loan money during
the last six months—and thousands of men
are out of work! Australia is probably the
only civilised country in the world to-day
in which there is unemployment, and it is
so mainly because it has not mastered the
problem of how to earn a living. Meanwhile
note, as the probable explanation of the
whole matter, that Australia is still import-
ing most things, from clothes-pegs to pianos,
and the year ended with a‘ record of 764
strikes.
Sympathy as a Staple Food.
About a year ago a deputation of Austra-
lian poultry-breeders made certain remarks
to then Customs Minister Tudor and invited
him to take action. He listened courteously
and offered his sympathy, and that was the
end of it. Last week another deputation,
from the National Poultry Breeders, col-
logued with Minister Archibald and asked
him to do something. They got his sym-
pathy also; whether they will get anything
else remains to be seen. Yet the poultry-
breeders’ troubles are obvious enough; the
disaster that threatens them is equally
plain, and the remedy can be noticed with
the naked eye. The few relevant facts are
(1) that in 1913 China dumped 200.000,000
dozen eggs on the ’EMscd market; (2) that
the U.S.A. promptly put a prohibitive duty
on eggs; (3) that China then turned her
attention to Australia, among other coun-
tries, and last year unloaded £26,700 worth
of hen-fruit on the Commonwealth, as against
£1176 worth the year before; and (4) that
the existing revenue duty of 6d. per dozen
is absolutely no protection against China.
In his reply Archibald vamped up the quite
irrelevant story that the U.S.A.’s Protective
tariff is successful because she has a home
market of 100,000,000 people, quite forget-
ting that Victoria’s Protective tariff first
applied to about 500,000. Still, he gave the
breeders his sympathy. They will have that
consolation when they have been driven out
of the business; also they will have leisure
to reflect on the wisdom of sending Free-
traders and fiscal sinkers to Parliament.
Likewise, when a mere handful of people
are struggling with the vast debt which this
war is going to leave us, other people besides
ex-poultry-raisers, may do some cursing. For
Ah Sin, who grows the eggs that we ought
to be growing ourselves, and Gin Fat, his
brother who supplies him with the hen-
fodder that a good Australian ought to be
growing, will certainly not carry any of our
huge interest load.

Repatriation by Jaw.
Another agreement has been arrived at
between the Federal and State Governments
in reference to repatriation. It is described
as being “a broad general agreement.” The,
details are not yet open for inspection. It
is about the 167th agreement and in all proba-
bility about 46 more will yet be unanimously
arrived at. Meanwhile the Federal Min-
istry is talking over the appointment of a
Director of Repatriation. As it is just
about time that this business was redeemed
from a chaos that is at once tragic and
ludicrous, such an appointment looks desir-
able —if the right man gets it. But who-
ever the Director of Repatriation may be. a
great responsibility will still rest upon the
politicians. The policy or scheme to be fol-
lowed must be the work of Governments, and
it is this that Australia wants to hear about.
Up to the present there is nothing but
vague talk. The latest contribution -comes
from the N. S. Wales Minister for
Lands, who has been explaining that
when the repatriation scheme gets qn
its legs men will be able to acquire
farms and advances, and after going into
residence will be paid regular wages for two
years. This sounds better than ever; but
The Bulletin wearily asks for someone to
explain where the “farms” are and how long
is it going to be before the returned soldier
can find their address, a supply of cash for
capital expenditure and a two years’ job.
Until something definite is available this
continual flow of words merely suggests that
for a start the Director of Repatriation
would be best employed in applying a few
gags.
Planting Venereal Disease in the Park.
Meagher, Gaud Mayor of Sydney, has been
talking the usual platitudes in defence of
the attempt to grab a further portion of the
Domain for hospital purposes. His Emi-
nence’s first flabby remark was that the
claims of the sick were above those of public
recreation. This may be so, but it has no
sort of bearing on the case. There is the
whole breadth of the State in which to find
housing for the sick, while the breathing
space of Sydney is limited and, once lost,
wall never be restored. Meagher’s argu-
ment might be made to cover a lot of ground.
The claims of the sick are more important
than those of the travelling public. There-
fore the Central Railway Station should be
turned into a hospital. The claims of the
sick are more important than those of a
Gaud Mayor. Therefore the Gaud Mayor’s
screw should be handed over to the sick.
But the whole shallow premise is wrong.
The claims of disease are not greater than
those of recreation. To heal the sick is an
important business, but it is not nearly so
important as to keep the healthy healthy;
and the breathing spaces of the city are a
means towards the preserving of public
health. “Recreation,” says Meagher, “is
of little use to a sick man, unless he is first
nursed back to health.” But does this futile
reasoner, who would cut down shrubs and
trees to make room for venereal disease,
actually imagine that the whole population
is sick and that there are none needing
recreation until they are first cured of
syphilis? Prevention is still many degrees
better than cure; and the horse-sense of the
public in demanding the integrity of its parks
is still some distance ahead of the fatuous
reasoning of the chief despoiler.

Cocky and His Wheat.
Cocky has made a demand on Australia that
he should be guaranteed 4s. a bushel for his
wheat during the next couple of years. Pro-
tected on the local market he wants protec-
tion on the foreign market, too; in this in-
stil nee the request is for a shield against
(1) the submarine, (2) the probability of the
Canadian harvest this year being a normal
one, (3) the likelihood of John Bull grow-
ing more wheat on his own account, as he
certainly can and should, and (4) anything
happening to liberate the vast accumulations
of grain in Russia. It is a good, able-
bodied demand, and if it . were granted and
Cocky could induce the churches to co-oper-
ate and arrange for a rainfall more copious
than it was in ’l5, and less emphatic than
in T 6, he would be in for a fairly good time.
But the demand is made in the wrong place.
It is really one for the British Government,
not the Federal Government. Britain is
the buyer. If Britain is prepared to say
that she will give Cocky 4s. for his wheat
during the next couple of years the rest is
easy. But there is no good reason why
the Australian taxpayer should be asked to
do this on the off-chance of the Government
being able to get through on the deal; and
there are plenty of good reasons why he
shouldn’t consent even if he were
asked. The gamble is big enough as it
is without the Commonwealth undertaking
to indemnify Cocky at the public expense
against risks that are common to every busi-
ness. The basis of the existing arrange-
ment is that growers shall share equally in
the realisation of the crop and, by the elim-
ination of the speculator, secure the full
selling value. By this the grower has been
protected and the State is not involved in
risk. That is far enough for the Australian
Government to go. If, however, the British
Government feels that to secure a supply for
its own people it should bid higher than was
sufficient to provoke the production of well
over 300,000,000 bushels in Australia in the
last two harvests, there will be nothing to
object to by anyone.
Conscientious Objection.
Whether the British method of dealing with
the conscientious objector to military ser-
vice is all that it might be or not, the result
has shown that the number who will face the
consequences of refusing to bear arms is a
negligible quantity. Up to August last only
1700 had been arrested on the charge, and
900 of these, who had been actually enrolled
in the army, were afterwards tried by court-
martial for refusing duty. The procedure
in England is to hold the person with a
scruple against war in the safety of a civil
prison pending inquiry by the Central
Appeal Tribunal. When that body con-
siders that the case is a good one, the
objector is offered work of national import-
ance under civil control, lie can take the
work or stay in prison. The small per-
centage who first and last find their con-
sciences stubborn enough to stand the test
proves that the reign of peace per medium
of passive resistance is still a long way off.
With the prospect of work before him and
the means of escape open by way of war, the
average person who has suddenly developed
a distaste for blood will generally hear the
bugle call. Australia’s conscientious ob-
jectors at the Referendum poll appeared to
number hundreds of thousands; but, then,
the casting of a No vote didn’t entail the
awful alternative of real toil which couldn’t
be side-stepped.

“Copabella”: The official methods of the
lately-deceased conscription campaign were
so full of bad tactics as to give the impres-
sion that the Government was trying to

phase suggests that the new recruiting
scheme is to run on precisely the same lines.
That every soldier gained by this last absurd
campaign will cost the country about his
weight in gold is obvious to most men ; but.
unless the whole thing is a gigantic face-
saving fake, at least a pretence of sincerity
of motive might, be made. The movement
was formally inaugurated in our suburb last,
week, and this was the method of it. The
ex-officio local chairman is a notorious “anti”'
with two eligible sons driving expensive
motor-cars in the teeth of the men it is
hoped to recruit. He only has to rise to his
feet in a public meeting to have the query
thrown at him: “What about your boys?”
The ex-officio deputy chairman’s family of
four eligibles' hasn't given one to the army:
two of them plunged into matrimony at the
first hint of universal service, and father
wept tears of blood in an attempt to save
a third from the tender mercies dealt out
in the recent Exemption Courts. The ex-
officio electoral chairman of the divisions of
which we are a part stumped the country
during the Conscription campaign as
an apostle not of Anti-Compulsion but of
We’ve-Done-Enough. The organising secre-

did his work then conscientiously, and natu-
rally is anathema to the 900 cold-footed

funk-parade. The sight of him approaching
is sufficient to nip sprouting patriotism in
the bud. As a and Gallipoli vet r
eran he would probably do excellently some-
where else —so lie’s left here. Perhaps that's
why ! Remains then the recruiter. He, too,
is a and Loos soldier, but he is
turned on to this work in a strange suburb,
without so much as a tin tag on him to show
who he is, and his accosting of the “pro-
spect” mostly earns the question: “What
th’ell’s it got to do with you?” I showed
him round on the first night after his ap-
pointment. I heard him address three groups
and be told to “Get ” twice and “Go to
” the other time !
The Australian Commonwealth’s present
bad habit of making the War Loan free of
income taxes is unfair to the small investor.
Income taxes in Australia are almost in-
variably graduated; and the Commonwealth
has allowed a full deduction of £156 from
income derived from any property. That is
to say, a person whose income from pro-
perty is £156 or less has paid no income tax.
Therefore the provision freeing an invest-
ment in the War Loan from income tax is
worth nothing to the small investor. But it
is worth a great deal to the large investor.
In effect, the richer the investor the more the
War Loan is worth to him; and that is a
rotten system. And it looks still more
rotten when it is offered by a Government
which calls itself Labor. The proper plan
is to make this investment subject to taxa-
tion like any other investment and fix the
interest at a much higher figure accordingly.
Moreover, if that were done the investment
would look far more attractive.

A quaint Sydney delegate to Melbourne
—one Harris —has objected to J. C. Wat-
son’s proposal to bring the apparently-
hopeless Aus. Nat. Democratic League into
a straight line with the National Federa-
tion “because the Victorian section was in-
tent on advocating High Protection.” While
Mr. Harris was attempting to identify the
new party with the old Freetrade low-wage
crowd, some Victorians were interviewing
Customs Minister Archibald for the re-
moval of the duty on imported wattle-bark,
now 30s. a ton. “Tanners,” said Zwar,
president of the Vic. Tanners’ Association,
“required 30,000 tons, and not one-third of
this was in sight in Australia, and it was
obvious that 20,000 tons of it would have
to be imported.” Kane, of the North Mel-
bourne Bark Mills, said his firm required
2500 tons and had between 200 and 300
tons:—-
There was no possibility of getting the balance of
2200 tons locally. The areas under wattle were
steadily diminishing. An abnormally wet December
had put back stripping until it clashed with the
wheat harvest, and there were not enough men to do
both. The bark would not be worth more than 30s.
to 40s. per ton. America and Europe had already
awakened to be strong rivals by buying South
African bark, and they were not handicapped with
the same dut5 7 as Australian tanners.
Here you have the blessings of Freetrade.
Our main work in the handling of one of
the “natural resources” bleated about by
the Reids and Cooks has been to supply
wattle-seed to Africa in order that we might
buy back the bark in competition with
America and Europe! And yet the quaint
Mr. Harris is against the Victorian end of
the new party because it advocates High
Protection.
Song of the Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So long ago, so long ago, when Spring was
laughing-eyed.
The brawling tide of action swept a-near and
thundered wide,
A rancor-woven tempest rose and raged
incessantly,
And Youth awoke and steeled himself to
cross the leagues of sea.
Youth with roses in his hat
Fared a-field to bear his load,
And the hoofs beat rat-ta-tat
Down the Parramatta-road.
And vigor marched with hope beside—so
long ago it seems—
So long ago, ere hate destroyed their slender
woof of dreams:
When comrades voiced a chorus high that
spoke of hearts a-swell,
They passed away together down the aisle of
blurred farewell.
To the martial strains of war.
In a human stream they flowed,
And the foot-beats sounded far
Down the Parramatta-road.
The long highway is ringing yet and now
the chorus swells
In sweetly-measured praises of the silent
Dardanelles.
And other men are marching for the coming
great advance
With their comrades from Australia out in
Sinai and France.
Some are up and marching back —
As in other days they strode
To the ghastly night attack
On a crimson-wetted road —•
Mended men are marching back
Down the Parramatta-road !

GERARDY.
White Ants.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Treasury strong-room, where the gold reserve
of the Commonwealth is stored, has been invaded by
white ants. They arrived in some boxes, contain-
ing copper coins, from India. The Director of
Quarantine was called in and administered poison.
White ants are gnawing at a nation’s gold—
White ants from overseas.
The shining coin we fondled well of old
Is crawled upon by these
In some still place where, hidden from the
light,
We thought the lid was on the treasure tight.
White ants are gnawung at a nation’s
strength-
Home-bred or foreign-grown.
Through strike and threat of strike we learn
at length
How little is our own
Of that potential wealth that only toil
Can conquer from the richest, kindliest soil.
White ants are gnawing at a nation’s hope.
We know 7 not whence they came.
Blindly along the dark we stumbling grope
With weary feet and lame,
While politicians without shame or pride
The seamless garment of our faith divide.
White ants ■ White ants ! The woodwork
crumbles fast.
May the foundations stand
That on the ruins we may build at last
. With firmer, surer hand.
White ants! White ants! And still they
gnaw and bite
Till someone poisons party out of sight.
N.S.W.

FIRE—MARINE—WORKMEN’S COMPENSATION Insurances at Lowest Rates.
How the Farmer Benefits;
A glance at the magnificent results attained by other co-operative cos. indicates
what the Farmers’ and Settlers’ Co-op. Insurance Co. of Australia, Ltd., can do
for the farmers of Australia in handling their insurance.
For instance, the Canterbury Farmers’ Co-op. Association (N.Z.) pays 8£ p.c.
dividend, 5 p.c. bonus on purchased goods, and 15 p.c. on selling commissions, and
has built up a powerful financial institution.
The Farmers’ and Settlers’ Co-operative Insurance Co- of Australia is established

The Wait to Wade from Mud to Blood.
When proceedings were interrupted by the
mud 12 months ago the newspapers were
full of yarns about the German’s empty
pantry. The same thing is happening now.
Before the water had drained away last
year the German had. launched his attack on
Verdun and he has been busy all this winter
in Roumania. That he will not p fight hard
when the campaign is again resumed on
the Western Front is far too much to expect.
The chances are that it will be more terrible
than any that has yet taken place. Gigantic
preparations have been made by the Allies;
and the readiness with which the German
met the danger threatening him in the Bal-
kans is in itself sufficient evidence that lie
has the force and organisation to encounter
still heavier blows elsewhere. Nevertheless,
the very fact that the pending engagements
will be terrific gives hope that the final
phases of the war are not far distant. Mean-
while tin' appearance of an enemy raider
in the South Atlantic and the activity of
the submarine are reminders that the
enemy’s powers of destruction are still
strong, and on neither sea nor land have
undergone any serious diminution during the
past 12 months. Should he with the assistance
of outsiders have succeeded in establishing
bases of supply in out-of-the-way places for
commerce destroyers, the work ahead of the
navy will become more exciting than
ever; and it is probable that, knowing of
the diversion of shipping to secure for
Britain and France supplies of Australian
wheat, something of this kind has at least
been attempted. The possibilities of such
an enterprise carried out on a large scale
are not particularly pleasant to think about.
However, lost the situation should be with-
out its comic relief, Greece still comes to the
rescue. In the history of the war this
gigantic joke will require a separate volume
to itself. The other day there appeared to
be a doubt in London as to whether Von
Falkeniiayn had not arrived in Greece on
an aeroplane and started organising ’Tino’s
army to attack the Allies in the rear. There
was no certainty for several days one way
or the other; in fact, the idea that there
is any certainty about anything at nil in that
locality until after it has happened is
mighty hard to swallow.
Cable from London :
The Bar Council has overwhelmingly defeated a
motion proposing the admission of women to prac-
tise at the Bar. The Attorney-General (Sir Robert
Finlay, K.C.) and others argued that it would be
unfair to consider tlie question while 1300 barristers
were absent on active service.
A woman has no right to have any brains.
She can be a motor-driver or a munition-
worker or a farm-laborer while the men are
away fighting; but to be a lawyer or even
start to acquire the necessary equipment
while the men in that line of business are
away would be morally wrong! If this is the
best argument these mouldy defenders of a
sex monopoly have, it looks as if they ought
to be inoculated against the ravages of
senile decay.
Boyd, the Freetrade M.11.R. for Ilenty
(Vic.), recently introduced a deputation to
the Customs asking for a duty on eggs from
Asia. According to Boyd, “Australian hens
are good enough to lay Australian eggs for
Australian stomachs.” But Australian men
are not good enough to make Australian
wool into clothes for Australians or Aus-
tralian hides into Australian boots or Aus-
tralian iron into Australian steel. Appar-
ently the Freetrade gospel is that the Aus-
tralian hen is a vastly superior animal to
Australian men.
Burgess, the ex-Treasurer who forms
most of Lee’s 'majority in the Tasmanian
Assembly, has asked the Hobart Liberals
to let him resign. He says he is sick of the
whole business and that the Liberal Ministry
is landing the country in a worse mess than
its Labor forerunner. The Government’s
war-time economy has bumped up the
Speck’s expenditure from £1,294,709 for
1915-16 to an estimated £1,405,191 for the
present financial year, while in the last
three years the interest bill has soared from
£464,000 a year to £618,000 and is still soar-
ing. They persuaded William to hang on
for a while, but he is pretty gloomy about
it.
The young man defended himself with an
alihi against a charge of assault on Sunday
at Randwick (Sydney). He led in wit-
nesses to prove that at 6.30 a.m. he had
taken a lady to Bondi to surf, and they
breakfasted there. At 10 a.m. he joined a
mixed motor party, which took him to
National Park. At 4 p.m. he was on a
launch in Port Jackson, and stayed for a
picnic tea. At 8 p.m. he was at a club, play-
ing billiards; and before 10 p.m. he was at
the house of “a friend.” He reached home
at 1 a.m. Evidently he was a religious
voting man, who believed in keeping the
Sabbath wholly.
