Auditorium “Ramona’
Once upon a time Sumner Locke wrote
a book called “Mum Dawson, Boss.” Then
she wrote a play with the same title. It
would be interesting to find out where that
play has gone to. At Sydney Criterion on
Saturday Bert Bailey produced a play whose
only connection with the book was the fact
that a few of the characters bore the same
names. The central character, Mum Daw-
son, is subordinated to Old Man Dawson,
and even when she makes a pretence of
taking charge, we learn in the next act that
she has been over-ruled. There is much
shouting but very little boss about this new
Mum. The play that Bert built, or had
built for him, is as like his first Steele Rudd
success as is possible; evidently this is
the shape into which all Australian plays
must be squeezed before the only Australian
who has the pluck to produce Australian
plays will present them. The formula is a
simple one—broad farce, peopled by carica-
tures of the folks Outback, and invaded at
frequent intervals by a fiercely-melodramatie
and much-complicated plot. There were in
the first of this brand of play many touches
of real comedy, and the character drawing
was sometimes unspoilt by exaggeration.
Pathos was also introduced. In “Mum Da'”
son, Boss” it becomes bathos.
Sumner Locke’s story showed the humors
of a struggling selection; Sumner Locke’s
play deals chiefly with a get-rich-quick
scheme and the trials and troubles of a
manufacturing concern. Certainly the
second act. which shows Old Man Dawson
as a harried managing director, is very
funny, chiefly because Bert Bailey carries
most of the comedy upon his worried shoul-
ders. But the rest of the play suffers from
too much loud-voiced financial plot; and the
brief attempts at pathos are too obviously
dragged in by the hair. Occasionally a
remark by Sumner Locke can be detected
amid the medley, but mostly these are
swamped under wearisome caricatures of
superfluous characters.
Without Bert Bailey in his perennial
part of Dad the play would sag under its
heavy weight of plot. He carries the
whole thing on his shoulders, and carries it
“MUM DAWSON, BOSS," AT SYDNEY
CRITERION.
Dick Bellairs, country undertaker, and
Harry McDonna, storekeeper.
triumphantly. He gets small assistance
from Lilias Adeson’s Mum. She shouts her
way through, but there is fitPe pretence at
individuality behind her vigorous methods.
Fred Macdonald is hidden beneath a
beard, though occasionally the real Uncle
Dawson emerges. Guy Hastings and
Leslie Woods, the rival Rufus Wallingfords,
double-cross each other loudly and persist-
ently whenever The Plot takes charge of the
stage. Edmund Duggan makes a facially
expressive comedy Wallingford. Richard
Bellairs is a wearisome undertaker, and
there is too much of Harry Mac Donna’s
storekeeper, while IV illie Driscoll provides a
grotesque character that bears a suspicious
resemblance to his famous part in ‘ On Our
Selection.” Laura Roberts is hardly con-
vincing in a part out of her range, and
Clarice Hardwicke is satisfactory as a very
knowing ingenue. The season is limited to
two weeks, and the play is worth rushing roi
its delightful farcical second act, which is
chiefly Bert Bailey.
The Fullers’ “Bunyip, at Sydney
Adelphi, is still proving to misguided man-
agements that an Australian panto, is just
the tonic all Australian youngsters up to
70 have been wanting. Even the breath of
the gorgeous bushfire is refreshing compared
with the twang and slang of the imported
shows.

applaud the show at the Tivoli (Sydney)

must not be overlooked. A Chinese girl, she
is one of the daintiest figures that has ap-
peared on the Rickards stage, and her melo-
dious voice has alreadv won many friends.
The Bulletin is one of them.

Among the Taits’ captures for the cast of
“Very Good Eddie,” which opens at Sydney
Palace on February 10, are two sweet young
Amurkans, Fayette Perry and Lilian Tucker.
Fayette is a diminutive blonde with spark-
ling blue eyes, a rose in each cheek, and
enough natural vivacity to turn a Methodist
funeral into a wake. Lately she was
appearing with Andrew Higginson in “The
Amber Princess” in Noo Yark. Lilian
Tucker is bigger and fairer and has the eyes
that poets’ dreams are made of. It is
alleged that the beauty of a Norwegian
ancestor is struggling for recognition in her
charming countenance, but the girl was born
Amurkan, thinks Amurkan, and wears the
latest Amurkan clothes. Before packing up
for Australia, she was using her soprano in
“The Soldier Boy” in Philadelphia.
Romance and melodrama on Sydney
screens this week :
Lyceum and Crystal Palace : “The Midnight
Riot,” the fourth instalment of Billie Burke’s
“Gloria’s Romance.”
Empress : A five-reel film of Maurice Leblanc’s
famous detective story, “Arsene Lupin.”
Hoyt’s: “Dorian’s Divorce,” an unusual mixture
of comedy and melodrama; “Her Mother’s Secret,”
in which the Fox Co. thinks of a few things that
may happen to a man who tries to run two homes.
As regards “The House of Glass” at Mel-
bourne Royal, one is first struck by the fact
that the heroine is introduced without a
character. Margaret Case lacks friends,
relatives and commonsense. Her simplicity
is a libel on girls that can earn 18 dollars
a week by stenography in Noo Yark City.
She is picked up casually by a young chauf-
feur who is also a twice-convicted crook,
and he apparently takes her for a joy-ride
and wins her confidence. Whilst courting
Margaret the jaunty crook burgles some-
body’s safe and secures jewellery worth 70.000
dollars and 20.000 dollars fn cash, where-
upon he tells his girl that he lias inherited
an unexpected fortune and will flee with
her at once for matrimonial purposes. The
artless maiden and the young professional
thief have known one another just a fort-
night when the curtain rises. Meanwhile
they have attracted the attention of detec-
tive Carroll by their conspicuous holiday-
making. They had both chucked up their
billets and gone shopping for four days be-
fore they were to leave New York. Hence
the natural police assumption that the lady
was in the know, if not actually in the bur-
glary. She herself was incapable of assump-
tions or suspicions. She took it for granted
that her young man’s legacy had been handed
to him immediately after the decease of his
supposititious uncle. When he produced a
pearl necklace worth 50.000 dollars she
accepted it as a fitting wedding present from
a bridegroom in his position. A babe unborn
is not more free from misgivings than Mar-
garet was ere detective Carroll came along
and caught Jimmy Burke in her room and
found the pearls in her travelling trunk.
That, the prologue, is Margaret’s Past
in the play, where she is 10 years older.
Having served IS months’ gaol owing to
her inability to tell the truth and shame the
jury, she is released on parole. But she
breaks her parole and escapes to Kansas
City, where she assumes stenographing and
marries the boss.' He takes her without
question, even as she had taken Jimmy
Burke. Soon his keen business intellect raises
him to the dignity of railway magnate and
makes him a terror to evildoers. The drama
in due course finds excuses for Jimmy and
Carroll to cross Margaret’s primrose path.
Jimmy turns up as a penitent and chas-
tened chauffeur; but the detective is an
incorruptible sleuth always sniffing for
trouble. That is his point of difference from
other Yankee detectives in previous dramas.
He is horribly incorruptible. lie wants the
silly Margaret who broke her parole, and
when he finds her in the wife of the great
railway manager new trouble begins.
Agonies of apprehension ! Groundless sus-
picion on the part of the husband who hap-
pens to have a derry on criminals! Kindly
efforts by mutual friends to avert scandai.
Manly behavior of chastened crook. Happy
ending.
* * * #
Robert Gfeig, as a large, fat lawyer,
gets the most grateful job in the drama. He
is always making jovial comments on free
drinks, or taking professional measures to
defeat the ruthless endeavors of the chuckle-
headed sleuth, or telling the worried hus-
band that he ought to be proud of his
distracted wife, which is very soothing to
the girls in the amphitheatre. Styan is a
harmless incidental millionaire. Louis Kim-
ball is an engaging young crook to whose
countenance and figure 10 years are even
as 10 minutes, and who never breaks the
rules of chivalry. Boyd Irwin is the same
hard, angular policeman as usual, except
that he tries to modify his tone of brutality.
Frank Harvey—a good actor with a monoto-
nous manner- —is the shrewd railway man
who marries his 26-year-old stenographer
without guessing what a simpleton she used
to be, and who sees nothing extraordinary
in her melodramatic agitation until she disr
closes her Past to him. Miss Florence
Rockwell is the new star in the part of an
injured unaccountable innocent. The part
is conventionally theatrical and the actress,
who starts with the disadvantage of looking
older than 19, plays it in a suitable manner.
Margaret Case is a person of limited intel-
ligence. She has no moral courage what-
ever, no candor, no turn for dissimulation —
in short, no “character” to speak of. When
she is alarmed she wears her terror on her
sleeve and when she is found out she
crumples up. The intense feebleness of
heroine Margaret should commend “A House
of Glass” to all self-supporting spinsters
who dream of having romantic careers
thrust upon them.
At Melbourne Princess’s the picturesque
old comedy of “She Stoops to Conquer”' is
still going for nearly as much as it is worth.
The humor has the manner of a very by-
gone age, but the characters remain as inter-
estingly human as though Tony Lumpkin
took his mother for a circuitous drive in a
motor-car and the home of the Hardcastles
had the telephone laid on. The Wilkie pro-
duction of this graceful old comedy includes
all the gags which reverent revivalists have
allowed to enter it. These presumptuous
intrusions are an undoubted success; some
people refuse to consider that a wheeze is
necessarily out of place because Goldsmith
didn’t put it there. Goldsmith is not a
more immortal wag than Shakspeare, any-

At Melbourne King’s Allen Dotme O’Shea
maintains his proximity to Rose McMichael,
lesoite Captain Aubert’s endeavors to keep
them apart. Aubert is Allen’s disgraceful
rival, though he never amounts to a menace
in the matrimonial field. But Allen persists
in treating him seriously, and even elimi-
nates the warrior in a spectacular manner
when he wants the sentimental arena all to
himself. The hero, however, is not specially
fierce or incendiary in wooing his lady. He is
more suggestive of glucose than paraffin.
Still his donah seems to prefer his gradual
method, probably because it gives her a
chance to pose as a diffident Miss and bring
her battery of pouts and dimples into play.
At Melbourne Tivoli the departure of
Weems has left The Follies in much the
same state as heretofore. The advt. still
insists on calling the new bill “wonderful,”
but the people who pay the bill have the
right to call it what they like.
The possibilities of yeast in raising inter-
est on a mortgage are disclosed by June
Caprice at Hoyt’s, Melbourne, this week.
June sells the substance to keep the mort-
gagee from the door in a five-part Fox sob
entitled “Little Miss Happiness.” Two
familiar apparitions, Francis Bushman and
Beverly Bayne, reappear on Hoyt’s screens,
and are again uplifted by their mutual fasci-
nation in a new Metro subject, “Man and
His Soul.” “The Valiants of Virginia,”
screened only at Olympia, features the pro-
pinquity of Arthur Shirley and Kathlyn
Williams in a tangled story of sentiment
covering three or four decades.
Nigel Brock, director of the St. Kilda
(Vic.) Dandies, has invested in a new
dancer, Linda Ockleshaw, who made her
first appearance on Saturday night. Linda
has the hall-mark of a J.C.W. education, and
dances effectively alone or in specialty
duets with the nimble John Campbell. The
little Scotchman, by the \yay, has tempo-
rarily dropped his dragtime recitals for a
Lauderesque vein of humor in Hieland cos-
tume. Also his usual vocal accomplice, Ida
Newton, has acquired a new song anent a
strange actor-adorer with a red, red nose
and a crumpled career. Ida declares that the
mummer’s hug is quite artistic, and The
Bulletin allows that her manner of describ-
ing it is en suite.
“Ramona.” at Melbourne Auditorium, con-
tinues to show how the enlightening influ-
ence of Uncle Sam was spread among the

forated Injuns who have fallen under the

“Damaged Goods” is safely launched at
Adelaide Royal. Some good brisk influence
was brought to bear in advance, and the
Holy City has decided to accept it as a
moral crusade instead of something offensive.
The opening private performance was heav-
ily patronised by doctors and parsons, and
the booking is good. The Tivoli is having
the briefest of holidays. The Andersonian
melo. co., which came in last winter for
weeks and stayed for months, is to reopen
almost at once. The Majestic’s variety bdl
is strong on the musical side, with Moli-
nari’s tenor; but Rodriguez on the wire is
a pleasant feature, too. Real summer at
last is good for the Dandies, though late
daylight is not. West’s have the delightful
Dorsey expedition films, Wondergraph shows
Marguerite Clark and Hazel Dawn (and
what more could a patron desire?), and the
Empire has a “sweet” story on the advan-
tages of not letting baby die.
W. E. Morrisby, who passed out recently
at Bellerive (Tas.), aged 79, was the son
of one of the Norfolk Island settlers who
were moved to Tasmania over a century
ago. The Morrisby family settled down at.
Sandford after they were transolanted and
multiplied so that in those parts you are
fairly safe in addressing a.stranger as Mor-
risby, unless lie happens to be a Calvert.
An Adelaide woman has been found guilty
of conduct “not that of a lady,” and fined
£5 7s. for it. That was not the legal charge,
but it was how it struck the Bench. The
technical offence was carrying a dog on a
tranjcar and refusing to put it off; and the
unladylike part was telling the conductor
not to be “so damned officious.” A real lady
hardly ever swears in public.
Senator McDougall has been protesting
against the exportation of our surplus crop
of onions to the U.S.A. and Canada. Of
course. The more of other people’s manu-
factures we import and the more of our
own products we retain the more goods we
have in the country— i.e., the richer we
become —obviously (always provided the
wretched foreigners don’t require payment
for their goods).
When Parson Marsden went to Maoriland
in 1814 to Christianise the natives, he took
with him three young men as assistants or
teachers. One was the grandfather of poet
Henry Kendall; another was named King.
The latter is brought to memory by the
announcement that Mrs. Philip King, of
Waiaka, Auckland, has just celebrated her
101st birthday. She arrived in Auckland
in 1840. Her late husband was the infant
son of one of Marsden’s helpers at the time
of the party’s arrival in M.L. in 1814.
“Bluebeard”: The inhabitants of North
Melbourne are living a life of plenty, thanks
to the proximity of the Royal Park Camp.
Owing to the methods of the army commis-
sariat, much prog for the soldiers is wasted.
Loaves of bread and just-perforated tins of
jam and legs of mutton are handed to the
youth of North Melbourne who swarm
round the mess-sheds for the overflow of
food ; and the sight of young Bert or ’Erb
staggering home with a bulging sack is not
uncommon. And yet our watchwords are
supposed to be “Organise! Economise!”
As yet no red-tape has been given to the
kids to tie the mouths of their food sacks
with ; but that may come.
“Silas”: People are questioning whether
woman is built for hard yacker, declaring
that the sex’s war-time effort is a mere
flash in the pan. Well, I can produce a
woman of 38, the mother of a family, who
puts in her eight hours daily wood cutting at
Gembrook (Vic.). She does her house-work
before going on and after knocking off, and
she has for some time past earned Bs. a day.
She fells the stick, crosscuts it, and splits
the logs (seven-foot lengths ) all on her own,
and when questioned recently expressed her
surprise that other women in the district did
not seize the opportunity of getting “easy
money.”
“Playbird” : The State-school kids of Vic-
toria have raised by weekly pennies, concerts
and bazaars no less than £151,000 towards
their own Patriotic Fund. - Besides this,
each district in turn provides food for the
military hospitals, to the value of about £7OO
per month. Every soldier in the hospitals
had a poultry dinner for Christmas, sup-
plied him by the nippers of the Warrnambool
district, and there were enough birds to
give the sick in other hospitals a wishbone.
Some of the youngsters showed amazing-
ingenuity in raising money. One boy, in bis
spare time, made wooden horses and hawked
them from door to door. Another took a con-
tract to clean up the local recreation
ground in his holidays. But the dinkum boy
borrowed a horse and cart and by night dug
up half an acre of potatoes, took them to
market and made a good sale. It was fine
to see the nipper’s face when he handed to
the teacher the money he got. (I’m not
going to tell you who the teacher was.)
It was better still to see the face of the
owner of the spuds when he found out about
it.
Sands’s indispensable Directory of N. S.
Wales makes its 57th appearance in its usual
attractive style. Despite the prohibitive
price of paper, in addition to the thousands
of pages of addresses, the 1917 edition
includes a useful collection of commercial
and general information.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Harrison O.”: Allan Wilkie’s “She
Stoops to Conquer” is marred by puerile
gagging, just as his “School for Scandal”
was. Any possibility of young Marlow’s
nervousness in Kate’s presence being
accepted as credible vanishes when, in
attempting to say something about “hypoc-
risy,” he can only get out “Hip-hip-hip—
hooray.” Another delicate touch of humor
is the misreading of a letter beginning
“Dear Squire” as -“Dear Squirt.” Wilkie,
in a foreword on the programme, attempts
to excuse this drivel, urging that “gagging”
is “quite permissible.” Only an actor would
put forward this impudent plea. Presum-
ably Mr. Wilkie would also consider it “per-
missible” for a scene-painter to add a few
touches to a landscape by Corot, or for
the conductor of a vaudeville orchestra to
enrich “Mhdame Butterfly” with a bar or
two, or, perhaps, a couple of arias which
unfortunately Puccini had not thought of.
“Glen II.” : A movement is on foot to in-
troduce Dennis’s Sentimental Bloke and his
Doreen to film students. If the scheme comes
off Ginger Mick will follow —though to me
at least there appear to be greater pictorial
possibilities in the story of the “gallant
gentleman” of Spadger’s Lane than in that
of Bill and his diner.
“M.P.A.” : Beaumont Smith (B. 11/1/-17)
claims that the light in and around Adelaide
is better than in any other part of the
Commonwealth for filming purposes. I
would like to know the grounds on which
Beau, bases his opinion. The cinemato-
grapher employed by the Union Theatres at
the co.’s dark rooms at St. Kilda (Melb.)
compiles a weekly budget of film news made
up of various topical subjects received from
the principal centres in the Common-
wealth. In Hobart the topicals are
Man's idea of woman, and woman's idea of
man. Any old pantomime.
taken by a man who is admittedly an
amateur, and the negatives he has sent
through to St. Kilda have firmly convinced
the expert that Hobart light is easily
the best. Hobart light is much softer
than the glare of Adelaide, Melbourne and
Sydney, and the fine particles of dust which
trouble an operator even on a calm day
when he is working on an object in the dis-
tance, are not in evidence in the Tassy
films. There is such a thing as too much
light. Take for instance “Melbourne
Fashions,” shown regularly in Pathe’s “Aus-
tralian Gazette” several years ago. Bertel,
the Paths operator, got ample light for the
job in an ordinary photographer’s studio,
and invariably obtained good results. Too
many mistakes have been made in Aus-
tralia by tinkering with artificial light. It’s
very tricky. The lamps installed in a studio
built at St. Kilda were all the wrong type
and no possible good could result from them.
Ilereault, Pathe’s Australian manager who
was killed early in the war filmed the
Argus machinery with the aid of a special
set of electric lamps and obtained a splendid
subject. Herbert Findlay did a similar pic-
ture of the S. M. Herald, with even better
results, because he chalked the wheels and
prominent parts of the machines. But that
sort of. work is most difficult and the films
very uncertain.
“Van Ec-k”: The advent of the Famous
Players-Lasky people into Australia as
film-producers should be the best thing in
the movie line this country has known. These
organisations are two of the few that devote
time to art. Leaving mullock and sensa-
tional dope to picture cos. that spring up
like mushrooms in the night, they have built
up a reputation in America that is second
to none. A.new company, which has taken
over the agency that hitherto released their
films in Sydney, promises to produce pic-
tures not only for Australia but for their
own huge market in U.S.A. and other Eng-
lish-speaking countries. With such an
assured field the co. can spend lavish sums
and still keep a profit. In the way of act-
ing, Australian talent is to get a show, so
we may look out for Mary Pickfords and
Pauline Fredericks.
“Lou Par”: An interesting personality
in the show world who blew into Sydney
a while ago is J. A. Lipman, an Adelaidean
whose meteoric rise has attracted attention
in picture circles. Lipman, who is an actor
of more than fair ability and can do a bit
of writing, left this country a couple of
years ago for America with a pocketful of
hope and a truckload of grit. He returns as
president of one of the biggest film exchanges
in New York. One of his first acts in Syd-
ney was to sell his interests in Famous
Players-Lasky concern and open an ex-
change for the rival Mutual Co.
The first encouraging result of the Taits’
opposition is the formation of a new J.C.W.
Comedy Co. Perhaps the imminent arrival
of a Taits’ Musical Comedy Co. will result
in a development of louder vocal talent
among the J.C.W. opposition. Within the.
memory of Australia’s oldest inhabitants
there never was such a dearth of voices in
light opera as there has been of late.
“ Jeune Premier”: No wonder “The
Woman,” which was recently given a flutter
at the Theatre Royal (Melb.), reminded
playgoers and picturegoers of many another
’Murkan piece. It was produced quite long
enough ago for every Yankee dramatist to
have copied anything that was worth copying
in it. And no dramatist earning his pork
and beans in that quick-lunch country is
any good unless he can copy. “The
Woman” was “tried out” by Dave Belasco
at the New National, Washington, in April,
1911. Cuyler Hastings, by the way, figured
as the Hon. Matthew Standisli in the origi-
nal cast.
“Chudah”: Strunin, of the Yiddish
Dramatic Co. at Melbourne Temperance
Hall, has just celebrated his fifteenth anni-
versary as an actor with the production of
the grand spectacular opera “Bar Kochba,”
by Goldenfarden. Seven ladies, four gents;
mixed chorus of 20; ballet, directed by
Barbaloff, reminiscent of Genee’s Moscow
dancers; principal . danseuse, Madame
Homaz.
.“Rye Dry” : Recent vaudeville booking-
arrangements between Fuller and the
African Theatre Trust gives many chances
to Australian performers to make good out
of their own country. Latest doing well
are Tommy Armstrong and his wife, Mabs
Howarth, and old “Jimmy” Bain and

comedian Claude Dampier and Irene Vere,
his wife.

“8.J.”: “White-eyed Kaffir” Chirgwin is
now the owner of a pub at Bigbury-on-sea,
England, but makes a return every now and
again to the ’alls. Lately he was at Lon-
don Palladium. Australian Albert Whelan
(Waxman) was on the same bill.
“Screen”: E. J. Carroll’s big film
“Ramona,” showing at Melbourne Audito-
rium, owes its birth to a woman who wouldn’t
be suppressed. Her name was Helen Jack-
son, and years ago she was employed by
the U.S. Government to investigate the
alleged illtreatment of Indians in California.
When her report arrived it was duly
pigeon-holed at Washington. But Helen
wasn’t taking anything like that, and wrote
the result of her inquiries into the novel
“Ramona.” It made the greatest sensation
since “Uncle Tom,” and before the boom was
over million copies had been sold.
“Ancient Prompter”: Australia’s old
friend G. P. Huntley is making fun for
London just now in “Selling a Pup.” It is
a farcical sketch in which “G.P.” is assisted
by Titian-haired Irene Browne. How versa-
tile she is ! She played in Shakspeare and
the “Royal Divorce” in Australia, and since
her return to England she has tried comedy,
musical-comedy and music-hall sketches.
Some enterprising manager will capture her
for revue purposes next, and they’ll both
congratulate themselves.

SAYS PHOSPHATE INCREASES
STRENGTH AND ENDURANCE.
Weak, nervous, debilitated men and women
who become exhausted after the slightest
mental or physical exertion, to whom sleep
is denied, and whose drawn, haggard fea-
tures bear mute testimony to their pitiable
condition, can regain health, strength and
endurance in a few weeks by obtaining from
the chemist a supply of 5-gra.in T)itro-phos-
phate tablets, and taking one regularly at
each meal. All the above painful conditions
are due to weak nerves and lack of nervous
energy. Bitro-phosphaie teects the starved,
shrunken nerves, and builds up a reserve of
nervous energy by supplying in the only
assimilable form those phosphoric food
elements which are the life of the nervous
system. The results following its use are
most gratifying. Sleeplessness usually dis-
appears within a week or 10 days, thin
people put on flesh, strength returns to
shrunken muscles, the power of endurance is
doubled, sunken cheeks fill out. the eyes
become bright, and all the old nervous
worries and dreads disappear, and life
becomes what it should be—worth living.
Try it and see.— Apvt.

Note. —According to some, the fez is built like
it is so that the Faithful can have an uninterrupted
view and not miss Mahomet when he calls down for
’em. “The Hats” or “the Hatted” is a term of
contempt or hatred for the infidel in strenuous
times. In peace we are all pretty much alike, but
in war the Turks are perhaps more like us than
any other of our enemies.—lLL.

“Lance” : It was in the earlier days of the
war, and that tough old warrior Col. of
the nth Battalion had the greater part of
his unit paraded before him on a charge of
something or other absolutely deplorable
from the army point of view. As each
prisoner passed through the orderly-room he
rapped out the penalty awarded, and added
as the culprit marched off, “You’re another!”
This remark puzzled most of the men exceed-
ingly. until one hard-doer came up for Ins
turn. The old fire-eater rapped out the
penalty as usual and added “You’re an-
other!” Before the orderly-sergeant had
time to roar “Right-turn-quick-march the
prisoner put in very briskly: "Don t uuder-
standchir, sir.” The old chap looked up at
him quickly. “Well, I’m damn sure, he
roared, “that as soon as you get outside
you’ll say, ‘Ain’t ’e a old - ! ant *
I’m getting in first!”

“Trooper Bluegum”: There are weird and
wonderful happenings in the war game, but
I guess the record belongs to Lieut. Ilepping-
stone, of the th Australian Camel Regi-
ment. He is, so far as any of us know, the
only soldier to be knocked off his camel by
an aeroplane. We were out in the desert
and “Hippy” was streaking along at a great
bat when a ’plane swooped down. He did
a Tod Sloan act on his camel’s neck, but
couldn’t escape the bird. It sent him flying,
broke his arm and did other frightfulness.
When last seen his camel was doing a
marathon towards Romani and the aero-
plane was turning somersaults.

“Trallwm” : Immediately upon the procla-
mation of the new summer-time fad our local
Parsons’ Union saw in it a chance to aug-
ment the collection-plate by putting on the
evening service by an hour, thus roping in
the beach-frequenters and the lovers of the
afternoon nap. The reverends jumped at the
idea and made the necessary announcements
of the changed hour. Then came the shock
of their secluded lives! Their congregations
struck —the oldsters deprecatingly, the
younger worshippers with unanimity and
enthusiasm! “Come out at !).30 —and go
straight home?” said the couples. “1 don t
think!” There was never such a furore
since Kikiyu. Most of tin 1 shepherds
climbed down without trying the projected
experiment; one stood from under after one
Sunday; the strong man of the union left
word for the later hour to have a month’s
trial and —skipped for a month’s holiday.
Ali the sinners of the suburb are enjoying
the situation, and the parsons are going
about wearing the expression one might
expect a sheepdog to nurse if a lamb had
suddenly turned and bitten him.

close-quarter weapon in the shape of a
double-edged dagger, made to fit on a
Mauser automatic revolver. With it one
gets a very untidy experience. It is also
used without the revolver in saps. Another
sample of Ivultur is a knuckle-duster with a
4in. double-edged dagger-blade sticking
in the middle thereof. Recently it was stick-
ing in my middle, worse luck.

“Sapper” : We were sitting in our hut on
the eve of setting out for France, when the
mail arrived. Bill got one letter, and pre-
sently I heard him remark, “Damned inter-
esting.” The letter bore the address of an
English Military Camp, and was from his
good Australian bush brother. It contained
two words: “Arrived. .Tim.” Bill’s reply
was equally expansive. It read: "Depart-
ing. Bill.”
“II.R.”: Love moves in a hys-
terious way its blunders to per-
form. He was very young when
he attempted suicide because he
suspected her of loving another.
They cut him down and extracted
the bullet and sewed him up, and
the conscience-stricken girl was
kind to him—too kind. However,
he refused to marry her because
he thought, darkly, that the child
might not be his. Whereupon the
infuriated young woman attempted
to shoot him. After such displays
of affectionate devotion on both
sides there was only one thing to
be done: they married. One day
a friend of the husband’s called in,
and waited for him to come home.
When he did come home he vio-
lently accused his wife and friend
of infidelity, and turned them
both into the street. Then he
went out, picked up the first
strumpet he could find and brought
her back. He told his wife, who
was waiting on the steps, that the
girl and he had been friends for
months, and after ordering the
wife away took Yenus inside. The
friend found a lodging for the
wife. The next scene, Sydney
Divorce Court. The Judge con-
sidered the husband’s petition base-
less, and that, anyway, owing to
the way in which he had treated
his wife, he' had no ground for re-
lease ; but he granted the wife’s
petition, and she went away weep-
ing as if she was not at all pleased.
Later, the ex-husband asaulted the
man with whom the ex-wife was
now staying, and walloped her. At
the police court she refused to give
evidence against him, and when he
was fined she paid the amount. Then they
went awav together, and the harassed infant
took a long sigh and wondered where it
would bring up next.

“P.X.” : If the expensive-shout competition
hasn’t closed I’ll nominate a recent breaking
of the drought at Auckland. Only one
drink was involved; hut under the law of
Jimmy Woods and Maoriland it cost the
man who said “Fill-’em-up” £7, and the
man who said “I don’t mind if I do” £5, and
the man who said “What’s yours?” £7, and
the man who employed him £25. However,
it might be worse. You can still be sociable
enough to ask Sandy (or even Sandy’s wife)
to share your umbrella when it rains and
not be fined for it.
llow it strikes a lawyer:—
The Bulletin (2S/12/’l6) poked fun at the Law
for judging a man not guilty of false pretence
where he had, in another name, sent a telegram to
himself that money would be in the bank at a cer-
tain date, file distinction about any statement
relating to the future not being false pretence is
good common-sense, not a legal quibble. It hat
was the man who told you the Kaiser would be out
of Berlin by Christmas, or that Faircow couldn’t lose
the Cup? A liar? No; just a false prophet! But
those were obviously mere forecasts, you say. All
right; come a bit nearer home. If you assert that
you dined with Jones last Sunday, when you didn’t,
you are a plain liar, and cannot complain if he
brands you as one. But if you promise to dine
with him next Sunday, and anything—even mere
laziness or unwillingness—keeps you away when the
time comes, you would feel greatly hurt if he
called you that. If every man could be gaoled
whose tailor’s bill had ever stayed unpaid after the
date fixed for settling, or whose promissory note had
ever got overdue, it would be a. bad look-out for
most of us. A promise is a promise, and should
be kept if possible; but it is not a statement of
fact. There may be half a dozen good reasons
for not keeping it, and the consequence of breaking
it is not to be gaol. In refusing to call a broken
one a False Pretence —which is the legal way of
saying a Lie —the Law does its usual best to sho-''
itself the “perfection of commonsense.”
So when a man says “I shall have money
coming to me to-morrow,” and on the
strength of that obtains goods from you, it is
not a false pretence even though he have not
the smallest reason for expecting money to-
morrow. The thing is not commonsense. It
THE WHOLE CASE FOR ALGERNON.
PROSPECTIVE PA-IN-LAW: “Marry my daugh-
ter, sir! Don't you understand that
she has always been accustomed to have
every luxury she wanted?"
A LFY: “Yes, sir! I’m one of the luxuries!”
is hair-splitting. The man does falsely pre-
tend, in such a case, that he has something
which he hasn’t got—to wit, knowledge that
he may reasonably expect money on the fol-
lowing day. He doesn’t take the other down
by his promise, but by his plain lie that he
has something which he doesn’t possess.
ROMANI.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Turkish prisoners of war in Egypt express
themselves delighted with their camp. They have
no complaints to make.- I—Cable. 1 — Cable.
We’ve had a scrap with Abdul—’twas friendly,
understand ;
He came out like a gentleman and met us on the
sand,
And we were glad to see him and took him by the
hand.
(’Twas not like when we met him on Anzac’s tragic
heights.
It was a day of Eastern days, a night of Eastern
nights.
The desert seemed reflecting the golden points of
lights.)
We didn’t feel embarrassed, we didn’t feel alarm ;
We joined the Cross and Crescent with a bright star
for a charm—
And then waltzed round the desert, like old pals,
arm in arm.
We had a game of cricket, and a game of football,
too,
And then went to the races just as gentlemen should
do,
And swore about it coming home, all just like me
and you.
(It was not like the Landing on Anzac’s tragic
height.
It was a day of Eastern days, with a golden-starry
night;
The sands, like lakes, reflected those golden points of
light.)
Ilis uniform was ragged (or, rather, it was rags) ;
His marching boots were tennis shoes, and some-
times soogee bags—
The same in which they put their kits, and carried
them like swags.
We took him in to Cairo, we took him to Port Said,
And gave him canned Australian pork, which, we
swore, was sheep’s head,
And beer that we called “sherbet” —or something
else instead.
We shouted shoes and slippers for his desert-blistered
feet;
We stole some gaudy awnings to wrap him from the
heat,
And hats like blanky flower-pots to rig him out
.complete.
We did the Block with Abdul, ’neath Egypt's starry
dome;
We did the Block with Abdul, and we took him for
a roam —•
He saw the things and smelt the things he’d seen
and smelt at home.
And when we found the “sherbet” was going to his
head
To an unbelieving publican his Faithful feet we led.
And we laid him in a hammock and his prayers for
him we said.
It’s sunrise—or it's sunset. Our heads are far from
clear
(It might as well be midday to one that’s lying
here).
But Abdul prays to Mecca, and we pray for a beer.
And Abdul shouts a gallon—all just as if he knew —-
And we drink deep to Abdul, as all good Anzacs do.
For (by the Beard!) old Abdul’s a good Australian
too.
So we go forth —“the Hatted”—to breakfast and a
swim ;
And Abdul squats reflecting, with hat without a
brim,
So’s he can see Mahomet when Mahomet comes for
him.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

“Seven Bells”: When the 14,000-tcin
oattleship Cornwallis was torpedoed in the
Mediterranean, an old acquaintance of the
aids who held Gallipoli went out for good.
The Cornwallis was-one of the vessels which
covered the landing, dropped ironmongery
on to the Turkish forts, and was a very pre-
sent help at the evacuation. Scarred like
an old-time prizefighter, it often had to go
into dock, and this gave the Auzacs in hos-
pital at Malta a chance to renew their friend-
ship with their old pal. The sailors of the
Majestic and the Triumph (both torpedoed
at the Dardanelles) were wont to link arms
with the Cornwallis’s lads and walk a mile
to the hospital in order to convey delicacies
to their khaki-clad cobbers. Then they would
return to their ships and steam east for
another go at Abdul. How brisk ago it used
to be is illustrated by the fact that one of
the Cornwallis’s guns fired more rounds in
one day than it was supposed to do in its
whole life. The ship was a great old
museum. It had on the quarterdeck the
foundation-stone, with inscription in Arabic,
of the No. 1 fort at Sedd-ul-Bahr, demolished
by the fleet. Another memento was a shell
from Abdul which had dived through a fan-
light into the engine-room, and then, failing
to recognise its chances, had failed to ex-
plode. One of the naval V.C.’s won-at the
Dardanelles was gained by a Cornwallis
middy, who swam ashore with a lifeline and
saved a number of men.
“Doctor Fish”: That the point to con-
sider in classifying men is the make-up of
their stomachs and not the shape of the
head or the size of the brain is the conclu-
sion arrived at by two doctors, Bryant and
Goldthwait, after 500 post-mortems and other
messy operations. They say there are two
types of men, distinguished by the length of
their intestines—the tiger-man, or the car-
nivorous type, and the ox-man, or the herb-
ivorous type. The intestines of. the first run
from .13 to 20 feet, while the bullocky kind
has them 25 feet long or over. The carniv-
orous fellows are active, adventurous and
excitable ; warriors and the livelier kind of
politician belong to that type. The other kind
are more steady-going. The doctors say that
the herbivorous type ought to be vegetarians
or pretty near it, while the carnivorous type
naturally flourish on a meat diet. But these
two fellows don’t explain how a man may
tell, without going to the trouble of a post-
mortem, how long his intestines are, so that
lie may feed correctly.

HER: “Yes, my doctor says I’ve to go to

HIM: “Tut! Tut! Fancy it getting up

There were two heroines in a drama staged
the other day in the Richmond (Vic.) Magis-
trates’ Court. A single girl, charged with
child abandonment, gave it in evidence that
a soldier on active service was the father
of her two children, and that she had been
sheltered by the soldier’s wife. . Having
thus put the soldier’s wife in the heroine
class, she joined it herself by declaring that
she would not take any money if it had to
come out of the wife’s allotment. The
stage-manager then rung down the curtain to
bring the matter under the notice of the
Defence Minister and the State War Coun-
cil, apparently with a view to helping the
girl, whose only helper hitherto had been
the party who in the Fifth Act would be
described as the Wronged Wife.
"Job’s Gully” ; A shabby, elderly building
in Bourke-street, Melbourne, alongside the
old cottage where Redmond Barry used to
live, will be put up for sale in a few days.
There are two legends on the walls, one of
which refers to a regular supply of towels
and toilet paper, and the other, a much
older one, says: “Registered Office of the
Howard Submarine and Torpedo Inventions
Co. Limited.” Howard, I think I remem-
ber, was a Tasmanian inventor who tried
to get the Vic. Government 30 years ago to
take up his undersea steameiv Presumably
he was sent to Throgmorton Gully. London,
where most likely he joined the flocculent
matter constantly banked up against the
walls of the Stock Exchange. But what-
ever happened to him he deserves recogni-
tion as a pioneer of the submarine industry.
“F.N.T.”: Algernon Darge, Melbourne’s
best-known out-of-doors camera man, re-
cently took a bike-and#side-ear trip with a
mate to Wood’s Point and Matlock. Once
the road leaves the Yarra Valley for the
ancient goldfields, it shoots straight up to
the top of a lofty range and stays there, with
breathless, beautiful views at every turning.
When Darge got back he was able to bear
testimony to the earnestness with which the
Victorian backblocker dodges his social re-
sponsibilities, for the trip involved dismem-
bering the outfit dozens of times in order
to get it over or around big timber which
had fallen across the road and been left
there. Practically this main road is a
closed one, though one of the most picturesque
in Victoria; yet within the past few years
the State has spent thousands of pounds on
opening up, mainly for tourist trarnc, new
roads which lead through no finer scenery.
To judge by the appearance of many Vic-
torian roads, their maintenance and repair
are nobody’s business; shire councils consist
of 1.W.W.-ers, and a sort of backblocks
anarchy prevails, shamefully encouraged by
an administration kowtowing to the rural
voter. It all means appalling waste, too;
for tens of thousands are spent in building
expensive tracks up to noble views when
even nobler views could be obtained merely
by shifting a dozen dead trees out of the way.

by wrapping the rub-

laugh ted at the recollec-

wonder who’ll get hold
of that • parcel you
left!” “What parcel?”
lie exclaimed. Then:
“Why, great Scot! That
must have been the
whisky!” It was a
terribly dry night, and
Jones cursed me quite
a lot. But how was I to know?

“The Old Soldier” to the rescue:—
That Australian (B. 11/1/’l7) whose idea to save
thousands of lives in this war was hung up by the
War Office, is not at all singular in his experience.
Ilis was the common lot of inventors who had rela-
tions with the W.O. But that department is not
altogether to blame, as I found when I was acting in
the early ’6o’s as assistant to the Superintendent of
Experiments at Shoeburyness. The British army
was at that time seeking a rifled field-gun. Arm-
strong had offered a complete system of such artillery,
but there were doubts. The detached vent-pieces
would blow out and the leaden jacketing on the
shells nearly always stripped off and spoiled the aim.
However, at last Armstrong got a bonus of £20,000
and some fine contracts for the supply of his guns.
One battery was sent to China, another (Mercer’s)
to Maoriland. and others to India. The one that
went to China was sent to the rear as soon as it
opened fire, because the flying lead-casings of its
shells were killing the skirmishing infantry in front.
Armstrong had another invention up his sleeve—a
muzzle-loading gun, but Woolwich Arsenal fired it
out quick, and invented a gun for itself. Can you
wonder at it?

One of the most tragic documents ever
issued by a scientific body has been pub-
lished by the committee which is inquiring
into the causes of death and invalidity in
the Commonwealth. It deals with the pre-
ventable disease of syphilis—the ghastly
thing which affects not only the sufferer but
his (or her) descendants. Having ascer-
A RESPITE.
W IFEY: “Poor mother has done nothing all
day but sit with her chin resting in her
hand.”
THE BRUTE: “H’m —I didn’t think she had
the sense to give her chin a rest.”
tained by investigations running into thou-
sands of cases that the disease is approxi-
mately as common in Australia as in France,
the committee prepared a table showing,
upon the basis of a schedule adopted in Paris,
what proportion of deaths in the Common-
wealth from certain diseases were attribu-
table to syphilis in 1914. Apart from the
deaths due directly to the scourge it is
declared that the . whole of the deaths, from
general paralysis and locomotor ataxia had a
syphilitic origin; that say one-third (.TOG
out of 2119) of the fatal cases of apoplexy
were traceable to this abomination; one-
third of the cases of softening of the brain,
“paralysis without specified cause,” and
organic heart disease; one-fifth of the cases
of Bright’s disease; and one-tenth of the
cases of meningitis and epilepsy. And
these are only a few of the diseases resulting
from a virus which the State still allows to
be spread through the community. In the
one year 1914 the Commonwealth lost 7189
citizens through the ravages of this disease;
it probably lost twice as many more through
sterility or because of the refusal of persons
suffering from the disease to marry; and it
lost the productive labor of thousands of
men and women unable to work on account
of their sufferings. The war has lasted
less than three years. This disease, which
probably kills as many people every year,
goes on continually.

- “Masetto”: Years ago in the Land of Hot
Air, when Dr. Cook put up a big bluff about
his explorations in the Frozen North, Peary
called him an auctioneer, and got the best
of the controversy that followed. Now some
of Peary’s own “discoveries” have been cut
out of the U.S.A. navy maps, and it is
doubted that he “discovered” the Pole. Peary
affirmed that he had proved Greenland to be
an island, but subsequent voyagers have
established that the channel which he named
after himself does not exist. lie said it was
there in 1901, but five years later Erichsen.
a Danish explorer, looked for it in vain, and
four years ago another party found an upland
instead. Lately an expedition sent out by
the American Museum of Natural History
has disproved other allegations of Peary and
demonstrated that one Yank explorer is very
much like another.
“D.M.”: The London College of Music
yearly proves that there are thousands of
young Australians trained and taught to
despise home methods of teaching and
encouraged to strive for outside examinations
and diplomas. The people who train these
pupils are mostly Australians with no other
than an Australian education in music and
all else, and the local representatives of the
London College of Music are Australians
born and bred here. Yet none of them seem
content to be’examined as to efficiency by
Australians of high musical attainments,
and prefer the hall-marks and diplomas of
persons on the other side of the* globe in-
habiting the most unmusical and non-music
loving country in the world. For whatever
else the British may be, their climate for-
bids them to be a musical people naturally.
Here in Australia our air, our environment
and climate breed musicians in myriads. Yet
we implore foreign bodies like the London
College of Music and Trinity College of
Music to confer power upon local music-
teachers to set their exanfination papers in
pianoforte, singing and elocution ! Every
Australian boy and girl ought to be told
that he or she carries a conductor’s baton
in the music knapsack, and every Australian
Uni. ought to be told that it must make pro-
vision for Australian professors of music
to examine Australian musicians in exactly
the same way as they examine “junior” and
“seuioi - ” students in history,* geometry and
the other things taught at. the schools.
“F.8.D.”: People find that, how-
ever successful they are in persuad-
ing themselves to do things an hour
earlier by the old woman’s device of
putting on the clock, they can’t so
easily pull the legs of cows and other
intelligent animals. In the Western
district of Victoria the cows refuse
to stand about the yards to be
milked in the hot afternoon sun
when they ought to be having a
snooze, so the Warrnambool con-
densed milk factory has gone back to
the old time. There is much the
same growl about the change at
other country places, and there’s
likely to be two times soon —a town
time and a country time an hour
behind it. Even the mild churches
■ have declared themselves rebels and
gone back to sun-time in numerous town-
ships.
The Victorian retailer of justice still
knows his job. The other morning the Mel-
bourne City Court Bench (which included
O’Callaghan, ex-police commissioner) fined
a man £2 for resisting arrest. During the
mix-up the prisoner had been “rescued” by
the crowd. Afterwards, when discovered
in another part of the city and re-arrested,
he had kicked the Law in the face. For-
tunately O’Callaghan knows the worth of
a constable’s face; and he fixed £2 as a fair
thing for kicking it. Three days previously,
at Geelong Vest, nine men were prosecuted
for playing the cruel and brutal game of
two-up. In that callous pastime the law-
breakers stand around in a gloating group,

HOW DRUGS DELUDE
DYSPEPTICS.
A MENACE TO HEALTH.
Dyspeptics who take drugs commit a crime
against their health, for drugs do not cure
dyspepsia, neither do they possess the power
to neutralise acid in the stomach, which is
the underlying cause of most forms of diges-
tive and stomach trouble. Drugs may appear
to give relief in some cases of indigestion
and dyspepsia, but that is because they
numb the nerves of the stomach and render
them insensible to pain. Herein lies tin*
chief danger; the symptoms of the trouble
are covered up and hidden, while the cause
of the trouble, that is, the acid in the
stomach —remains as active and as danger-
ous as ever, and may in course of time cause
gastric ulcers to form.
Physicians have demonstrated over and
over again that the stomach cannot regain
strength or the digestive organs recover their
power to function normally unless kept free
from irritant acid, and this can be achieved
with safety and certainty by taking half a
teaspoonful of bisura ted magnesia in a little
water immediately after every meal It can
be relied upon to neutralise acid and prevent
fermentation of the food.
This method is now being employed by
hospitals throughout the country with
marked success, and we are sure the recipe
will prove valuable to many of our readers.
No difficulty should be experienced in obtain-
ing the bisurated magnesia, for it is stocked
by high-class chemists everywhere, but make
sure that you get the bisurated form of mag-
nesia, for the oxides, sulphates and citrates
are quite unsuitable.— Advt.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
During the past week various changes
have been made in the Defence Department,
and returned soldiers who have proved them-
selves in the field have been appointed State
(or, as it is officially called. District) Com-
mandants. This involves the retirement of
militia-officers Ramaciotti and Williams,
who have been for 12 months or so Com-
mandants respectively in N. S. Wales and
Victoria. There is plenty more to be done
in the same direction. For example, when
last heard of, the position of Chief of Staff
was held by Colonel Foster, an elderly Eng-
lish engineer, for some years past a Univer-
sity professor, and one of the world’s autho-
rities, on the siege of Jerusalem. Ramaciotti
and Williams at least know Australia and
the Australians and for very many years
have been connected with the only army
Australia has had. The world’s authority
on the siege of Jerusalem doesn’t and hasn’t.
Is there still no returned Australian soldier,
with experience of the most modern war, who
could possibly be Chief of Staff? If there
is the sooner he is put there, so that he may
get the training for still higher commands,
the better.

Killed in France, apparently on the last
day of the old year, Reg Lindsay, younger
brother of Norman and Lionel and Ruby
and Dan. He was the only one of the re-
markable tribe who did not more or less
follow art; and even he could draw, as he
showed in some recent letters from the
Front. But the studio was not for him : he
was a big, sun-tanned, frank Australian
who could only breathe out of doors, and as
a forester in the Victorian Government ser-
vice he seemed to be placed just right. He
was 28, the second-youngest member of the
family. The youngest, Dan, is also in
France.
The first mummer to gain the V.C. in the
present war was Lieut. W. Hartnell, born
in Carlton (Vic.) 30 years ago; and his
widow and daughter are living in the pretty
little rural suburb of Murrumbeeua. He
and Frederick C. Selous, hunter and ex-
plorer, who was killed the other day at
the age of Do, while leading his men in an
attack against the German Ea§t African
stronghold of Bhohebo, were officers of the
same regiment—the 25th Battalion of
Frontiersmen. It was one of the units of
the Royal Fusiliers (City of London Regi-
ment), which carries on its colors honors
going back to 1695. Hartnell was the first
to enter the fort at Port Bukoba, on \ ic-
toria Nyanza, hauling down the Hun flag
with his own hands. On the day of his

carried to the rear, wounded, when he

Patriarchal Archbishop Dunne, who has
passed out at Brisbane at considerably over
80, was the oldest prelate in Australia. Dr.
Duhig, his successor in the see, is the
youngest archbishop. He is 45, and when
translated from Rockhampton to Brisbane in
1905 was the Benjamin of Roman Catholic
archbishops in the English-speaking world.
Ages of other R.C. archbishops: Kelly (Syd-
ney), born 1850; Carr (Melb.), 1840;
Coadjutor Mannix (Melb.). 1804; Delaney
(Hobart), 1853; Spence (Adelaide), 1800;
Clune (Perth), 1804.
The blood of Grace Darling, who, with
her father’s aid, rescued the survivors of
the Forfarshire wreck in 1838, runs in the
veins of five Carlton lads now doing their
bit. Their dad was born in gaol: for his
father was deputy-governor of the big Lon-
don prison of Pentonville. He was a cousin
of Grace’s. Going to sea as a youngster,
dad was alongside at the time of the Abys-
sinian war, when King Theodore’s wife and
son were brought down from Magdala after
Theodore had suicided on the capture of
his stronghold. Later. Darling married and
settled in Victoria, whence five sons left to
put up this present record : Sergeant S. C.
Darling, 22. wounded at Pozieres; Private
F. Darling, 27, wounded in Gallipoli, now
fighting in France; Private O. G. Darling,
29, ditto; Private E. E. Darling, 34; and
Vet. Sergeant R. S. Darling, both at the
French Front.

Gone to his final address, Richard Henry
Hipsley, who was born 81 years ago and
joined the telegraph service in N. S. Wales
59 years back. He was the oldest living-
postal official of Australia. Two years after
joining Ma’s service he became postmaster
at Bathurst, and was here and there until he
was made assistant-manager of telegraphs in
1887. He retired in 1896. When he started
operations the sending of a 10-word tele-
gram took 3s. It now takes several days.

sian traveller that the Falkiners shore about
250,000 sheep one season. The incredulous
Russian, whose middle names were Thomas
Adidvmus, promptly related that in Siberia
a pal of his had that many sheep-dogs.
Unable to make his own name famous,
because its use had to be discontinued foi
tactical reasons, a N. S. W ales soldier is
now entitled to Lieutenant in front of his
nom de guerre and D.C.M. after it. This is
(to give him the description applied in a
late casualty list) “R. McGregor, alias
J, Iv. Williams.” He has been severely
wounded for the second time. John Iv.
Williams twice tried to enlist in his own
name and was twice rejected. Then he
took the monniker of Roy McGregor, was
accepted as a private, and as a corporal of
the 3rd Battalion gained the D.C.M. at
Gaba Tepe, where, the ammunition-carriers
having been killed, he brought supplies from
the support trench under heavy fire. On
the strength of that he got a commission.
“Billy Blue”: Re George Uther Hosking,
the water-finder of Manly ( 8.28/12/T6). The
pioneer of the family came out as school-
master in the first year of last century. He
had two sons; the elder. John, was the first
Mayor of Sydney 11842 L The younger,
Peter, father of the water-finder, was the
first medical student to go from Australia to
study and obtain his diploma as a doctor of
medicine. lie left Sydney for that purpose
in 1820, returned in February, 1831, and
started practice in the city.
One of Duntroon’s star pupils, Major Alec
Steele, is in the last list of D.S.O.’s. Now
20 years of age, he left the Military Col-
lege, where he was assistant-instructor in
machine gunnery, as soon as war gave him
the opportunity to put his precepts into
practice. He was attached to the oth (Q.)
Battalion as sergeant-major of the machine-
gun section, and when the whole of that sec-
tion, except himself, was killed or wounded
by Turkish fire at Gaba Tepe, he manned
his gun in such fashion that he got the
D.C.M., a wound and his commission. Ilis
‘ promotion was rapid, and when he was
handed the D.S.Q. lately for gallantry on
the Somme, he had been for some months
a major. He comes from South Aus., but
three States may compete for him. since he
has been in khaki in every one of them.
One of the latest things in Melbourne
statuary is at the terminus of the tramline
leading to the Zoo. The lower part of the
monument is a drinking fountain, inscribed
with the information that Councillor George
llawkins levers erected it as a thanks-offer-
ing to the ward for having again elected
him to the City Council. But the permanent
advt. of Cr. George Hawkins levers doesn’t
end there. Above it is a bust of the man
himself in imperishable marble. Hundreds
of people every week are thus hit in the eye
by the effigy of a conventional city councillor
perched atop of a drinking-fountain. The
thing hurts.
SIR HARRY BARRON, Governor of W.A.
The recent birth of four children at a time
to Mrs. Keogh, of Kensington (Vic.), has
reminded an old-timer of how much better
they did these things in the good old days.
He takes his affidavit that in 1857 three
mothers in Melbourne had a dozen children
between them —one three, one four and the
other five. They petitioned the Assembly to
encourage them by a grant, but John
O’Shanassy was dead against the idea as a
matter of principle. “If the State patronises
this sort of thing.” he observed, “they will
breed like rabbits.” Rut he offered to put
up a £5-note to show his own personal sym-
pathy.

“Joe” Murphy, who for 35 years had filed
affidavits and issued writs in the public office
of the Ma State’s Supreme Court.- Murphy
was one of the first lot of “old boys” pro-
duced by St. Joseph’s College, and was senior
assistant in the dusty old office looking out
over King-street. "What he didn’t know
about Supreme Court procedure no one has
yet discovered.

Adelaide has acquired two good men from
other States —Charles Fenner (of the Bal-
larat School of Mines) as Superintendent
of Technical Education, and Herbert Heaton
(Yorkshireman from Hobart) as director of
the Workers’ Educational movement at the
University. Both men have new billets,
financed by Government. Fenner has been
going round Australia since he left Bal-
larat, picking up hints everywhere. It is
not his fault that he finds S.A.’s biggest
technical school outside the grip of his de-
partment. Langdon Bonython is chairman
of its Board, and it was no doubt his influ-
ence that got it exempted from the scope of
the new Act. This folly badly needs amend-
ing. Meanwhile, Fenner has to stage
“Hamlet” with the part of the Prince of
Denmark left out.

Canon West, the very muscular Christian
who used to be a rector in Adelaide, is these
days chaplain of the garrison at West
Hartlepool (Eng.). When he went back to
England he p*id the passage-money for a
great barracker for Australia, and about his
greatest joy since he reached London has
been to Avatch the gradual acceptance of
Australian experience and the results of
Australian experiments—in social legislation,
views of life and government of the church.
“Bad Australian’s” send-off: —
Dead on the field of honor, Lieut. Cecil Stuart
Bussell, white Australian. Five years ago, when I
went aboard the Rotorua at AVellington (M.L.) on
my return to S. America, I found my cabin mate a
long, wiry, sunburned young Bananalander. He was
going out “on spec.” He had hardly recovered his
land legs when he started survey work for a rail-
road in the foothills of the Andes, where he picked
up the language and made good with astonishing
rapidity. When Mad Bill cut loose Bussell was
away out in the back country. Forgetting all about
his comfortable job and his rosy prospects, he
saddled his mule and started on the long journey to
the railroad. Unfortunately he met with an acci-
dent on the way, and was rejected by the doctor
appointed to examine local volunteers. He didn’t
waste time worrying about his luck, but made the
remaining 1100 kilometers to Buenos Aires by train,
and when he had recovered from an operation paid
his own passage to England, obtained a commission
in the Artillery, and was sent to France, where he
remained until the end. He was wounded twice, and
had been recommended for promotion only a few
days before he answered the Last Call. Young
Bussell was a grandson of Stuart Russell, one of
Queensland’s best-known pioneers. He was a true
Australian. Just that —and all the words in our
language cannot express greater praise.
THE MANNERS OF MARY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The manners of Mary are strange and con-trary,
Her smile it is roguish, her eye it is sly ;
She moves through the world like an exquisite fairy ;
Iler step it is lightsome, her poise it is airy ;
Her manners are, still, an offence to my eye.
Perchance no one misses a surplus of kisses
Expended at random, in manner most free,
On folk undeserving of such tender blisses;
But judge the offence 1 imagine that this is,
Who think they should all be expended on me!
It would not so fret me if she would but let me
Escape from the thraldom in which I now lie ;
But still her lips, pouting, eternally get me,
While, outraged, my Ego, with taunts will upset me:
Her manners are thus an offence to my I.
N.S.W.

A big factor in the policy of this bank is
that no dividend can be paid until a certain
debt to old depositors is wiped out. These
old depositors were given inscribed stock to
represent ,15s. in the £ of their receipts;
the other os, was to be paid out of future
profits, and until it was paid there were to
be no dividends. For a good many years
after this arrangement was made in 1890
t!ie bank was too busy clearing out other
wreckage and scraping up interest on the old
Government and private deposits to pay off
any ot this dead-horse 55.; but of recent
years very good progress has been made, and
tilings have now gone this far:—

The accounts don’t show the ss. in the £
as a liability, and the ninepences when they
a re thrown into the old bog entirely dis-
appear from sight. The foregoing figures
are therefore merely as near as this paper
can get to the facts.
Why 9d. in the £ should have absorbed
£43.269 last half-year while it got away
with only £38,462 on the previous payment
isn’t explainable. Maybe the £43,269 in-
cludes something paid in taxes. Apparently
in order to put the position more accurately
t.he form of the notice with regard to the
next payment on account of the old dead-
horse lias been altered. Formerly “a repay-
ment of [say] 9d. in the £ on the amount
written off the original deposit receipts”
was notified. Now it is given out that there
will be “a bonus of 4d. in the £ on the face
value of the inscribed stock.” Probably
some snag has been sighted in the stream.
It isn’t good to have to admit it, but it is a
fact nevertheless that when one sees (as one
does see half a dozen times a week) a change
in the form of a co.’s accounts, it is generally
safe to suspect that the tax-gatherer is re-
sponsible. And it is mostly the tax-
gatherer’s own fault, because he has been
far too prone to take points.
8o 14s. 6d. in the £ of the old debt to be
paid out of profits has been paid, and there
is another Is. 3d. in hand = 15s. 9cl. alto-
gether. That means three years to finish the
job at the rate of ninepence per half-year.
It may be about that. General-manager
Ralston might possibly shake out more
hidden profits if lie wanted to and thus
greatly expedite the process. But he will
be more likely to decide that there isn’t
much to be gained b-y rushing tilings, and he
will prefer to keep all he lias up his sleeve
in order to make things surer for himself and
the bank in the dog days that are coming.
When dividends do arrive they are pretty
sure to be luscious. The present capital is
only £480.000; and as plainly the bank can
P‘iy 10 n.e. on much more than that, the
share capital will most likely be increased.
The Q.N. is well placed to make money for
shareholders while money is dear. It has
no less than £3,091.639 of interminable in-
scribed stock—a perpetual deposit at 31 p.c.;
and that is remarkably cheap money these
times. All other liabilities amount to
£(.205,153, and no less than £4,471,298 of
the assets are liquid. The modern depositor
is therefore splendidly entrenched.

The profit includes additions made to the
insurance reserve. The published accounts
put it this way
Net Profits. Added to Insurance Fund.
1915. . £186,383 £46,467
1916.. 150,174 158j696
llow much of that Insurance Fund is true
profit this paper cannot say. The co. may
possibly be carrying some of its own insur-
ance risks, and in that case the fund is an
accumulation of premiums to cover an unex-
pired contingency. But this also must be
said : the co. has hitherto found it possible,
on reviewing the position, to transfer large
amounts put by against contingencies. In
other words, the co. has generally found its
contingency reserves a stocking into which
it might dip when it thought the shareholders
were extra-hungry. And the insurance fund
is not the only contingency reserve. Three
or four years ago assets were written up—
that is to say, internal reserves were dis-
closed. When the capital was then increased
from a million to two millions, the visible
reserves were reduced by only about £300,000.
Which showed that, at that time, the co.
had about £700,000 of undisclosed profits in
other stockings. There may be a good deal
falling into them now. Twelve months ago
there was a new line among the liabilities—
“ Reserve for liabilities not yet adjusted, in-
cluding provision for allowances on adjust-
ment of contracts, Imperial, Commonwealth
and Dominion taxes, and extra depreciation
on steamers in Government service,
£209,227.” This year that reserve has been
increased to £359.227. I low much of that
money is going to be called for this paper
doesn't know either. It is a contingency
fund, too; and it looks quite certain that it
is a provision for things which the co. can’t
be dead-certain will turn out to be liabilities
at all. On top of that there is the usual
unspecified provision for depreciation; and
the allowance has always been on the gener-
ous side hitherto. If by and bye the co.
finds that it can shake another uncousidered
trifle of a quarter of a million or so out of
the well-worn old stocking The Bulletin
will not be surprised. This war is a terrible
thing—in parts.

Down to eight or nine years ago the shares
in this co. were in very few hands, and
reserves were not accumulated. At least, not
visible reserves. Practically all outgoings,
whether on building additions or otherwise,
were paid out of revenue; and that in part
accounted for the comparatively small pro-
fits of some of the earlier years in the table.
Then the co. got a larger list of shareholders,
and new life was put into the management.
Local conditions were also favorable; and
the results have been strikingly good, and
no doubt could have been made to look bet-
ter still. This gives another view of them :
1911. 1914. 1910.
Liabilities ..£108,709 £204,740 £194,445
Freeholds .. 220,529 431,778 429,615
Leaseholds . . 15.780 14,356 14,369
Stock .. .. 169,275 174,.659 221.782
Book debts and
liquid assets .. 97.397 93,519 62,341
Since 1911 £43,770 of new capital has been
got in, bringing the total to £400,000, equally
divided into 0 p.c. pref. and ordinary. In
live years the liabilities and capital (includ-
ing premiums) have thus been increased by
£147,014. Against that the co. now has, on
book value, £203,086 more in premises; and
though book debts and liquid assets have
been reduced by £35,056, the stock is valued
at £52,057 more. On that reckoning there is
in these items an improvement, in round
figures, of £73,000 in five years—a fine per-
formance in view of the solid dividends paid
on ordinary shares. Reckoning the goodwill
(which has now been struck out of the
balance-sheet) as a reserve to support the
pref. capital, then the £200,000 of ordinary
capital is represented by visible assets valued
at £308,662, after paying the final dividend
for last year. And the actual total is pro-
bably a good deal more than that, because,
as said just now, in former years at any
rate large sums spent on the improvement of
the property—probably the finest block in
Sydney—were not added to capital account.
The pref. capital carrying only 6 p / c. has, of
course, been a great help to ordinaries. It
is a very different proposition when a co.
puts half or more of its ordinary capital into
freeholds, and expects to pay 10 p.c. or more
on the lot. There is, however, no reason
why pref. capital shouldn't be got on such
security at 6 p.c.; for the freehold is worth
at least the total of that capital plus all
liabilities. Capital which requires security
of that kind has no right to expect more
than 6 p.c., and there is a lot to be said in
favor of dividing the shares into two classes.

It was the “bed and breakfast, other meals
optional” idea which stood to this co. when
it needed a buttress. Six o'clock closing
no doubt affects it a little, but not to any
serious extent, because it never had and
never wanted a bar trade. But above every-
thing the remarkable success of the co. has
been due to highly developed bookkeeping.
Any old sort of person, it is generally sup-
posed, can run a pub. And any old sort of
person can run one sort of pub. More
money has been made with less intelligence
out of beer than out of any other thing
Australia knows. But to sell beer and
make money out of it is one thing and to
run a large restaurant and lodging-house
and make money out of that is quite another
thing. The Metropole had the remarkable
good fortune to be taken in hand by people
who know something about costs and the
itemising of accounts, which is the beginning
of all wisdom in the management of a busi-
ness where waste is liable to occur. That
intelligent management and the fact that the
big pub-boardinghouse stands in a position
from which it can hit the eye of every
arrival by sea are between them responsible
for fine figures in the face of adverse circum-
stances.
The financial position is very easy. This
is how it has moved in four years:—
1912. 1915. 1916.
Assets other than property
and plant .. £21,911 £36,271 £40,559
Less liabilities .. . . 2.063 3,759 4,476
Net liquid assets . . £19,848 £32,5‘12 £36,083
Of course there may be some depreciation of
property and plant not counterbalanced by
outlay during the past terms. But it doesn’t
look like it. All repairs, renewals, improve-
ments and additions have been paid for. and
during the past four years, besides providing
the dividends, the co. has put by £16,235 in
hard cash or its equivalent, and in the past
year £3571 has been similarly socked. The
capital is now £120,000. all in ordinary
shares.

The co. commenced planting in 1910 and
finished during the past year. This shows
how the work progressed:—
1910 . . . . . . 36 acres.
1911 175 ~
1912 46 „
1913 69 „
1914 251 ~
1915 21 „
1916 172 „
Total . . 770 acres.
So far only 1910-11-12 trees have been
tapped; but the 1913 planting will come
under the knife during this year. Apropos,
the same old trouble with contract labor is
found in Malaya:—
The damage done to the trees by Chinese tappers
working on contract has led to the substitution of
Javanese coolies employed on day labor. The change
lias lessened the quantity of rubber harvested, but
the cost of collection is lower, and the injury to
the trees has been minimised.
Considering that it tackled a business which
Australia knew nothing about, though there
is a tremendous lot to learn in it, this co.
has had remarkably good luck. It appears
to have got hold of a first-rate patch of rub-
ber country ; and that was a comparatively
rare occurrence. The trouble ahead of it is
the trouble that all employers meet: to get
the labor they want at the price they think
they ought to pay for it. Even now, with
much less than half the estate bearing, and
with many of the trees in the older part
not yet fit for tapping, there is some diffi-
culty. “Coolies are not so plentiful as for-
merly. In particular tappers are scarce and
our output has suffered in consequence.”
And you can’t let a rubber plantation look
after itself, for, apart from weeds, it soon
gets dirty, and the white ant is übiquitous.
However, though these are clouds, the
outlook is bright enough. Last year 31,2381 b.
were harvested : this year 61,4501 b. are ex-
pected, and it is surprising how close these
Malayan estimates have proved, as a rule.
Certainly the market isn’t what it was by a
long way. When the co. first took off its
coat rubber was worth about 4s. 10d., and
a fall below 3s. was not regarded as possible.
Last year the co. averaged a shade under
2s. Old. at Singapore. Including a propor-
tion of Melbourne expenses (apparently the
other part was charged , to development
account) and depreciation of plant, costs
were Is. sd. per 11). If the co. can average
a net profit of 7d. per lb., which seems a fair
figure to assume, the total for the current
year should be about £l7BO, which is equal
to just about 8 p.c. on the very moderate
capital of £22.431. With ordinary luck the
output should be nearly doubled in the fol-
lowing year. The financial position is easy:
on September 30 the co.’s liquid assets
(cash and rubber) were about £lBOO in
excess of liabilities.

The directors have lopped off a couple of

mainly from rents and subscriptions, and
might shrink a lot before the 6 p.c. div. was
in jeopardy; while the property provides as
good security for the principal as any free-
hold can.
