Shareholders in the Royal Bank of
Queensland met in Brisbane last week and
followed those of the Bank of North Queens-
land in unanimously adopting- the resolutions
authorising amalgamation. The shareholders
desired also that their thanks should be con-
veyed to general-manager Edward Denny
Day “for valuable services rendered during
the past 25 years.” Mr. Day. whose health
is now bad, retires on a pension equivalent
to his salary.
The salesman who knows his job makes the
best of his goods; but the Commonwealth in
issuing its War Loan doesn’t. The loan is
put forward as a4 5 p.c. investment, whereas
it is something far better than that. In the
first place, it is free of income tax; and,
though that is at present worth nothing or
little to the small investor, to the man with
a large income from property it may be
worth anything up to 1 p.c., and perhaps
more. But even to the small investor the
interest is better than 44 p.c. Payments to
the loan may be made in 10 instalments, and
on December 15 of this year £3 15s. p.c. will
be paid to investors. This means that on
each £lOO the investor, great or small, will
get a bonus of £3 10s. 6d. Including that
bonus the average interest actually earned
over the whole period of the loan will be
£4 13s. 7d. p.c. per annum. Taking into
account the concessions and the security, no
investment offered in Australia has been so
attractive; and if patriotism doesn’t com-
mand a very large response, mere business
considerations should.
Dunedin knows as much about gold-dredg-
ing as Sydney does (in fact, Dunedin
started it), and it has always puzzled many
people on this side why Maorilanders over-
looked or neglected the Iliinu group, re-
cently acquired in Sydney and Melbourne.
Here is the defence of a Maorilander:—
I went over the ground about three years ago
with a dredging engineer with the idea of forming
a company to thoroughly reprospect it, and, if
satisfactory, work it on a comprehensive scale. We
had before us our earlier experiences of dredging in
the same locality and a useful -comparison with
ground we were working in Otago and other parts;
and after a superficial inspection and a considerable
amount of information gleaned from various sources,
we decided to turn it down. We were guided in
this decision by the heavy nature of the ground and
its depth over a considerable portion of the whole
area. Over a mile down the flat there is a shaft
sunk a few years ago. This was about 65 feet deep
and to our surprise was dry, and that in face of
the fact that it had rained solidly for 24 hours,
t here .was plenty of water on the surface which was
running into the shaft, and the only conclusion we
could arrive at was that the water found a channel
through the large stones and so percolated down the
Hat to the sea.
Some years ago, and just prior to our visit, an
American expert, acting for a syndicate, had the
lower portion of the flat bored, using our Govern-
ment boring plant. We were shown the prospects
which, in ordinary dredging ground, would be con-
sidered highly satisfactory; but the Americans turned
the proposition clown, too, probably for the reasons
we have already suggested. The old Sandy Point
dredge now being erected on the lower end of the
flat has somewhere about a seven-cubic-feet bucket;
and if you could handle 10 full buckets per minute
for a 120-hour week, the result would be well over
The Bulletin's estimate (B. 14/12/T6) of an acre
of dredging per month. This result may be obtained
in ground of which Mr. Powell has had experience,
such as Adelong Flat, for instance; but he is up
against a different proposition in Rimu Flat. He
will find the contents of many buckets per minute
to be mostly stones and water, with a considerable
waste of time through breakages and wear and tear.
However, the portion of the Flat where the dredge
is being erected is much shallower than two or three
miles above it, and it is possible will give more than
payable results ; but anything approaching the figures
quoted is quite ridiculous. It will require £2OOO
to pay a shilling dividend on the issued capital,
and I am afraid it will take a long time to provide
funds to build another dredge while paying share-
holders interest on their invested capital. Let me
add that I have no doubt Mr. Powell and Sydney
vendors are perfectly honest in their utterances
regarding the value of this ground. So were Mr.
Kershaw and party who put a Kershaw pump and
plant on somewhat similar ground in that neighbor-
hood and promptly lost their capital.
I have no axe to grind; but I have been closely
associated with mining and dredging for over a quar-
ter of a century, and know the great difference be-
tween working heavy stones and free running gravel.
And there is a larger question involved: we could do
with lots of Australian capital to assist in developing
our mining industry, but we know that a few dis-
appointments may prove sufficiently disastrous to
prevent our getting anything for even the most
deserving ventures.
From T. H. Martyn:—
Mild Cat (B. 18/1/’l7), commenting on my
description of properties secured for Malaya Tin
Corporation in Siberia, omits to mention what I
emphasised at the shareholders’ meeting, i.e., that
the reefs were opened only on the surface by costeen-
ing. Shafts are now being sunk, and will tell their
own tale. As regards the gold and platinum pro-
perty, I recommended that it be tested by steam
drill, and gave as a reason for its availability that
there were difficulties to be contended with in its
clay contents. As regards the Russians, they may
be sleeping', but the British are not, and a lot will
be heard of Sibera east of the Urals after the war.
I note your remarks are apparently based on a
report printed elsewhere, which I regret I did not
notice, and hare not in consequence contradicted.
Clearly it needed correction.
Mount Hope, the co. which some years
ago took over the big copper deposit west
of Condobolin (N.S.W.), and then com-
menced to pray for a railway, is ready to
make the mine talk, if the State Govern-
ment will only give it a chance. The origi-
nal survey of the Condobolin-Broken Hill
line ran right through Mount Hope. A
fatuous Minister made a deviation, which
took the line 11 miles away; but a
spur line was promised if the mine war-
ranted it. The deviation has proved to be
an expensive bungle, the Government is
hard put to it to find the money for the
spur line, and presently we shall see the
main unfinished line running to a dead-end
at nowhere. Meanwhile Mount Hope, with-
out its spur, is crying for an opportunity
to furnish the line with traffic. Chief Gov-
ernment Geologist Carne examined the mine
recently, at the instigation of the Govern-
ment, and recommended that the spur be
built speedily. The copper production of
N. S. Wales has fallen away alarmingly;
and the Department sees here a means of
checking the fall. Full of faith, and ex-
pecting that the Government will honor its
promise, the co. is continuing development
work. Audley Smith says the mine con-
tines to open up remarkably well. Mean-
while the enlarged leaching plant and con-
centration plant are ready waiting for the
main line to be completed as far as Mata-
kana siding about the end of this month.
A second issue of debentures just made by
the co. was 50 per cent, over-subscribed.

Broken Hill last year exported minerals
valued on the spot at £3,873,632. In
December there were 6392 men and boys
employed, so taking that as the average for
the year the output was £6OO per head. The
field produced 212,397 tons of lead concen-
trates (£2,811,721), averaging over £l3 per
ton, and 206,196 tons of seine concentrates,
valued untreated at £938.008, or less than
£5 per ton, though the value of the contents
was probably five times that much.
Gt. Britain (jSI.S.W.) made a profit of
£2228 last half-year, and as there are only
10.000 shares (of lOs. each) it was able to
hand out four dividends of Is. each
(£2000). The mine produced 83 tons
IScwt. of tin, which realised £96o3—nearly
twice the amount of the capital. And that
is after steadily working for nine years and
paying dividends most of the time —no less
than 15s. a share in 1911.
After dawdling about for two years doing
next to nothing and missing the best metal
markets that ever were, the British Broken
Hill has an idea of making a move.
The Australian management wants shaking
up. It has been in want of it for years.
As the Adelong Greek No. 1 Dredge stuck
so long in 2ld. ground when costs were
over 3d., the profit earned by the No. 2
plant was whittled down to £l5OO for the
last half-year. Such a response isn’t likely
to set the Yarra afire, hut a run of the long-
talked-of sd. returns might help to build up
the flickering flame of old-time enthusiasm
in the Adelong field. At present repairs and
maintenance expenditure just about equal
the cost of dredging. Both the Creek and
the Estates must have learnt by this time
that, if poor ground is to be dodged, close
boring must be the order of the day. What
is the use of butting into 24d. ground if
3d. dirt alone pays? So Gordon Lyon is
setting to work to cut out some of the
unprofitable patches.

New Burragorang (N.S.W.) made a work-
ing profit of £2408 out of its streak of ore
last year; but administration exes, ate £325
of that, and income tax £219. A dividend of
10 p.c. ('£lsoo') was paid, and on November
30 the co. had £3450 of liquid' assets and
owed £1476. mostly to the vendors. No such
thing as making an estimate of ore reserves
is possible in a mine of this kind. But there
is no reason to expect an early failure of
supplies. Considering the size of the vein
its persistence is remarkable: this co. is now
sinking below ,the 900 ft. level.

At Westonia someone is badly needed to
look after the interests of the smaller cos.-
On one show, at which a Bulletin man
recently made a call, unamalgamated gold
was discernible throughout the launder, and
more than one person known to be of
doubtful character was hanging about the
premises. On another the boss was drunk,
and had, according to the man on the next
lease, been like that for a week. The
manager of a third show was also “para-
lytic,” and snent the day talking fight and
the night firing off a revolver. On a fourth
most of the boss’s time seemed to be taken
up in finding lucrative jobs for relatives;
and on a fifth detectives were watching for
the arrival of some stolen gold which it
was alleged was being brought along to
sweeten up a crushing.
Edna May Junction has taken an option
over Slavin’s lease at Bodallin Soak. The
reef is very wide, and, judging by the last
battery return, values have suddenly in-
creased. Generally the reef has a ' pipey
appearance and isn’t likely to continue satis-
factory. Nevertheless gaudv development,re-
ports may be looked for during the cur-
rency of the option.
Old-timers are poking borak in Perth at
the long, attractive list of returns from
abandoned mines which the Mines Depart-
ment thinks might be given another trial.
In the old days every gold thief had a
mine to which he credited the proceeds of
his thefts. Not much reliance, therefore,
can be placed on figures furnished in regard
to yields from dead mines.

W. J. liickard is .finding out that it
takes a heap more cash to mine in West-
ralia than in Bendigo. When lie was work-
ing at 1000 ft. at the New Chum Goldfields
at Bendigo, his whole exes, were covered by
what it costs per ton for surface work at
the Empress, Mt. Magnet (W.A.). Of a
total wages bill of 255. 3£d. he pays 12s. Id.
for surface jobs. So, like a wise man, he
is doubling his crushing power. When
Hartrick left the Empress it was a sick
show; but Rickard after 12 months’ work
can write that “there is a far better pro-
spect now than there was six months ago.”
This isn’t very high praise, though, for his
total reserves above 338 ft. are only 2000 tons,
averaging Bdwt. per ton. Putting costs at
255. the margin is 75., which will give enough
to sink the shaft another lift. What Rickard
looks for are fresh lenses of ore north and
south. The inducement north is that off
the old Lady Brassey shaft pig-rooting to
75ft. gave a lot of gold. As lie is down
338 ft. and well on the way towards the
shaft, this bit of prospecting may prove to
be the salvation of the Empress. But the
work is a pure hazard.

Black Range Pinnacles (W.A.) cannot
have the quantity of ore above No. 1 level
that was thought, otherwise Plans Irvine and
S. J. Staughton wouldn’t counsel the stop-
page of crushing to get on with shaft-sinking.
They say that the battery could put through
double the 1250 tons now extracted monthly
at no extra cost. If so, why blame the
shortage of skilled mining labor? Isn’t the
real source of all the trouble confessed in
the words, “your visiting directors in con-
sultation with the mine superintendent pro-
pose to remedy the serious depletion of ore
reserves above water level by suggesting,”
etc. To sink is wise, for with this serious
depletion, and with heavy sulphides intrud-
ing, there may be a complex-ore problem
to tackle as well as water and other troubles.
With £6OOO to jingle the policy is sound,
even if the reasons for stopping ore-extrac-
tion seem to be a bit thin.
Edna Deeps on November 30 owed only
£2559. of which £2300 was for plant and
leasehold. Until the £2BlO or so owing
on the debenture issue came in it had only
£577 to play with. That isn’t much to a
mine like the Deeps, but it may carry the
present lift down. Surveyor Macartney's
plan is out. but the Board doesn’t testify
whether he backs up manager Stuckey’s sur-
mise that ore. reserves are 8000 to 12.000
tons from 500 ft. up. Still, Stuckey talks
cautiously on the point; and no wonder, for
with No. 1 winze inclined towards the shaft.
No. 2 only a shallow working and the grade
of any “possible” stone north-east and
south-west of the faces unknown, it would
be folly to make definite estimates. The
ore proved at 480 ft. is 117 ft. long and 20ft.
wide. Give 20ft. of reef overhead to the
Edna boundary and 20ft. to the 500 ft. mark
for the full length of 117 ft.. then 7000 tons
are proved and “probable.” Add another
40ft. to the length of the shoot and the
total swings to. 9000 tons, though the “pro-
bable” factor is increased thereby. Costs
at 255. per ton with the stone graded at
60s. will leave £13,500 profit above 500 ft. to.
set against the debenture debt of £lO.OOO.
Of course, to use manager Stuckey’s phrase,
all this is a “rough guess,” but so is his
assertion that “below the 500 ft. level the
prospects of the mine are good, and a large
quantity of ore will be won of a grade
probably equal to ore above that level.”

Read at a rate far lower than is possible

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, January 22, 1917.
My Dear Akim, —
It was announced that members of the
Actors’ Association would farewell their
president, our only Julius Knight, at a
midnight supper, which sounded interesting.
But, after all, it was over the afternoon
teacups and the buttered bun that they
kissed him quick on the brow and parted. In
spite of a striped blue and-white collar,
Julius retained the gallant Monsieur Beau-
caire air that has been one of his best assets,
and kept a stiff upper lip during the agonis-
ing moments of farewell.
The big, hearty Greig laugh came with
Robert and his plump Beatrice. Miss Eily
Malyon looked cool (which, considering the
altitude of the mercury, she could not have
been) and sweet (which she was) in a
biscuit-colored suit and hat of wallflower
brown tulle. Miss Frances Boss brought
the tall, handsome Roberts girl with the
ambitious Greek name; also an accom-
plished fox-terrier that, at the word of
command, says a paternoster “for our boys
nt the Front.” Some incognito celebrity in
a sports suit of the color of kippered her-
rings stole into the party like a conspirator
and sat rolling his eyes and his chewing-
gum. Whether he dropped in accidentally
or Avas a set-piece one could not say. Others
noticed among the crowd were Judge Back-
house, Frank Harvey, Mr. and Mrs. Kay
Souper, Jeanie Pollock, Walter Bentley,
Hugh Huntly and Mr. Kimball.
Matrimony got an unexpected lift in the
public estimation when on Saturday Wedding
Day won the Challenge Stakes at Rand wick
with the odds at 25 to 1. And members of
the very set you would never expect to back
the holy estate were the ones who gathered
in the shekels. There are some people
Avhom experience cannot teach. But the
handicap that generally follows success may
divorce Wedding Day from the winning
post on Anniversary Afternoon.
The flat and other places Avliere straw
hats and hard-hitters most do congregate
Avere very populous on Saturday, for the
lawns were lovely; but the antiquated tunes
dug up by De Groen’s band from some sar-
cophagus reverberated sadly through empty
stands. Tulle frocks were much worn, and
looked appropriate. Mrs. Fred McMaster
backed her fancy in a symphony of blue
and white, and Mrs. Nivison (Walclia) was
in a lacy robe of white and a black hat with
blue flowers. ‘ Mr. Jack Campbell was pre-
sent, but I didn’t see any of his womenfolk ;
in fact, the names of the multitude I didn’t
see Avould fill a column.
The Metropolitan rifle clubmen who rolled
up to the tune of about 900 on Saturday
afternoon to take a farewell of Brigadier-
General Ramaciotti were certainly a miscel-
laneous lot as far as costume and hirsute
appendages go. But their hearts seem to be
in the right latitude and longitude, and their
bandsmen’s uniform dandy, whatever their
crochets and demi-semi-quavers may be.
Skylark Melba chirps cheerily from Hono-
lulu, which she says is a modern paradise.
All plan to give her a joyous time. The
U.S.A. Regiment stationed at Honolulu
serenaded the Diva and ended their pro-
gramme with “God Save the King” out of
compliment to the Britisher. Little Peggy
Center, Melba’s pupil, is giving a concert,
and when the mail left every seat had been
booked in advance. Melba is hopping off
to San Francisco to meet her old pal Lady
Susan Fitz Clarence, who has lost her hus-
band in the Great Push. Several sacks of
gold have been proffered to the singer to
travel eastwards towards Manila, but “No,
I am on a holiday,” is the answer. Yet she
adds: “I may sing in California if I can.
get time.” She hopes to be able to induce
Lady Fitz Clarence to come and inspect
Coombe Cottage in the autumn. The afore-
said Susan is both a wit and a beauty and
an excellent amateur actress to boot.
Emile de Baillou, general manager of
Messageries Maritimes in Australia, who
died in a Sydney private hospital the other
day, had looked after the interests of the
French shipping firm for 32 years in vari-
ous parts of the world, including Port Said.
Shanghai, Cochin China, Zanzibar and
Noumea. He was president of the Alliance
Franqaise and the French Benevolent Asso-
ciation in Sydney, and as an ardent linguist
who spoke out-of-the-Avay tongues like Hin-
dustani and Arabic, as well as most Euro-
pean languages, he was specially interested!
in the Institute of French .Conversation
which ex-Education Minister Carmichael and
Consul-General Chayet started in Sydney
two years ago. Every Wednesday night de
Baillou was in attendance at the old Girls’
High School in Castlereagh-street to talk
French to the flapper from Mosman or
Strathfield who wanted to practise inquiries
for the inkstand or her grandmother in that
language. A pretty, fair-haired daughter
generally supported him on these occasions.
Three sons are among the poilus helping to
turn the Hun out of France; and one of
them won the French War Cross the other
day.
The strangest court-martial ever held in
Australia sat one day last week. And,
though the papers haven’t said a word about
it, it passed sentence of death, too. It was
out at the new Zoo. A consignment of
monkeys, mostly little fellows, arrived in
Sydney lately, and the Zoo people bought a
lot of them. Cages were not, however,
ready, so they were left in their travelling
boxes. A couple, not liking the look of
things, decided to get back to Central
Africa or some other place where the diet
isn’t so largely peanut, and, biting their
way out, made off towards Clifton. They
were soon posted as absconders, and the
Zoo’s Military Police went out with a small
decoy monkey—an old inhabitant of the
camp. He filled his part so well that, before
they quite realised what had happened, the
two deserters were under arrest and back in
the Zoo. Apparently the three were quite
good friends. But when the Tyrant Man
had turned his back the court-martial was
held —by the prisoners. They didn’t leave
any written record of the proceedings, but
as far as can be made out from the gossip
in the monkey cages the decoy was charged
with having sold himself to the enemy,
and sentenced to death. Anyway, next
morning he was found dead, torn to pieces.
His judges had left nothing to chance, and
had been his executioners, too.
One of 50 nurses who left a while ago
for India and other parts writes in gasps
of astonishment at the unexpected good
time the Army Nurses Club gave them in
Adelaide. “First they took us to the Club,
on North Terrace, and made us quite feel
that it belonged to us; then off they
whisked us to the Grand Central, the finest
thing Adelaide has in hotels, and lunched
us; then a talk and afternoon-tea at the
Club; and between times motor-cars ran
us about, taking us to see friends if we had
any (and wc all felt we had a thousand
before we left), or for a spin through parks
and gardens if we liked that better. But
even that didn’t satisfy the Club. Somehow
it was found that some of us—we had been
whipped together from N. S. Wales and
Queensland and Victoria and Tasmania and
Maoriland —hadn’t deck chairs. ‘Oh, that
won’t do!’ and oil these hospitable people
pelted, and a consignment of chairs went
down to the Harbor with us. Where they
came from I don't know —‘We just scram-
bled them together,’ the superintendent,
Airs. Kelly, said, as though she ought to
apologise for not knowing all along that we
didn’t have chairs. Then they saw us back
to the boat and blushed when we tried to
say thank-you, and insisted on'saying thank-
you to us instead.”
The lied Cross Book depot is gaining
itself a reputation as a matrimonial agency.
Another of its devoted workers, Miss Wat-
ford, has announced her engagement—to II
B. Baldry, of Essex (Eng). The lady will
now exchange paste-brush and brown-paper
for a needle wherewith to furnish the put-
away box. This is the third attack since
the scissors and paste campaign was initi-
ated.
News arrived last mail of the marriage
of Louisa Beveridge, youngest daughter of
late squatter Beveridge, of Dollar Vale,
Junee. The wedding occurred at Port Said,
and chaplain Cecil Granville, D. 5.0., was
the ’groom. He very nearly missed her.
Once before she almost changed her
name, but on the arrival in Sydney of her
English fiance further proceedings were
abandoned. Mrs. Beveridge, who still enjoys
life, was a daughter of Dr. Robertson, of
Ballarat, and Jessie, her sister, is Mrs. Mat
Sawyer, whose best man owns various sta-
tions at Junee and some good horses in other
parts of the State. Mrs. Granville will live
in England until the strife subsides and her
husband gets into mufti again.
Having despatched daughter Nesta to
India with her three companions, Mrs. F.
C. Griffiths is busy packing up her flat at
the T, and G. Buildings. An early flight for
Colombo with the Fog to follow causes the
upheaval. The popular elder Griffiths girl
goes also, and will be missed in war-work
rooms. The War Chest women are dealing
with the lonely soldiers’ correspondence
until the Griffiths girls get back into the
scheme they originated.
Marjorie Brownlow, the pretty youngest
daughter of Captain and Mrs. Brownlow, of
Double Bay, is engaged to Sergeant Evan
James Williams, son of the late Evan James
Williams and Mrs. Guy Boucher of Eliza-
beth Bay.
The wedding of Thirza Phillips, of
Longueville. to Harold Percy Christmas, son
of Mrs. Christmas, of ISieutial Baj, was
quietly celebrated at St. James s Churc o
Tuesday. The church was decorated with
pink and white flowers and the bride with
white Georgette, an exquisite lace train
and a tulle veil. She was given away-by
uncle Colin Ross. Miss Hazel Phillips, look-
in* like a rosebud in a dress and hat ot
shell pink, was the bridesmaid. Afterwards
a reception was held at the Carlton. A
huge floral bell was suspended over the
table, at which the bride and bridegroom sat,
the other decorations being carried out in
pink and white. Mr and Mrs. Chnstmas
are to live in Melbourne.
Captain Bob Harper, D. 5.0., a son of the
old Scotchbyterian College professor, has
just received the right answer to Ins ques-
tion from Sylvia Lance, of Strathtield.
Old ' squatter Christian and his wife are
mourning the death of their youngest daugh-
ter, the wife of General Syd Christian, her
cousin. About two years ago young Mrs.
Christian migrated to Egypt with her sister
Helen, who did some nursing thereabouts.
Lately Miss Christian returned, and her
sister went on to London, to be compara-
tively handy to her husband, who has a
command in France. Mrs. Antill, of the
War Chest, is the eldest daughter of_ the
squatter Christian’s family, and there’s a
son, William, in khaki.
Mrs. Stephens, who died at Darlingliurst
last week, aged a good old SS, was widow
of that William John Stephens, M.A.,
F.G.S., who in 1855 was headmaster of Syd-
ney Grammar School, and stayed there till
in the ’sixties he founded the Eaglesfield
School, where he reigned for 15 years. Then
he was appointed Professor of Geology and
Paleontology, at Sydney University, and
settled comfortably in that chair till he died
in July, 1890. That’s when T. W. E. David
left off being a geological surveyor and
became a professor.
Robina McLeod, who was shot by her
father’s hysterical chauffeur, is indirectly a
life taken by war. Two of her' brothers are
in a fighting-line. They were early enlisted,
and, thinking of them, the girl would get
exasperated at sight of shirkers. Her fre-
quent remonstrance with young Taylor for
not taking a rifle and goimr brought about
her own death. And a broken-hearted
mother and father mourn their loss. Robina
McLeod was one of the strongest workers
for Red Cross and other agencies. She
was, besides, a fine, handsome, athletic girl,
clever and resourceful.
Mrs. Alex. Hay and Miss Alice Burdekiu
are going east —really east —-as far east
as a ship can take them. Another Sydney
society dame who is hoping to inhabit the
same ' comfortable but unmentionable ship
next voyage is Mrs. Vievers, who is putting
in time at Point Piper, air. and Mrs. East-
man have also an eye on a state room.
May Vale Gilfillan has left her sketching
haunts of old Melbourne beauty gardens, and
is transferring to canvas some of the bold
scenery of the South Coast of our State —
rocky points and brawling surf and the
spiring cabbage-tree palms of that fascin-
ating corper.
On Tuesday night, when the- saved-up
daylight had departed, about 50 gilded
guests had a special view of a colored col-
lection of lantern slides, taken chiefly in
Egypt and Japan by the host of the show,
John Donkin, of Wyalong. Daughter Bea-
trice, looking fragile in ivory fripperies,
helped mother in the hostessing. The view
happened at the Blind Institute Hall, and
among the importants were General Finn
and familv ’(in front seats), Archbishop
Wright’s sister, the Consett Stephen pair
and the pleasant Dowling dame, with a
batch of friends. The lantern will shed its
light to help some Red Cross charities later
on.
Mrs. John Fell, who was one of the most
stick-at-it war workers until she received
the modern reward of virtue, a break-down,
is spending the summer at Tliirroul. Her
cottage is built on a cliff so close to the
Pacific that you can keep one eye on the
doings of the mermaids while you eat your
breakfast oats, and it is only a hop, skip
and jump from your verandah bed to the
breakers. Here she entertains many patri-
otic workers, luring them from their funds
and depots and battalions ,to take a course
ef ozone. Among her recent guests were
Mrs. Blaxland, of Longueville, and Mrs.
Irwin Smith, whose splendid boy Murray
has just given up his life in France. Mrs.
Fell’s own boy, John, jun., who on being
rejected for active service on account of
defective eyesight, went to Egypt and
Europe on war-relief work, has just in-
formed his family of his engagement to an
Australian nurse in England.
Mrs. Edgeworth David, who left rather
suddenly with daughter Molly for England,
was Cara Malet, a slender girl, when, many
years ago, she came from England to be
head of the Hurlstone Training College in
Sydney. Big brown eyes were hers, and a
skin of cream and roses; also a dimple.
One of the many admirers that strewed her
school-marm’s path in Sydney owned a
portly form, wore an eyeglass, and now
adorns high halls of council in an older
country than this. Rut she chose the young
geologist instead. Mrs. David had always
an original outlook and never-failing re-
source. She needed it. Once when she went
on a geologising trip with “Twed,” as she
called her Thomas William Edgeworth
David, they put up for the night at a coun-
try pub. The Professor was astounded when
bedtime came to find his lawful wedded
perched like a white bird on top of the bed-
tester among the mosquito drapery. “Cara,
my deali!” he exclaimed in pale Cambridge
accents. “Whatevah does this mean?” It
only meant, the lady replied, that, as every
other part of the room, including the floor
and the bed, had been covered inches deep
with precious fragments of the Pleisto-
cene period, Jurassic junks, pieces of pale-
ozoic deposit, etc., she had concluded that
this one spot had been reserved for her.
Uncle Sam’s first Congresswoman, Miss
Jeanette Rankin, has some association with
Maoriland. After a strenuous battle to get
woman suffrage in Washington, Cali-
fornia and Montana, she lost her moderate
income and her health at the same time.
Ordered a sea trip she borrowed the coin
for the voyage to Maoriland, and worked
there as a seamstress for six months. Re-
turning, she was offered the Republican
nomination in a State that was supposed to
be Democratic, and the old wise birds have
not yet got over the shock she gave them.
A line from a Melbourne girl:—
‘ There will be lamentations in the land when
Commissioner Piddington takes back to the Ma State
his elegant young secretary Newell, whose pale grey
suitings and delicately-tinted socks have caused him
to be generally known as “the Spring Song.” The
young Irishman belongs to the rale ould genthry,
and he is as determined as ever to accumulate
sufficient strength to force the medical barriers, which
arc at present down between him and khaki.
Contrary to expectations, it was not in
the poorest suburbs that the Baby Clinics,
established last year by the N. S. Wales
Government, were best attended. There are
now 10; and Woolloomooloo, which stands
for all the Eastern Suburbs, was attended
during December by 960 patients—an easy
first. But red-tiled North Sydney was third
with 492, while populous and poorer Alexan-
dria was last but one, with 311. In all,
4684 parents were advised, and their infants
treated, during the month. But, although
one of the objects of the clinics was to
advise expectant mothers, only 59 of them
appeared.
The new coat is amongst war-time labor-
saving garments. It's a happy-go-lucky mix-
ture of a coat and a princess robe. The
owner stands into it—that’s the way—
fastens a few buttons, and looks in the
glass. The effect is good enough on the slim.
But the over-plump have mountainous curves
when they try this dress-in-liaste make-
believe. But, then, who seems to care for
the sorrows of the Fat Lady? Except, of
course, the kiddie who pays 2d. extra to see
superfluous flesh at ■Show time.
Dr. Cecil Purser has just pulled through
a serious operation—performed by the con-
quering Alexander MacCormick. the man
the military geniuses set to work on measles
or something of the" sort behind the trenches.
It is quite surprising that a Government
so fond of putting its fingers into other
people’s pies and upsetting, on general prin-
ciples, the public apple-cart, has not yet
interfered with the Great Bridge Menace.
An Act for the closing of Afternoon Bridge
parties and the compulsory inoculation of
the Smart Set with anti-Bridge serum
would bring a beneficial change over the
scene, and let loose for the service of their
country a whole host of hefty folks who
now spend most of their waking hours going
no trumps. For a year or so of the war
there was a more or less decent interlude in
this time-wasting mania; but the dreadful
daylight thing is becoming acute again.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, January 22, 1917.
My Dear Yakbie, —
Lady Munro Ferguson and a friend, with
a bodyguard of Gent. Helps, had an evening
with the llardeastles at the Princess’s last
week. The Fuller management rose nobly to
the occasion by creating an impromptu pen
in the centre of the D.C., and filling the
cavity with emaciated chairs. The official
pathway to the enclosure was indicated by
a strip of carpet suffering from a bilious
attack, and the petty-cash box was further
assaulted for satin programmes and a pink
bouquet. The vice-regal s were politely
tickled by the perversities of Tony Lump-
kin, but forbore to be vulgahly doubled-up
with laughter for fear of wrecking the deli-
cate furniture. A political flavor was given
to the show by the presence of Joe Cook
and Defence Minister Pearce. Joe’s shiny
head heliographed his great expectations
from a seat in the stalls. Pearce, accom-
panied by his melancholy spouse, was in a
perch upstairs.
G.-G. Ferguson, back from galavantiug in
South Aus. with Gov. Galway, has been
staying with the Clarkes at Mount Gam-
bier. The hosts arc quite used to Governors.
They don’t even have to employ an inter-
preter to converse with them. The Clarke
girls are those highly spectacular maidens
who brighten up racecourse scenery with
their frocks. Mamma Clarke, however, never
changes her style of dress and hair setting,
but remains as much like Queen Mary as
possible.
Melba is taking the usual ’busman’s holi-
day. On the voyage to Honolulu she organ-
ised a concert and sold flags and photos, so
energetically that the Red Cross benefited by
over £l5O.
Commodore Gordon Smith will collect his
wife (1) and children (2) and label his lug-
gage for England in a few weeks, and Cap-
tain Musgrave, R.N., will fill his billet on
the Navy Board. When Mrs. Gordon Smith
brought her good looks to Australia she
first settled with them in Geelong, about
four years since; but lately they have been
housed in Malvern. The velvet-eyed lady
has liked us quite well, but still will be
glad of a glimpse of her own folk at Malta.
By way of reproof to those gay sparks in
khaki who have wedded English girls, Cap-
tain Clive Steele, on service in France with
one of the Victorian Engineer com-
panies, has declared that there is no love
like thfe first love by standing up with
attractive Amie Billson, and saying “I will”
in a tone entirely different from that in
which he addresses his men. It was done in
London when the year was six days old.
Amie, who is well known in the Western
District, has a pa and ma who live at
Toorak, and has made herself more than
useful to the soldier boys here and in the
Cold country as a nurse, being an
R.V.T.N.A. member. Clive Steele, who is
the son of Herbert Steele of Collins-street.
went brilliantly through Scotch College and
the University, and turned his engineering
experience to the disadvantage of the Kaiser
in Egypt and afterwards at Pozieres.
Blue-eyed Jean Knox crosses the pond
with her mother next month. Fianc6 Lieut.
Erie Murray is in France, but hoping to
soon bask in smiles if Fate is kind and four
days’ leave forthcoming.
Vera TJmplielby, daughter of the late
Major Tom, is getting ready for a latter-
day quiet wedding. Jack Spiers, of
the Elsternwick and old-time Geelong
McAllister-S'piers family, is the gentleman
who will be happy to receive congratulations.
Mrs. TJmplielby has sent her blessing to her
chick from Fogland.
Maudie Gregory, just back from England,
brings word that Marie Hall, the Bendigo
heiress, is really married. In future she
will be addressed as Mrs. Shepherd. Her
better half spends most of his days in a
trench. Finally, Marie’s trousseau was just
wonderful!
One of the boys writes me :—•
There’s been a row in the camp of the 2nd
Infantry Brigade Depot, and our Brigadier-General
.1. K. Forsyth was the unconscious cause of it.
When the At Home in the Austral Salon was
first proposed the executive committee intended to
make it a very select welcome to the Brigadier-
General, and a V.R. party was asked to put the
finishing touch on the affair. Suddenly the execu-
tive’s little plan was entirely frustrated, and their
Excellencies signified their intention of being other-
wise engaged. And why ? The sub-committee of
the 2nd Infantry Brigade Depot had issued invita-
tions to its own circle of friends. The result, as
you may imagine, was simply chaotic. The Aus-
tral Salon seats accommodate barely 400, and nearer
800 people turned up. There a huge crowd
standing all night, and the atmosphere of the
Salon was thick. Many who should have been
present didn’t come, and dozens who should not
have been there were conspicuous. The company
was decidedly mixed, and oh !so very dowdy. If
Brigadier-General Forsyth was aware of the muddle,
he good-humoredly didn’t give his knowledge an
airing, and appeared to enjoy his audience almost as
much as it enjoyed him.
Mrs. Roy Greene has been sitting tight
at the family estate near Bacchus Marsh
since father-in-law’s death, seeing only a
few intimate pals at week-ends. Her sport-
ing better-half, having cut loose from the
Stock Exchange, is making his first appear-
ance in the character of a country magnate.
Needless to say, he dresses the part with his
usual accuracy. The pair intend travelling
for a year or two when it becomes easier to
dodge submarines and raiders.
We are rejoicing in the usual invasion of
Bananalanders. Hordes of womenkind are
arriving at Queenseliff, l'ortsea and the
mountains. Easily the most ornamental of
the band is the handsome songstress, Mrs.
Gilbert Wilson, now on a visit to solicitor
Bob Whiting and his family at Macedon.
Mrs. Yal. Macdonald and her husband
have gone to join the army of mixed bathers
at Portsea who disport themselves daily on
the yellow sands in the latest 'Continental
water-garments. The little lady in question,
who hailed from Queensland originally, is
a champion swimmer, and her dark-blue
bathing suit is eminently becoming to her
fair hair and dazzling complexion. Among
other leaders of water revels are the Coopers
—daughter Dulcie, fair, blue-eyed, bewitch-
ing, in company with a brunette mother so
paralysingly youthful that really one can’t
help believing secretly that it’s all nonsense
—she must be an elder sister!
From a Billjemima in London: —
Slim and attractive Maggie Niall Burnham is
busy arranging a decorative flat at Hay Hill. Her
husband was damaged in the lletreat from Mons, and
is now doing work at the War Office. Mrs. Burn-
ham is never forgetful of old Australian pals, and
her hospitality has.the savor of her wit.
Old Melbourne citizeness Connie Waugh
has cropped up over the horizon again.
London-dwelling Australians have spied her
at the Carlton with a Russian entourage.
Her clothes are as wonderful as a sunset in
a fit, and she is as modish as ever. Elderly
Australian dowagers have bowed to her
familiar face ; then, discovering their error,
have rushed and pulled themselves together
with cups of tea and buttered toast.
There was wassail at Fasoli’s on a recent
evening, and afterwards on the top of a city
skyscraper, to mark the departure from Mel-
bourne for Sydney of John Shirlow, the
etcher, whose artistic friends in both cities
are beginning to realise that his unobtrusive-
ness is not the best advt. for his handiwork,
and who consider that besides putting the
acid on the copper-plate, some of it might be
applied with advantage to art-patrons’ gold.
Garry Roberts, the jolly Maecenas upon
whom art and its exponents have never
called in vain, was the founder of the feast,
upon which the glittering superstructure
was placed by water-colorist W. J. McNally.
The bashful John, who is going to Sydney
by boat, was presented with a chaste but
useful compagnon de voyage in the shape of
a kiddy’s tin bucket, brilliantly painted and'
lettered, “Wanted on the Voyage.—John
Shirlow, Retcher.” The accomplices in this
atrocity were Frank S. Williamson, poet
and philosopher; Dave Low, cartoonist;
Bob Croll, educationist and pedestrian ; Hugh
McCrae, bushranger; Dave Wright, Man of
Memory ; E. Wilson Dobbs, Compendium of
Information; and others less well-known to
the police.
Unable to take her place in the ranks,
Mrs. Marion May Hughes, a Malvern
woman who wears a practical mind behind
her glasses, has been hanging out an offer to
help her country. Her proposal, made in
all good faith to Lieut. H. V. Mays, the
local recruiting-officer, is that she should
take the place of a male cook, in either a
home or a public institution, so as to release
him for service in the field. She has a
thorough knowledge of cooking, and she will
hand over all her earnings to the dependents
of the man whose place she fills. She is still
waiting for an answer to her suggestion.
The Stewart divorce case is still in a
state of unfinality, and the aged lawyer-
plaintiff, although he won the first round in
the Divorce jurisdiction, is not yet done
with his youngish second wife. Edith Rosa
Stewart refuses to be downed and takes the
verdict to an appeal. She is a fine, com-
manding Juno with the nose of a general.
Miss Ella Walter writes cheerfully from
England. When Europe burst into flames
she was a nurse in Dr. Shields’s hospital,
Park Lane, London, and with most of
the staff crossed to France and helped to
prepare the first Australian Field Hospital
under the more or less useful guidance of
Lady Dudley. When the hospital was
adopted by the Aus. Red Cross Society
Miss Walter was stationed at Boulogne
and remained there for nearly two years.
Returning to England, she became a
nursing sister at Lady Quilter’s beautiful
home in Suffolk, where she had an eye to
the welfare of convalescent officers. Since
then she has been nursing the Marchioness
of Ripon at Coombe Court. "They are all
charming to me,” she writes, "out none of
them have half such a grand manner as
Mrs. George Chirnside.”
A talented family, which includes
“Kodak,” of The Bulletin, and “Bush-
woman,” lost its mother the other day when
Mrs. Mary O'Ferrall died at Brighton. Her
husband, many years dead, was an old
officer of the Education Department.
That very charming lady, Mrs. Clements,
is sorrowing. Her eldest boy, who was ex-
ceedingly handsome and attractive, died at
war last week in France.
In December about £lOO was netted by
Mrs. Scott Sharp, of Berwick, for the Bed
Cross, out of penny-votes and a soldiers’ box
scheme. She has since been inundated with
letters from country Red Crossers saying
that they had seen the news in The Bul-
letin and would she please let them know
how she did it! "We are getting fearfully
down in funds,” they nearly all wail. Mrs.
Sharp thinks The Bulletin should now
help her find a secretary to deal with the
rush, especially as she has her own troubles
in these Mary Aimless days.
Elsa Grice has at last attained her heart’s
desire. She is oil to London to ljyirse the
sick and wounded. Papa is on the commit-
tee of Melbourne Hospital, and daughter
slipped in there under his arm as a novice
as soon as war broke out. She was put
to scrubbing wards and washing unholy
Chinese, and even Freetraders, but she stuck
it and now she goes away with a tally of
good marks.
Last Friday’s great heat tempted two
girls into the water at Sandringham too
soon after lunch. They were botli quickly in
trouble and sank. One, Ivy Higgins, was
rescued and revived, but May Crichton,
15 years old, was drowned. Miss Crichton
was the daughter of Alderman Crichton, that
city father whose fine voice boomed out the
toasts at the memorable Hughes dinner
given by the Mayor after the Waterloo of
October.
From that cousin of ours: —
I've been week-ending at the Government Glialet,
Mount Buffalo, my dear. It’s an A 1 place—but
never again! Amongst the 50 guests there were one
archbishop, Mi’s. Arch and daughter, one bishop,
Mrs. Do. and daughter, one bishop with no female
impedimenta, one rural dean (also unattached), and
one common or garden reverend and his wife and
family. Sunday was a day to remember. We had
service in the morning, and at night (bitterly cold)
all the good people gathered in the lounge and droned
hymns. Some of the unregenerate wished to indulge
in a sinful game of billiards, but were gently told
that the billiard-room was locked. Of course, none
of us pretended that we knew how to play cards.
It was, indeed, the Day of Rust.
The Alexandra Women’s Club lias moved
into its new Collins-street premises—for-
merly Dr. Beaney’s, later Dr. O’Hara’s.
Delightful—there are over 130 windows to
peep out of at the women in the street! The
dining-room has 20, the reception-room 15.
The Club spent £22,000 on its house, part of
the amount going to build a new storey for
the kitchen. Miss Living, the manager and
secretary, is in London, looking round such
clubs as the Lyceum and Imperial; her
locum tennis is Miss Clarence. N.B. —This
is not a cigarette club.
A. J. Connell, of Benalla, is one of the
latest recruits in Red Cross work. He has
departed, at his own expense, on behalf of
Victoria’s Red Cross business abroad. Each
of the States has made a change of repre-
sentatives oversea. Mr. Connell has leased
his sheep-run near Benalla, and has packed
his sisters comfortably on his Corowa home-
stead to await his return.
Somebody has to do it. Lieut. Bland, son
of Major Bland, who pursues Costs on the
South Coast of N. S. Wales, has just gone
back into the trenches after being patched
up for the third time.
Adelaide’s little lot:
Captain d’Erlanger, the Governors stepson, is con-
valescent, and has been taken from the North Adel-
aide Hospital to Marble Hill. His Ex. himself has
gone for a brief spell to Tasmania before setting
the recruiting ball rolling in early February. He
left here with the G.-G., whose hasty visit was
mostly for the purpose of camp inspections. Mean-
while the Stanleys are seeing something of the South-
East under the guidance of the Clarkes. An At Home
in the Mount Gambier Town Hall (how the village
will fizzle over who was and who wasn’t asked!) and
a day’s fishing on the Glenelg River have been two
of the items on their programme. No doubt there
were other items, but let us not be too inquisitive,
my dear. The visit ended on Thursday. Speaking
of the South-East reminds me that the district is
losing the T. O. Ellises, of Benara, who have de-
parted for Invercargill (M.L.), where Sarah Gamp
and Betsy Prig have to lock the door and pull down
the blinds when they’re so dispoged.
The British Red Cross Unit, of which Dr. A. P. E.
O’Leary, of our Glenelg, is in charge, is now in
Roumania. It is the gift of the miners of the Old
Country to our latest ally, and is to be known as
the Prince Mirces Hospital, in memory of the baby
Roumanian prince who died. Mrs. O’Leary, w T ho was
one of the Bakewells, has been in England saying
good-bye to her husband.
The agitated person who broke into the news-sheets
to say that he had found a snake with wings has
been soothed by the Museum Ornithologist, who
announces that it wasn’t a snake, but a scale-foot
lizard; ■ and that they weren't wings, but rudi-
mentary hind leg>. This sort of thing didn’t happen
here prior to six-o'clock closing.
Another of our lawyers has gone into camp. He
is J. S. Murray, who went from St. Peter’s College
to Cambridge and was admitted to the Inner Temple,
London, in 1909. One of his partners, Lieut. C. I?.
Cudmore, has been for some time in France with
an English regiment.
Having gazetted Mrs. Young to be one of the
Board of Governors of the Public Library and
Museum, the Government announces that it intends
to make similar appointments whenever opportunity
occurs. The Botanic Gardens and Destitute Asylum
Boards already have women firmly seated upon them,
and we have also two more women J's.P. —Miss Amy
Tomkinson and Miss 11. A. Stirling, the latter a
daughter of Professor Stirling.
A piece of the boat which Sturt built when he was
camped on the Stony Desert, about 150 miles north
of IVileannia, so that he might explore “the vast
inland sea,” which he understood lay before him, has
come into Dr. Angas Johnson’s hands. It was sent
from Brisbane to Arthur Searcy, who gave it to the
doctor. The more you hear about that expedition
the more you wonder why it was let out without its
mother.
The Red Cross Tea Garden on Yerco Buildings’ roof
handed in a £5O cheque, which represented its
December profits. As the place is open only on
Wednesdays that’s not bad, is it?
The Ilamilton-Matheson wedding occurred at Glen-
clg last week. He is a Victorian who was locum
tenens for Dr. Alfred- Stokes while the latter was
at the Front, and she is a local girl.
Captain Fletcher, who arrived back in London
from his A.D.C. job here just as war broke out, and
had the bad luck to be interned in Holland after
the Antwerp hash, has been in England on brief
furlough from the internment camp.
The last shuffle of the military cards is to bring
us Brig.-Gen. Forsyth in Brig.-Gen. Irving’s place,
and is to take our present Commandant to Queens-
land. This makes the fourth change of commandants
we’ve had since war broke out.
Lionel Robinson has bought from the
Duke of Westminster Shadowland, which
dweller in silver-fitted stablehalls is to be
given an opportunity to clean up this coun-
try. Shadowland cost Robinson £l3OO, and
Lavius, also from the same stable, cost him
1500 guineas. You’ll only be interested in
these things because they make it look as if
the Australian, is coming to the conclusion
that there is no place like home after his
long sojourn in England in a palace bought
from a seedy aristocrat.
Admiral Creswell’s wife, a staunch worker,
lias remained in town through the dog days
to attend to her x various patriotic interests
and does not contemplate the usual jaunt
to the seaside or the hills. She has an
anxious look in her eyes, for three of her
boys are in the thick of the tussle.
Strinberg, Ibsen and even Olipliant and
Esson evidently don’t pay, for the Repertory
Club is busy organising a show to square
the losses of leader Gregan McMahon. Allio
Robson and Ethel Leon are understood to be
two of the damsels who will be the stars of
the occasion.
The handsome Canon Hughes, of St.
Peter’s, is hobbling about on crutches (there
is six-foot-four of him and a corresponding
girth to propel) amid the condolences of
his adoring flock. A heavy pedestal fell on
his foot and crushed it badly. Then it was
hospital for him and a surfeit of jellies and
home-made cakes.
People possessed of many household gods
are not always to be envied, especially when
they want to go holidaying. Their motor-car
they can have with them always, but their
other belongings haunt their pleasure hours.
A couple of these unfortunate folk left the
house in charge of cook. She was con-
sidered a Peach and a Treasure; so the boss
and the missus started out on a motor
voyage with a light heart. The missus locked
up her silver, put her jewels in a safe deposit
and gave cook her blessing and the run of
the house and larder. Some little time
later, when the holiday-makers were in the
Western District, there came from a neigh-
bor a telegram hinting that they
should return home. Hurrying back, of
course they were delighted to find the
house in a state of chaos. Cook
had decamped without removing the evi-
dences of her glorious house-parties. The
neighbors said that motor-loads of joy-
riders had tooted in to the park o’ nights
and filled the hours with revelry. The
missus’s best evening-dress had been worn,
her nighties were out of their lavender and
showed signs of alien use, and the larder
was despoiled of stores. Sixty dozen eggs
that had been preserved by the careful
housewife against winter were gone, none
knew whither. The precious table-linen was
too awful for words, and the best crockery
smashed. A battalion of repairers and
renovators is now working overtime on the
wreckage.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

The TOP-NOTCH Scotch.
ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Bushman”: “Pick” (B. 7/12/’l6) wants
to know “in what hitherto unheard-of region of
Australia” I have seen forests destroyed by native
bears. Well, the koala was to be found once in
immense numbers along the coast from Shoalhaven
(N.S.W.) westerly to within a few miles of Mel-
bourne. Around the shores of Corner Inlet and Wil-
son Promontory he was specially numerous. Near
the Promontory there is an isolated area of swamp
gum forest about four square miles in extent, half
of which Norman Lindsay’s cobber killed over 20
years ago. Then the hunters arrived and wiped the
little hungry chap out. To-day the timber can be
seen one-half dead, the other half as green as ever.
Bears were so plentiful in this forest that one hour’s
shooting would keep a man busy skinning during the
rest of the day. ,1 could show “Pick” many other
areas of forest killed by the bears if he likes to see
desolation.
» * » *
“E.S.S.”: Most people anti most birds
have a down on the poor cuckoo, because it won’t
hatch its own eggs, but imposes that task on others.
But every time I visit my grape-vines I wish the
bird was more commonly around, for it is the only
friend that will eat the hairy or vine-moth cater-
pillar, a most destructive pest to the viticulturist.
It persists in surviving and ravaging in spite of
everything when its natural enemy is absent. Every
grower should remember that. This would be a hap-
pier country for everybody if only everj'body under-
stood birds.
* * * »
A consignment of relics of the moa, Maori-
land’s extinct giant wingless bird, came to hand in
the Tuturau hills, in Southland, recently. They
lay sft. below the surface in a bed of peat and
were of at least three species. Bones of 15 birds
were closely packed together in a space of about
four square yards, and are reckoned to be the accu-
mulation of centuries —probably carried down from
tire hillside by flood water. In his day whoever
wanted to pat the moa on the head had to climb
up 12 or 14 feet to do it.
“Bad Australian”: Any of you ever tried
carpet-snake lining for your belt? It ranks amongst
the best. The first time I sampled it was with my
old pal Garry, a fine specimen of Upper Burnett
(Q.) binghi, who put up about the best record of
his time in the district as a sprinter and long-
distance jumper. (Incidentally he put up a record
for gin'-slaughter and side-stepping the conse-
quences.) I was bushed and wandering around like_
the Prodigal Son after a miserable night on the
Baramba Creek when I struck Garry’s hospitable
camp, and, being almost famished, didn’t ask any
questions, but got busy on what I knew to be a
fine fat whampoo a la binghi and what I took to
be some kind of fish a la ditto. After I had got
through and Garry had taken a liberal cut at my
plug of Two Seas I asked him to give the fish a
name. The noble savage upended a grass dillybag
which hung on the ridge pole of his gunyah and
out rolled several slices of the “fish.” I didn’t
have to look twice at the sausage-wrap to identify
the animal. Afterwards I frequently ate the same
kind of “fish,” knowing where it was caught, and
in time became as partial to it as I have to the
S. American iguana and the frog and the snail
and the octupus-wing and such-like local delicacies.
The iguana of South American epicures is nothing
like the yellow-and-black. half-bred ’gator which
I used to chase around a gum-tree in my youthful
days in Australia in the hope of blowing him to
kingdom come with an old gun. The South Ameri-
can reptile is just a plain “sleepin’ lizard” with a
Yes-No equator and an unconquerable desire for a
place in the sun. When he is carefully smoked he
eats something like mutton-bird.
“Simon”: Re “A. J. Grey” (B.
28/12/’l6). There is nothing remarkable about non-
drinking birds. Tom Frost, publican, of Waterloo,
Sydney, for years has been a parrot-fancier, and in
giving pets away to his friends he always advises
that they be not given drink. “Spoils their intelli-
gence,” he told me, and he ought to know. It also
brings on diarrhoea.
“Silas Snell”: Has any Aboriginally
naturalist absolute proof in support of the popular
belief that the dappled cucumber swallows her
young? Until such proof is actually forthcoming I
for one must decline to give credence to the story.
So strong is my conviction that this superstition is
the outcome of faulty observation, combined with
bad judgment, that the bush nature-student must
produce a dappled cucumber in the very act before I
can be induced to believe that she really swallows
her young at the approach of an enemy. I have
kept dappled cucumbers for years, and have had
them under close observation for months at a time,
setting a watch upon them when more urgent duties
called me, and never have I seen, never has a faithful
recorder reported, one action on the part of the
cucumber that could be construed into an attempt on
her part to swallow her young, even though excited
thereto by every conceivable device. When shown
a red cow or a portrait of a dry nurse, the dappled
cucumber’s only protective device is the one familiar
to all naturalists —she assumes the exact appearance
of a dormant vegetable.
* *

pulled 12 tons of wool from the Yanko with 35
horses'; bait coming on boggy ground he was forced
uo hook Oiek Maxwell’s team of 35 on to his. Even
then it was no picnic.

“Barcoo”: When tales come up of stock
sustaining life- for long periods without water the
record of these 10 wethers will take some beating.
It was on Isis Downs (Q.) during the big drought.
The jumbucks escaped into a back mountain rock-
strewn paddock that was without surface water of
any kind. Five years afterwards came a deluge.
The Barcoo escaped over its banks, and sheep were
turned into the mountain paddock to save them
from drowning. When they were mustered for shear-
ing 10 outlaws came in with the flock, all with
full, sound merino fleeces, the staple measuring 22
inches. ,
“Warrego Bill” : “Bunkie” (B. 28/12/’l6)
seems to be surprised that anyone should be bitten
by a ’possum. Let me tell him as an expert that a
’possum is quite capable of looking after himself in
a scrap. I have bitter memories of a day in my
youth when I dug a ring-tail out of a hollow log
in Central Queensland. I carried him by the tail
and he promptly turned round and bit me through
the thumb. I can still show the scars. The only
more, painful bite I remember was from a full-
grown horse who objected to the removal of his
winkers.
* * * *
“D.F.” : The fish that worried “Wart” (B.
4/1 /’i 7) was the freshwater flathead or pike, called
by scientists Aphritis. It’s a freshwater fish all right,
though it is also found in the brackish water of
estuaries. It looks for all the world like a miniature
flathead, ' and its habits are much the same; it lies
on the bottom waiting for something to turn up, and
where the bottom is sandy I have seen them bury
themselves in the sand with just their snout and
eyes showing. You can catch them with a worm, but
you have to steer the bait pretty close to them or
they won’t bother about it. Some of the pig fellows
run wefi up towards three-quarters of a pound in
weight. The fish is pretty well distributed in the
Speck (I never saw any on the West Coast) and has
relations in Vic. and also in South America.
* * * *
“Tiaki”: “Try Fluke’s” yarn (B.
14/12/T6) is not the only one told about the Maori’s
ignorance of gunpowder. About a ' century ago a
vessel name ashore about the region of the Kidnappers
and the crew was promptly added to the menu of
good things which a benign deity provides for God-
fearing anthropophagians. A barrel of gunpowder
was among the spoil, and Kaihomo had a plot of
waerenga cultivated and sown with the purapura
korau (turnip seed), promising great vegetable feasts
to his people. The stuff refused to sprout, however,
so Kaihomo put on a tohunga at overtime rates to
do a little incanting. Result: No rain, no turnips
and no tohunga. After the medicine-man’s obsequies
it rained like Sheol. Still no turnips. Then a
Ngapuhi man wandered into the picture. He ex-
plained the nature of things and was bribed with
women and other ornaments to bring a gun from the
top end of the colony. At his first shot Kaihomo
was laid out by the “kick” and threatened to make
the Ngapuhi’s life policy mature, but when he saw
the bullet embedded in a rata tree his delight knew
no bounds. He immediately established a sort of
Belgian raid, and the neighboring tribes furnished
the long-pig diet for months. But Kaihomo stored
his. paura in an old leaky whata and it got wet. A
trifle like this did not stop the ancient savage. He
procured a frying-pan and dried it over a fire. One
day, however, he overdried it and Mr. Kaihomo’s
soul went to Heaven in a dense cloud of smoke.

AT SWEETBRIARS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The world was wide and very young
And thronged with youth’s desires
At Sweetbriars, when the springtide hung
Red berries on the briars.
The green bush walled the garden round,
And birds were everywhere—
Shy curlews on the sweetened ground,
Wood-swallows in the air.
When morn stepped up the golden East
The feathered raiders came
To banquet on the berry-feast,
Green wings and breasts aflame.
And while the early magpies sang
Their songs surpassing words,
From scarlet-blossoming hedges rang
The joyous throated birds.
And, summoned from her dainty home
Of shell and leaf and flower,
Arrayed in green and bronze, would come
The lady of the bower.
Across the grass the winged seeds
Would drift upon the breeze,
While warblers, scuttering in the reeds,
Raised mingled melodies.
A butcher-bird upon the bridge
With searching eyes would seek
Furred quarry on the rugged ridge
That sloped to Tony’s Creek.
And Tony’s Creek would babble by
Not minding anything—•
The whence, the whither and the why
Of its sweet journeying.
From over reach and sandy bar,
And pool of summer foam,
Came sounds of lowing, faint and far,
Of cattle coming home.
And where the ferns bent down to glass,
And gaze upon their charms,
The plovers in the tufted grass
Would shrill their loud alarms.
Beyond the garden and the hedge
A whisper came to me
From o’er the green horizon edge
Of youth’s fair Arcady.
And jesting much at ancient Care
I followed on, her track ;
And evermore I forward fare,
And evermore look back.
And evermore with rose and pearl
The dawn relights the blue.
Oh, little grey-eyed, homestead girl,
Do you remember, too ?
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
Wo have examined the foregoing Balance Sheet, made up to the 31st December, 1916, and report that
we find the several items therein stated to conform to the Balances at Head Office and to the
Balance Sheets furnished by the Branches, these latter being certified to by the respective Managers and
Accountants.
Wc have counted the Cash and examined the Bills held by the Bank at the Head Office in Brisbane
on the 31st ult., and hereby certify that we have found them correct.
We further certify that in our opinion the Balance Sheet is full and fair, properly drawn up, and
exhibits a true and correct view of the affairs of the Bank as shewn by the books on the 31st Decem-
ber, 1916.

Mitchell Completeness means Keener Pleasures
You have in the Mitchell Motor-car the
essential things—durability, comfort, relia-
bility.
You have more —you have twenty-six extra
features which make for added comfort,
increased strength, greater power.
These extra features come to you without
advanced cost to you—they are paid for
by the factory savings effected by the
efficiency methods of the efficiency engineer,
John W. Bate.
John W. Bate has installed in the Mitchell
model factory 2092 efficiency machines.
Waste lias been reduced to a niinirnnni. Ten
thousand savings have been made.
Because of these economies in manufacturing
the Mitchell offers 20 per cent, extra value.
It embodies costly features which other cars
are forced to omit.
The Mitchell combines in one master model
all the best features of 257 of the world’s
finest cars.
You have to ride in it to realise the supreme
comfort and smoothness of riding given by
its Bate 50-inch cantilever rear springs, and
the roominess due to its long 120-inch
wheelbase.
Actual experience will bring home to you
the life-long endurance of the Mitchell. Six
Mitchells have averaged 164,372 miles each—
over 30 years of ordinary service. You will
appreciate, too, the utter reliability of its
powerful engine, fitted with unfailing mag-
neto ignition.
The surpassing beauty of the Mitchell will
ever be a source of pride and delight to you.’
Test the Mitchell yourself—enjoy the keener
satisfaction which the unique completeness
of the Mitchell will bring you.

“It is ill arguing with a hungry man,” says the
old proverb. But it is worse playing tricks with a
sick one. When the doctor orders brandy he means
real good stuff —the best.” Give the patient
Martell’s, and both he and his doctor will be
satisfied. Being rich in the higher ethers it is
unequalled in case of sickness.
Besides, it is perfect for social purposes.

Beauty Doctor Tells Secret
Beauty Doctor Gives Simple Recipe to
Darken Grey Hair and Promote its
Growth.
Miss Alice Whitney, a well-known beauty
doctor, recently gave out the following state-

“N’importe”: Re “Will o’ the Wisp” (B.
7/12/’l6). The mysterious illumination was pro-
br-Ur due to incandescent petroleum. This pheno-
menon is not seen over all oil-fields, but according to
Beeby Thompson occurs in the Baku and East Indian
fields. The cause is the forcing upwards of petroleum
through some pervious stratum by water pressure—-
petroleum is lighter than water. This finely-divided
petroleum ignites spontaneously and may continue
to glow for hours. On January 21, 1911, from
Ivingscote, -Kangaroo Island (S.A.), I witnessed an
illumination in the neighborhood of Murray’s Lagoon.
25 miles distant. A stretch of country about eight
or 10 miles from east to west was brilliantly lighted
for quite half an hour after 1 first saw it. This part
of the country has been taken up for oil several times
in the past, and many indications abound. The
cause of the incandescent petroleum appears to be
easily determined. The line of cleavage of earth-
quakes runs north and south through the Island.
After the big shock of 1902 Murray’s Lagoon went
dry, but in 1910 the rainfall was double the average
and the lagoon was choke full. Next year was
normal in rainfall, but 1912 was dry until the end
of June, when there was a heavy fall. About a
fortnight later another extensive illumination was
seen over the same country lasting for an hour and
a half. I have heard also that a similar illumination
has been seen near the mouth of the river Murray.
* * * *
“Fiat Lux”: What Australian bird lays
the largest setting of eggs? I nominate the little
woman about the same size as a canary, known on
the Murchison goldfields (W.A.) as the Java
sparrow. . Last summer I watched a pair build a
nest. I waited till the lady had placed 19 in the
bank. Then, as she appeared to be making a hobby
of it, I left the district.
“J.II.”: Know, any of you, of a fresh-
water seal? At Lake Echo (Tas.), far from the
haunts of the tourist, I observed a strange-looking
creature that seemed to belong to the seal or sea-
elephant family. It had a smooth round head of
the bulldog type, and seemed to be about the size
of a yearling calf. We couldn’t get closer acquainted
owing to its unsociable habits. Local residents told
rpe that this animal had been in the lake for as
long as they could remember, and had always been
alone.
* * * *
“Lamb”: “Lantana” (B. 14/12/’l6) asks
if a tally of 1835 sheep, shorn in one day by nine
men, is an Australian record. If it is, then the
Maoriland boys can give their Australian cobbers a
start. This tally was put up at Collins Bros.’ Aranui
shed at Mangamahu, near Fordell. Six men turned
out 1442 sheep during the usual shearing hours in
a day. Tallies: A. Wilson 305, G. Bolton 267,
C; Johnston 246, D. Capieron 209, W. Mahoney 208
and R. Gray 207 —an average of 240 sheep per man.

“S.A.”: Re “Barcoo” (B. 21/12/’l6),
who nominates Mitchell grass as our greatest natural
fodder. I move an amendment to include the kurrajong
tree, the hardy evergreen that has pulled many a
grazier through when every blade of grass had gone.
It’s always there, but “Bareoo’s” grass isn’t. The
stock will come at it any season of the year, and the
more it is lopped the spriglitlier it bobs up again.
Five hundred sheep can exist on one kurrajong for
two days. Edible timbers of all descriptions—broad-
leafed box, belar, myall, rosewood, whitewood, wilga
(the non-drooping type), willow (the immigrant, not
the indigenous) and wild orange—all make a pro-
found and respectful salaam to their superior in relia-
bility and succulence, the kurrajong. Gangway there,
please, for the champions of the broad-leafed box 1
“Farmer”: Re the sudden death of sheep
mentioned by “J.Z.R.” (B. 14/12/’l6). The experi-
ence of the sheepowners in Victoria Valley, at the
foot of the Grampians, may throw some light on the
cause of the Monaro (N.S.W.) trouble. The folks
in this valley are very careful about disturbing their
sheep when the bloom is on the lobelia (Isotoma
fluviatilis). If excited the sheep die, and then have
to be skinned at once. The stench is awful. The
little vegetable is a trailing growth with a bluish
blossom, and though innocent-looking it is the worst
member of a shady family who have a narcotic prin-
ciple. Other stock are sometimes affected also. The
other day a well-bred mare did a bit of a spurt from
the paddock to the homestead and then fell dead.
The diagnosis was lobelia.
“Barcoo” : Contributors on the dangerous
effect of mulga and other Australian bush splinters
when they become embedded in the flesh of horses
or cattle, neglected to name the “gutta-percha,”
which grows profusely in the saucer-like depres-
sions and swampy forest round the Gulf of Car-
pentaria. It has a dark-green foliage, grows up
to 20 pr 30 feet high, and when the bark is punc-
tured it exudes a milky substance. The quickest
way to cure the horse that gets damaged by this
shrub, especially if the twig be green, is to hand
out the victim a pill from the business end of
the Winchester. Stockmen when mustering in
“gutta-percha” country are particularly careful, for
should a horseman be dashed against the limbs of
the tree, the result is a painful running sore that
will take months of careful dressing to cure. In
the case of a splinter not being quickly removed
blood poisoning will set in, and even when a
cure is effected the patch has a tendency to break
out again. The worst danger, though, is when
the rider is tearing after breaking beasts and gets
slashed across the eyes by a branch. Total loss
pf the sight has followed; in any case months of
intense pain and suffering.

“Yondys”: At Moorland, on the N.S.W.
North Coast, a carpet snake 13ft. lin. long was
killed recently by two youths, who then proceeded
to skin the horror. They found 63 young snakes
in the body, all alive, and about 6in. long. Is
this a record?
“Jumbuek”: I’m not entering it as a
record, but it takes beating. A team consisting of
Clarey, Martin and Gantley pressed 102 bales with
a Ferrier wool-press in one day. The bales were
all over the standard weight and properly sewn;
there were none of your patent bale-hooks in those
days.

“E.S.S.”: About the three most shudder-
some things in the bush are the big yellow centi-
pede, the scorpion and the small stinging grub—a
black and yellow gregarious horror that clings in
clusters to the leaves of young box trees, and noisily
throws up its conjoint tail as you pass by. A 12-
inch centipede is just as objectionable for a bedmate
as a black snake. It calls occasionally on the tent-
dwellers, and it simply loves bark humpies. The scor-
pion seldom comes out of its hiding before dusk.
Tn the North it often grows to a length of six
inches. The female, like the female spider, is
larger and fiercer than the male. It carries its
long, armed tail curved over its back, and gener-
ally brings it to the house in the firewood. The
red-striped shares with the scorpion the distinction
of being the most poisonous of the Australian arach-
nida. The poison causes severe shooting pangs,
much swelling and nausea, but is not fatal to a
healthy being. A' station manager in Tongowoko
county (north-west N.S.W.) succumbed to the bite
of one of these crawlers in 1900; but such cases
are extremely rare. The scorpion strikes and stings
with the finely-pointed weapon on the end of its
tail; centipedes and spiders inflict their wounds
with the falces —the curved, horizontally-working,
pincer-like instrument with which the jaws are fur-
ifished.
THE COMING MILLENNIUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a rumor softly hummin’
Through the pulsin’ atmosphere
That the great Millennium's cornin',
And will very soon be here.
There’s forecastin’ and foretellin’
Of a universal peace
When all bay’netin’ and shellin’
And explosiveness will cease.
All mankind will love their neighbors,
And their neighbors’ wives. The Huns?
Well, they'll manufacture mattocks
From the metal of their guns;
While the Turks, with tears of penitence
A-runnin’ down their cheeks,
With the Bulgars, will be sobbin’
On the bosoms of the Greeks.
Then, I ’most forgot to mention,
We shall cease to fight like brutes,
That a sort of Hague convention
Which will settle all disputes
Will be formed among the nations
(When they’ve got the time to spare
From their war-like occupations),
IV ith the Kaiser in the chair.
Kaiser Bill will swap his sabre
For a length of garden-hose,
Do a bit of honest labor—
Say, plant cabbages in rows—
While in some suburban garden,
Catchin’ snails and sowin’ seeds,
lie’ll forget he’s Ilohenzollern
While lie’s busy hoein’ weeds.
While Von Tirpitz, with a basin, -
Full of love instead of hate,
In a, Nonconformist chapel
Will be handin’ round the plate.
It’s a most attractive vision,
And eve can, without a doubt
In our mind, with great precision,
See the whole thing workin’ out.
We can see the tiger lyin’
Calm, a-lickin’ of his hide,
Most benevolently sighin’,
With a lamb or two inside.
But, from what we know of Proosians,
We’d suggest it would be wise
If the British and the Roosians
And each one of the Allies,
While a-lookin’ sweet as candy,
Kept his faculties alert,
And a few explosives handy
In the bosom of his shirt.
Mind, we’re nuts upon Millenniums,
’Cos we hate all kinds o’ strife,
And we’d love to love our neighbor,
And especially his wife;
But our strong belief increases
That, if Germany’s to burst,
Not to break a peace to pieces,
We must donkey-lick her first!
N.S.W.

“5.0. A.”: A Perth butcher, evidently
wearied of verbal attempts to convince tbe
public of his determination to do nothing
but a spot cash trade, has hung a gun over
his counter, with an inscription attached: —
PLEASE
do not ask for credit, as this gun is loaded.
