“T. 0.1.”: Apropos an old George 111.
penny, dated 1797, found in an excavation
at Nock and Kirby’s, in Lower George-
street, Sydney—Colonel Johnston’s original
grant. J. M. Stevens, who died in London
two months ago, had a curious collection of
copper coins and tokens, gathered in the
Hit or Miss Hotel, Windmill-street, which
had been in the Stevens family for a century.
When J. M. Stevens decided upon demolish-
ing the old house and building a new one, he
packed all the coins and tokens in a large
glass jar and deposited the lot in the masonry
work over the main door—a find for someone
when the house knackers come along in a
century or so.
* * * *
“J.D.C.” : Some time ago Melbourne
hoardings were afflicted with a life-size figure
of an abo. clad only in a smile and a shirt.
He was advertising the shirt. Apparently
the snorts of the wowser were not without
effect, for soon he was given a pair of pants.
Socks followed. Then he further improved
liis worldly position and at present is garbed
in evening dress. I’m looking for him in
mayoral robes next. Anything is possible in
a democratic country.
“D.F.”: The Speck’s fruit industry made
a good showing last year in spite of freight
troubles and the cutting-out of the South
American market, the export being the big-
gest yet. There were 1,812,045 cases of
fruit, more than half of which Sydney took,
and 34,536,6301 b. of jam, most of which went
to feed the British army, not to mention
odds and ends like dried fruit and pulp and
canned fruit. But in a few years’ time,
when all the orchards planted out in the
last few years come into full bearing, Tas-
mania will have about twice as much fruit
to dispose of as at present. Then perhaps
it will be possible to buy decent apples at
a reasonable price in Hobart.
“McNorth” : Being fairly well acquainted
with industries that have large, comprehen-
sive stinks attached to them—from Chinese
gnapi-making, whereby small herring-like
fish are crammed into tall jars and allowed
to rot and then corked for future reference
at the dinner-table, to the growing of the
durian, that evil-odored but luscious fruit
known also as the Pride of the East—l
boldly state that the most smellful of the lot
is trochus shelling. These- shells are more
or less numerous on most of the reefs of
Torres Strait and the top of the North
Queensland coast. Being spiral cones and
not bivalves, they cannot be opened like
mother-of-pearl, so to clean them they are
simply placed on deck and the fish allowed to
rot out. When half a ton or so has been
under this treatment for a week or two the
stink interferes with the vessel’s sailing. If
any land is handy, the dreadful things are
put ashore, and the meat-ants have a beano.
Boiling has been tried, but even then some
of the too affectionate odor remains. Inci-
dentally, since the war, there has been a
great demand from Japan for trochus —for
button-making, they say.-
* * * . „ tt
“Cape Helles”: itoom for my cobber
Walker to dispute the position of Champion
Bluffer with the Billjim mentioned bv
“Jobber” (B. 28/12/’l6). In Feb., ’l5, 10
of us from Mena Camp projected a two days’
trip to Toussum, near Ismailia, the scene
of Abdul’s unsuccessful attempt to seize the
Suez. My cobber Walker was entrusted with
the cash to pucliase second-class tickets for
the party, but returned with third-class
dittoes which entitled one to a seat in a box
smelling like the back of a circus. He cut
short the profanity which arose and piloted
us into a first-class corridor car de luxe.
When the native ticket-collector arrived,
Walker distracted him with such a flood of
compliments in Arabic-Australian' on his
resplendent uniform and general beauty that
the grinning Ali, bewildered by such conde-
scension from a white man, left us in posses-
sion. On the return journey the party piled
into a first compartment again with third
tickets —excepting my cobber Walker and I,
as he had ridiculed the idea of buying tickets
again—and were engrossed in a game of
crown-and-anchor when another colored
ticket-puncher arrived. A mass collection of
tickets was made under the direction of
Walker, who demonstrated by Euclid to tbe
confused official that eight tickets were
enough for ten men. Walker failed, however,
to purchase meals for the party in the
dining-car on the strength of an old leave
pass, and not even his brilliant handling of
the porter at the station exit cleared the
gloom from his brow till we arrived at
Sault’s restaurant —where he treated us to
a blow-out of French pastry which mysteri-
ously cost him exactly two piastres.'
A Melbourne Bung, invited to the meet-
ing which was held the other day to consider
the question of selling spirits only by
measure, consulted an old Glasgie gill-vendor
beforehand. “Well,” said the local Bung, "I
suppose it’s right—a man gets just what he
pays for.” “Hey, mon,” said the old Glasgie
landlord, “ye don’t always serve what ye’re
paid for. Ye kin put yer thoomb i’ the dish.”
“X.X.” : One of the best-known Melbourne
pubs, run by one family since 1860, has just
changed hands. It is the Queen’s Arms,
better known as Champions’, at the corner
of Swanston-street and Flinders-lane. The
rent is £3120 a year, so apparently there is
still a penny or two in the beer-pump.
The Vacuum Oil Co. is a mighty good
firm or trust or monopoly as far as its em-
ployees are concerned, anyhow. This Christ-
mas it distributed £lO,OOO in bonuses to its
staff in Australasia. Permanent employees
of not less than a year’s service received a
full month’s salary; and this was quite apart
from ordinary increases of salary, which will
ampunt to another £7OOO. The bonus was
voted as a thanks-offering for loyal co-opera-
tion and in recognition also of the rise in
the cost of living. Twenty years’ continu-
ous service qualifies an employee for a
liberal pension at the age of 60-65; and
every employee has the benefit of a life insu-
rance policy for nothing. These benevolent
schemes induce the loyal co-mperation, of
course, while the co.’s “war policy” is a fair
inducement to recruiting.
* * * *
“Coot”: The bottle-oh has his own Cal-
vary, and he gathered in a deputation to
the Vic. Chief Secretary the other day to
paint a picture of it. It seems that when
lie is visiting the back entrances in search
of his lawful prey the local youngsters watch
their opportunity and filch a couple from his
cart. Then they wait until he reaches the
next street and sell him his own again, or
adjourn to a soft-drink shop and exchange
the bottles for sweets or ice-cream. How he
can be estopped from buying bottles that are
his own he’s blimed if he knows; but he
petitioned McLeod that the owners of sweet-
shops should be prevented from doing any
bottle deals. The Chief Secretary dropped
a tear and sent them away; and that is
probably the end of the matter. Still, it
may have done them good to merely talk
about it.
Brief ad. of long standing in the Melbourne
dailies: “Sir John Madden’s landau for sale,
at Tattersall’s.” Even a landau with a
judicial atmosphere is hard to sell nowadays.
But what a chance for ’Sonnah to get per-
manent space in the National Museum!
* * * *
Norway, with a population of 2\ millions
—about half that of Australia —is a poor
country with no great industries or capital,
less than 2000 miles of railroad and exports
of 20 millions sterling—about equal to the
exports of Algiers. But before the war it
owned two million tons of shipping, or
something over four-fifths of a ton per head
—double the tonnage per head even of ship-
owning Britain. Despite tremendous losses
by submarines, war has increased the Nor-
wegian tonnage greatly; but some of this
is disguised British capital registered in
Norwegian ships with the patriotic intention
of avoiding Government commandeering of
ships and the war tax on freight profits.
Almost every Norwegian has an interest in
shipping, wherefore Norway will be as sorry
to see the war end as if it were a share-
holder in a munition factory.
“J.A.K.” : While passing a George-street
(Sydney) shop the other day a tin scoop
used by grocers to mix sugar with sand
fell from the top storey and clattered on the
pavement, handy to a man who looked as
though he was often thirsty. For a moment
he thought. Then he dived for the scoop,
snatched off his ancient boxer hat and
savagely stabbed the crown of it with the
implement. At that moment a bare-headed
man in his shirt-sleeves hurried out and
said, “Oh, thank you. It dropped out of
the window.” “Yes,” replied the disrepu-
table party; “it dropped all right, and”
(holding out the scoop and the hat) “what
are you going to do about it ?’’ I went into
an adjacent hotel to condole with myself
about the lack of presence of mind that has
cursed me all through my life, and presently
the man of the battered hat came in and
ordered a whisky. And paid for it with a
nice new pound-note.
A SONG OF BATTLE EGGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rome, Tuesday.—La Stampa publishes a letter
written by the Papal Nuncio at Vienna, stating that
eggs in that city cost 20s. per dozen.
In her strong-room, late and early, sits the Leghorn
laying fairly,
While without the battle thunders and the loaves
and sausage fade;
For the message on the cable tells of poultry staunch
and stable
Laying eggs worth one-and-eightpence in the land
where wars are made.
Cash is gone and bits of paper pay the grocer and
the draper
For the wares behind their counter that they still
make shift to sell;
But the fowl-yard rocks with pleasure and the nests
o’erbrim with treasure
When the Leghorn takes advantage of the Hapsburg’s
hunger-bill.
Thus for Austria, home and beauty every pullet does
her duty ;
And the lien-fruit shines like nuggets of the long-
forgotten gold.
And the wealthy poultry-banker with a layer kneels
to thank her,
While the neighbors by her cackle of her splendid
deeds are told.
Yes, he kneels and duly praises the triumphant shout
she raises,
While without the battle thunders and the loaves and
sausage pine;
For he sees a king come creeping and a queen and
duchess weeping
Just to feast their eyes in secret on a new-laid egg
divine.
N.S.W.
MARY McC.
TO MEASURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Licensed victuallers in Melbourne have met and
considered the practice of selling spirits to measure.
The meeting was almost unanimously in favor.
See how this awful war our fine
Free institutions throttle. ,
Our new conditions undermine
Long-cherished rights, and now they steal
One privilege we love a deal—
The freedom of the bottle!
The old-time barman did not' think
His faith in us was risky,
When he let each man pour his drink;
We took a spanking nobbier then
And Heaven really helped the men
Who helped themselves —to whisky.
They talk of drinks “to measure.” That
My worst foreboding clinches.
’Twill suit the chap who’s big and fat —
But what of me.? thirst is full
Six foot, but by f the tape I pull
Four feet eleven inches!
VICTORIA.
BILLY T

Grey Hair—Home
Remedy,
An old-fashioned, home-made recipe restores
youthful appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey hair
is not desirable and plenty of reasons why
hair dyes should not be used. But, on the
other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want it.
To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has only
to get from the chemist two ounces of con-
centrate of tammalite and mix it with three
ounces of bay rum. Apply to the hair with
a small sponge for a few nights and the
greyness will gradually disappear. This
liquid is not sticky or greasy and does not
injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the for-
mula.—Advt. [2

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Tudor policy : “Exhughes me.”
Instead of living on its own resources
N. S. Wales has in the past six years pawned
itself to borrow about £45,000,000; and the
insane suggestion is now made by the P.L.L.
executive that it can get out of the bog by
going further into it. Also, having
£2,000,000 more interest to pay, the sugges-
tion is made that it would help the situation
if the workers worked six hours a day in-
stead of eight—on the well-known principle
that if you have another ton put on your
load the best way to shift it is by unhooking
a couple of horses from the term.
A SAFE PREDICTION.
TOMKINS (the strong man): “If I marry
I’ll rule the roost, or lonow why."
REV. N. PECK (bitterly): “You’ll know
why, all right."
The Federal Treasurer announces that in-
vestments in the War Loan will be free not
only from Commonwealth and State income
taxes, but from the wealth levy as welL
The principle is all wrong: these backstair
concessions always are—they are in the
same category as free postage for M’s.P.,
free motor-cars for Ministers and their
wives, free tram-rides for aldermen, and
similar perks. The State or other public
authority in such cases never knows what
it is paying; and the whole arrangement
is unbusinesslike. Poynton might well try
a new line: let him offer 54 per cent, or
even 5| per cent, for money and let it rank
as an ordinary investment. The size of the
interest will .then attract tens of thousands
of pounds which will not otherwise be
secured. This is especially the case in con-
nection with small investors. For them
income tax has no terrors. They have never
paid any, or, if any, none worth mentioning ;
and to them it is a 44 per cent, investment
and no more.
THAT ORGANISING GENIUS AGAIN.
Germany is using 50,000 criminals in her latest
army. —News item.
RECRUTTING SERGEANT: “Come on out, Hans
you’ve got to fight!"
RECALCITRANT HANS: “Fight he verdommt !
Haven’t I aways broke der law? Veil,
I demand me rights as a griminal to
finish der sentence!”
When the Treasurer is looking round for
new ways of raising loan money for war
purposes he ought to consider the question
of selling annuities. There are dozens of
women and old people who have limited
means and are living partly on their cap-
ital and partly on interest. They have no
dependents and they are under no obligation
to leave any principal behind them. For such
people an annuity is the very best thing
possible; they would hand over the whole
of their capital and get annually whatever
amount it would provide, the entire liability
m, the State expiring with their death.
These people are chary of investing any
of their principal in a war loan because
they know they must nibble at their capital
every year. Deferred annuities might be
considered, too; for there are other people
who, though not at present in need of money,
want to provide themselves with an annuity
m five or 10 or 12 years’ time. Annuity
tables don’t require any elaborate calcula-
tion ; Knibbs, with a couple of good actu-
aries, could fix them up in three days. They
offer a chance of getting otherwise unobtain-
able money; and all the money that can be
obtained is badly needed just now.
If ever there was a time when the N. S.
Wales State Labor party needed wise guid-
ance it is now ; and it seems to be getting
veiy little of it. When the party came into
power its declared purpose was to stop the
loan extravagance of the other crowd. The
culwlm' 1 had been borrowing less than
io, 000,000 a year; the Labor Government
gradually worked up to £9,000,000. The
party that was opposed to booms and bubbles
has merely blown the old bubble bigger and
biggei, until now the thing has burst and
thousands of .men are being thrown out of
Government jobs; and all that the PL L
executn-e can suggest, apparently, is more
bubble —and then more burst.
M.L. Premier Massey orating in London :
Whatever happens the Empire must set its teeth.
Good! Bill with his jaws clamped together,
thinking Imperially in a silk hat, will be an
inspiration. They haven’t been clamped
lately, and the result has been most depress-
ing.
• Donald luackiunon, director-general of
recruiting, represents Prahran in the Yic.
Assembly. Various attempts have been
made by the local P.L.C. to oust him, the
last Labor candidate having failed by only
a few hundred votes. Now it is proposed
to call on Frank Hyett, the vociferous secre-
tary of the Kailwaymen’s Association, and an
ardent preacher of the gospel of strike, to
submit his name for selection as Donald’s
opponent at the next election. As the rail-
waymen are strong in Prahran, Ilyett’s pre-
election will be a certainty. A soldier named
Parker, formerly an
articled clerk in the office
of lawyer Joseph Woolf,
was originally chosen to
contest the Prahran
seat for Labor next
time. But many things
have happened since
then, including Park-
er’s departure for the
Front. And why should
a soldier be chosen,
anyway, when a "pro-
minent industrialist” is
available to fill the
breach with violent
language?
“Y.Z.” : As an indus-
trious and affectionate
gentleman with an ex-
cess of forebears, I de-
sire to draw attention
to an anomaly in in-
come-tax conditions. It
is specified that an
allowance of £13 is per-
mitted for each child
wholly maintained by a
taxpayer, so that a man.
with four youngsters on
his hands sidesteps the
penalty on £52 of in-
come. I do not object
to that. By. all means
let the local producer
be encouraged. But why
is it that a child is not
allowed a similar deduc-
tion for every parent
wholly maintained by
him? Many a young
man in this State is the
sole support of a pair
of parents, with per-
haps a grandparent and
an aunt or so thrown
in, and it is shamefully invidious to wholly
ignore this white man’s burden, while gra-
ciously rewarding the fellow who, after all,
is only displaying a virtue common to animal
creation.
This is how it goes: Hughes, lifuse,
I-Ifwhews!
From the report of a speech at a Sydney
meeting “convened by the P.L.L. executive
to protest against the retrenchment policy
of the Federal and State Governments” :
They, the members of the P.L.L. executive, were
not opposed to the voluntary system, but they re-
fused to take any part in recruiting work until such
time as the Government restored the labor market to
its normal state.
This shows how the Holman Government
has demoralised the P.L.L. It thinks now
that a loan drunk is the “normal state” of
the country. And the tragedy of it is that
instead of punishing the profligates who are
responsible for the orgy, this P.L.L. execu-
tive, which ought to be the brains of the
Labor movement, deliberately proposes to
punish Billjim who is fighting the country’s
battles at the Front.
Cohen having put up the shutters, the
spendthrift N. S. Wales Government finds
itself left with uncompleted works all over
the country and railway lines that end
nowhere. One deputation wept over the
half-finished Nimitybelle railway the other
day; a speaker at the Labor Council talked
of hundreds of thousands spent on the Picton
line, where work is now hung up “for want
of a few thousands to complete it”; and
the Condobolin-Broken Hill line has stopped
short at a wire fence. Thus we face the
gloomy future with a few millions more of
unproductive debt—which means, of course,
a few hundred thousands a year more to pay
out of taxation.
The Censor has failed to realise the possi-
bilities of his job. He would be less disliked
—he might in cases be almost loved —if he
would quit suppressing the information that
other people want to publish and would
publish what other people want to suppress.
For a start he might investigate some of
the instances in which returned soldiers
haven’t been able to get the billets their
employers promised to keep for them. A
weekly list of those names would make a
thrilling story. In any case the Director of
Repatriation, when he arrives, ought to turn
one of his eyes that way. Prim,a facie the
best repatriation is the return of the soldier
to his old job; and the fact that a man is
in need of anew job should set the director
asking why.
Federal Works Minister Lynch, always a
strong conscriptionist, last week said that
some form of compulsion will yet have to
be resorted to.” But, owing to the blunder
of the referendum, the difficulties are now
tremendous. In particular Australia is
bound to pay great regard to the heavy
‘‘No” vote cast by Billjim. That throws on
Australia the responsibility of exhausting
every possible means of supplying reinforce-
ments by voluntary effort. Everybody has
been told ten thousand times that nothing is
too much to do for the men at the Front;
and Australia will not be doing very much
i c it adopts a plan—no matter how good
we may think it is—which very nearly half
the men at the Front have begged it not to
adopt. So far in order to get recruits there
is one plan we have not yet tried—we have
not handed over the business to a Gov-
ernment which has declared that the volun-
tary system is sufficient. The present situa-
tion is just as anomalous as if, a referendum
having declared that absolute Freetrade was
the proper policy, a Protectionist Prime
Minister clung ,to office to put his enemies'
policy into effect.
A demand is made by the Australian
wheat-cocky for a guaranteed 4s. a bushel
for the next two or three years. If he
doesn t it, he will, he threatens, go out
of the business in large numbers. He pro-
bably won’t; but, all the same, it is an
unpleasant and from a national point of
view highly dangerous fact that there isn’t
nearly as much money in the wheat-growing
business as there ought to be. By far the
greater number of Australian cockies don’t
average as much as a city tradesman; and
that is not allowing any interest on then-
capital, or taking into account the hours
they work and the inconveniences they put
up with. Their great trouble is that, while
others can pass on increases in costs, they
can’t, because their market is mostly foreign.
Cocky is up against a stone wall; and the
more the rest of the community takes the
less there is for him. If he doesn’t co-
operate and reduce his costs where he can,
he is in for a bad time.
A tremendous question has been agitating
Bendigo for weeks past. The old town has
been stirred as never before. Shopkeepers
have met and met again ; the City Council
has held special sittings; deputations have
waited on the Mayor; petitions have been
forwarded to the Premier, and there are still
alarums and excursions. For the issue hangs
yet in the balance. Anything to do with the
supply of reinforcements? With the war
loan? Don’t trifle! The question is.
gentlemen and patriots, shall Bendigo take
its weekly half-holiday on Wednesday or
Saturday ?
THE NEW EXCUSE.
“Thought you would be here at two-thirty.
Do you know it’s now half-past three?”
“Oh, that’s all right, dear! It’s really only
two-thirty, you know.”
THE CEASELESS SHIRK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The many things the Allies do,
The foolish things or wise ;
Strange stories that are partly true,
Or altogether lies;
The plans and schemes which critics guess
Will extricate us from the mess—
All these are published in the press.
And oftentimes, I must confess,
I wonder at the work
Accomplished in this time of stress
Against the Force of Frightfulness.
But —still the shirkers shirk !
The fighting on the Western Front
Is worrying tlie foe,
Whilst Kussia grimly bears the brunt
Of battles ’midst the snow ;
Brave Billjim mourns his missing mates;
■ The angry orator orates
Of bungles in the Balkan States;
And Europe patiently awaits
The downfall of the Turk.
Sly traitors safe within our gates
Sing silently their hymns of Hates.
But—still the shirkers shirk !
The foe remains on foreign soil,
And treats its sons as slaves
To help him in his warlike toil;
And we —we rule the Waves,
Where crops of seaweed grow, instead
Of corn, which serves to make the bread
Whereon all fighters must be fed,
And mines are but a cause of dread,
And deep-sea monsters lurk.
Big bombs from airships overhead
Kill babies ,as they lie abed.
But —still the shirkers shirk !
So many things there are to do
Before the war can end,
And we must win, or we must rue
The reckoning, my friend.
So many things are left undone
(Conscription over here, for one)
The test of grit has scarce begun.
1 see no glint of gladsome sun
A-peeping through the murk ;
A stream of blood has yet to run
Ere Peace be forced upon the Hun;
No peace can last which is not won.
But —still the shirker shirks !
VICTORIA;
EDMUND FISHER.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
-To have won the A.J.C. Challenge Stakes
at Randwick on Saturday Baronet Cooper’s £5OOO
Red fern would -need to have been a stone better horse
than he was that afternoon. It was. the weight that
stopped him, and, taking all the circumstances into
consideration, there is little room for doubt that the
handicappers are merely treating- this horse on the
blind. Rosedell and Dame Acre were in the* van to
the distance. There the latter melted out, and half
a furlong further Rosedell was punctured. ' Here
Redfern took charge, and for a moment looked like
coming on, but 9st. 111 b. was a big anchor, and last
year’s winner (Quinn’s Post) and Wedding Day
sprinted out for home. 'Hie mare had most steam
left, and just struck the line as her head moved in
front. Though she had been well in the public eye
lately on suburban and metropolitan courses, and had
appropriated the Villiers Stakes three weeks ago,
there was great joy in Israel over her arrival. She
went begging at 20 and 25 to 1, while Dame Acre
was backed down to 7 to 2. Why this should have
been so no man knoweth.
The betting on this race turned upside down. La
Steppe had been backed during the week, but was
struck out on the course, and Redfern promptlv
shortened from 5 to 2 to 2 to 1. But the public
didn’t know much about Redfern, and went after
Dame Acre at from B’s to 3’s and Quinn’s Post at
12 s to 7’s, the result being that Ilcey, who at an
early stage of the proceedings adopted a conservative
attitude about the high-priced English nag (taking
6 to 4 from place-bettors), was later on frantically
yelling for custom at sto 1. Had she remained
in the race La Steppe would probably have started
at a very short price. Why she was left in until the
eleventh hour and then withdrawn was not explained.
Even if it had been the chances are that it wouldn’t
have made the people who rushed the early doors feel
any more cheerful.
The best performances of the day were done in the
Cooper colors, Colugo winning the w.f.a. Centenary
Stakes from end to end with Bst. 51b. in lrnin. 39f
secs., and First Principle annexing the Welter with
lOst. 21b. In the first of these events there was a field of
only four. Woorak was not produced. Colugo easily
held his place at the finish against a challenge from
’the one-time boom, the imported Bombita, who in
getting within a length of the winner did about the
best he has been guilty of to date. But -First Prin-
ciple had a field of 16 to contend with. He was
tiring at the end, and the crowd that had taken 7 to 4
felt glad the post was not another 50 yards away.

Wedding Day has been a gold-mine to
iSydney bookies. Since December 10 she has won
1 the Moorefield Flying, the Villiers and the Chal-
i lenge, starting each time at a long figure. Hardly
■moi'e than a dozen people backed her on Saturday,
‘and the profit to the bags was peautiful! When Ike
thinks of what might have been done to him if a
i punter with a long head and a bold heart had been
lout for scalps with this mare, he feels as if there
was a block of ice on his spine. Wedding Day is held
on lease.by her trainer, Mark Thompson, and a sport
j disguised as “A. Trevelyan.” Unless they make
, another arrangement the lady goes back to her owner
| next week. _ She was bred at the Wambo Stud, and
:is by Antonio (imp.) from Wedding Present (imp.)
Iby Trenton. A little dandy.
* * * *
Whatever may be the matter generally
there is no doubt about it that when lie is good
the Australian nag is good. Than First Principle,
a winner at Randwick last week, there is probably
no _ better weight-carrier, in the Commonwealth to-
day. Malstei- and Field” Rose nicked well tog-ether,
and the pair have- been responsible for the produc-
tion of several good gallopers.
Felix Booth, who is uncle of Sam Hordern
and other parties of that name, lately took to racing,
and for a new owner has had great success. On
Saturday, at the A.J.C. meeting, his nag Quarale was
sent out a red-hot favorite for the January Handicap,
one mile and a quarter, and duly landed the goods.
Quarale is a five-year-old, and is more than useful.
Within the last few months he has taken Menangle
Park Cup, a mile race at Randwick, and the event
he secured on Saturday. It might be mentioned that
Quarale helped jockey L. A. Walker to land three
winners during the day.
Should the owner of Satin Bird contem-
plate changing the colt’s name, Consistencv would
be an appropriate label. Satin Bird lias ran Run-
times on Randwick recently, and on each occasion has
been .placed. Consequently nobody begrudged him a
win in the A.J.C. Trial Stakes, in which he had to
be hard ridden to beat the much-boomed Carine, a
full sister to First Principle. Safin Bird is bv
Malster from Love Graft, and is owned bv T. Long*
worth, who races under the name of W. T. Nowlan,
and is father of Bill, the Australian champion swim-
mer, at present serving in France, and Dick, the
boy who caused a stir a week or two back by
swimming a hundred yards in something under the
minute.
* * * •*
“Stinger”: The scratching of La Steppe
for the Challenge Stakes on Saturday left a nasty
taste in the mouth of one big punter. After seeing
the mare work one day last week, this backer of
horses, who invests in three figures, planked a hatful
on her and proceeded to shake hands with himself on
what was coining. I am not saying that it was
because of this that La Steppe was eliminated, but
the people behind the mare would have been in-
finitely more popular had they relieved her of the
engagement earlier. When she appeared in the list of
acceptors two days before the race the small fry
went bbzzing round to do their bit. Tliev did it too
Through some bizarre twist in the skein,
the business of tote-installation in N. S. Wales is
in the hands of Minister for Public Instruction
James;' who turns lightly from problems connected
with bursaries and parsing to the job of fixing the
mechanical odds-merchant in position. Of course
James knows all about it, and when he went down
to the Speck the other day and saw some machines
being operated he came back with a story of the
simplicity of the business and how a man registered
the investments on an implement that seemed to
be composed of “revolving jam tins.’’ Well, the
revolving jam-tin tote agitated by hand may be
right enough for use at Bungamunga but would be
about as suitable to Sydney racecourses as a refresh-
ment-room stocked with a sandwich and two bottles
of lemonade. For the small country meetings an
implement can be delivered on the ground in a
cart, and three people can handle the investments and
everything else. At Randwick the provision has
to be made for accommodating the demands of a
crowd that, would knock the primitive apparatus
out of action in five minutes, and it must meet the
rush quickly and accurately. Further, it will have
to be on principles that are absolutely watertight,
for some queer things happen occasionally if they
are not. There is one point James ought not to
forget in drafting his regulations for use of the
machine-—that the Tote should be automatically
locked five minutes before the start of every race.
There are solemn reasons for this.
“Scot” : Of course it is preposterous for
the A.J.C. not to look after Ikey’s financial position.
If a citizen makes a bet on the Randwick course
with a licensed odds-merchant, be does so on an
implied contract with the A.J.C. that if he wins the
bag will pay. Something more than a moral obliga
tion rests on the club in this matter, and it strikes
me that if the fielders were as cute as they think
they are they would make the A.J.C. look after the
public interest. Another thing the club ought to
take notice of is the frequency with which certain
individuals profess to be “full” or to have “no money
for ’im” when invited to name a price for a par-
ticular horse. The excuse may be dinkum some-
times, of course. But sometimes it isn’t —and on
those occasions the people who license the operator
ought to be holding an inquest. The tote never
refuses cash because it knows something. It knows
nothing.
* * * *
“Mujik”: Racing results, the anxiety of
breeders to secure English sires, the purchase of
horses in the Cold Country, the opinions of trainers
and handicappers—all these form a mass of evidence
relating to the standard of thoroughbreds to which
there is no answer. The English horse is the best and
there is no get-away from it. But what about it?
Obviously it is merely futile to penalise the English
horse in races and still more foolish to shut him out.
that would only leave the local nag going from
bad to worse. There must be a cause for the deterio-
ration of the Australian horse, and it should be the
business of someone or other to get down to the
roots of it. Meanwhile one certain thing is that the
falling away in the quality of the racehorse is being
followed by the production of a poor crop of ordinary
mokes. Horses that have lost any vitality they ever
had——weeds, squibs and rogues—are being bought up
every year for use as station stallions. ‘ The defect,
wherever it may be, is far reaching and raises ques-
tions coming well within the region of Parliamentary
action. If breeders will do nothing the politicians
will have to do something; for what is happening
to the Australian horse is a shame. There is a
national issue at stake here, and in most other coun-
tries it is taken notice of.
* * * »
The Maoriland mare Desert Gold goes on
her conquering way and seems to like it more than
ever. The Waterloo Stakes at Wellington were won
on Saturday, and the lady’s brother, Egypt, took
the second-money, just to keep the cash in the
iamily. Owner Lowry’s anxiety to let the mare go
through without a beating will, it is understood,
keep him from offering a trip to Australia in the
autumn. Of course, there are any number of Maori-
landers who reckon Desert Gold would clean up Aus-
tarlia’s best easily. Perhaps she would. But Lowry
ought to know fairly well, and it looks as if he
reckoned there are risks.
* * * *
Henry Gaisford, who died the other
day, had a long - and honorable connection with the
turf _ in Maoriland—a quiet and unostentatious con-
nection, too, though he was a man of much wealth.
He was one of the old-time Ilawkes Bay squatters
and the king-pin of the Waipukurau Racing Club,
which in its day was some pumpkins. To win the
Waipukurau Handicap was the great ambition of
the wool-kings thereabouts, and every year a nag
was reserved by big racing men like Ormond and
Russell and old “Bill” Douglas and Spencer Gollan
to g’o in pursuit of a prize thev held in as much
esteem as the C.J.C. Derby. But the glories of
Waipukurau have departed. Gaisford was a winner
there many a time with horses of his own breeding,
but the later race of punters knew him not, for his
ways were not theirs. And perhaps at bottom he
derived more enjoyment from a hunt than a race
meeting, and would as soon have bred a prize ram
as a Cup winner. He was 75.
* * * *
The American boxer Coyle, having
knocked his innards out of tune with ice-creams,,
had to lay up for repairs instead of joining Tommy
Uren in a duet at Sydney Stadium on Saturday.
Fritz Holland filled the breach at a catehweight con-
test, and after a scuffling, clinching disturbance got
the verdict; whereon Arthur Scott, the referee, was
solemnly upbraided for. making a bloomer. Well,
this page doesn’t know about that. If Uren didn’t
deserve to lose he certainly did not deserve to win.
It was a poor display altogether, but as the thing
went Fritz showed the greater skill. It strikes the
writer that Uren will have to brush up a bit, or he
won’t hold those championships very long- unless a
moratorium happens.
It was Harry Stone and Fred Kay at the
Stadium by the Yarra on Saturday. These two make
a weird mixture. Henry is the better jumper and
the more willing puncher, but Frederick the Awk-
ward is the better boxer, and when peace was
declared by effluxion of time the long ’un was blessed
by the referee.
Bobby Graham, late amateur flyweight
champion of Bananaland, and George Taylor’s little
brother Jimmy were let loose at Brisbane on Satur-
day with instructions to stiffen one another. The
disaster was put off; but they presented 20 rounds
of high-quality baulk, biff and back-away. Father
Marre couldn't decide either way. And in that he
wasn't far out.
Jack Johnson is down on his uppers at
last—in Spain—and according to cabled reports will
“probably become a bull-fighter.” If the obese darky
stays where he is lie is more likely to become the
menial who enters the ring after the toreador has
slain the infuriated he-cow, and seizing the carcase
by its limp, unrebellious tail, drags the meat away
'towards the local restaurant. Johnson bereft of his
golden teeth, his motor and swank and punch, dream-
ing of his concubines and their diamonds, and pull-
ing defunct bulls about by the rudder, would seem
to be a living reminder of what can happen when
all that glitters has been sold.

COURT OAKES, Resident Champion of
Tasmania.
Carfax” : Excuse me, I didn’t sing a song
about walking 40 miles, as alleged by “Wal” (B.
18/1/T7). 1 merely remarked that to do the journey
by boot in a day is a big thing. And I say it
again—in spite of this person’s blither about the
“10,710 Billjims in N. S. Wales who could trot over
40 miles in 24 hours on about two tills of the
pipe.” Some men can do a 40 miles’ journey on
foot in 24 hours, I know, but the number is limited
to a few very exceptional individuals, and the num-
ber of times they have done it is mighty small.
Vague yarns prove nothing. As for the old party
who crossed the South Island of M.L. from coast
to coast in 24 days and on the afternoon of arrival
swept the programme at a sports meeting, all of
which is narrated by “S.R.” (B. 18/1/’l7), permit
me to salute this valiant romancer. In the early
’7o’s it took Cassidy’s coaches three days to go
from Hokitika to Christchurch. The journey is in
part over high, broken country, and in those times
there were many unbridged rivers to ford. But this
chap did the 167 miles per boot in 21 days, “re-
freshed himself with a swim in the lagoon,” and then
annexed the mile run and all the rest of it. You
bet your life he did. A man who after training for
the job could on a prepared track, under cover, break
10 hours for 65 miles would be a world’s champion.
The Chch-Hokitika journey knocks that into a cocked
hat. But, as 1 said before, I don’t want to make
nasty remarks about anyone.
“Vic”: The talk about trips per boot
reminds me. I saw a mob of the London-Scottisli
Territorials (about 100) start on a walk to Brigh-
ton from London one Saturday night a few months
before war was declared. They left the metropolis
at 7 p.m. and arrived in Brighton, 52J miles away,
at 10 a.m. They were 17 hours on the road. Not
one of the mob had fallen out. The walk was
undertaken to beat the record of a Rifle Regiment.
men had trained only in their spare time.
Most of them sweated in city banks and offices
during the week.
“Cleiclo”: The remarks of “Spot” (B.
11/1/’l7) about physical-culture in its relation to
the training of athletes in general and pugs in
particular suggest that he is talking through his
hat. I have yet to hear of any proved system of
•training that does not embrace physical-culture.
Intelligently indulged in, it makes the weak man
strong, and the strong man stronger. Likewise the
slow man quick and the quick man quicker. To
boxers it increases the defensive fibrous tissues,
notably the muscles, which conceal the downstairs
and commissariat departments. It is safe to give a
written guarantee, with your life as forfeit, that
physical-culture will improve the strength, quick-
ness, punching power and endurance of any devotee
of the slugging game. Tf “Spot” modifies his asser-
tions and says that certain branches of physical-
culture are not advisable for an athlete requiring a
high degree of speed, he will be nearer the truth.
Swimmers generally are not in need of additional
exercises, although in the “off” season any time and
trouble expended on the development of those large
back muscles that bulge out under the arms will be
well repaid by increased swimming strength. Finally
there is no need to wait until the war is over to
hear what Georges Carpentier has to say. He has
already said it in his book.
“M.P.A.”: Some sound commonsense is
dealt by “Scot” (8.. 18/1/’l7) about training
methods and the ruin of young athletes by lunatic
theories and rule-of-thumb methods. In a good
many years of practical experience amongst athletes
of all classes—peds., boxers, wrestlers, swimmers,
cyclists, weight-lifters and ’bailers —I have been
firmly convinced that 90 per cent, of the so-called
trainers of athletes are mere rubbers-down. I have
watched men who knew no more of anatomy than of
Sanskrit directing the operations of young athletes in
training. It matters not whether a man is a ped.,
cyclist or swimmer, it is absolutely necessary that he
should be treated according to his build and tem-
perament. A trainer should be to an athlete what a
doctor is to a patient. If a trainer has three or four
men under his care, each man should be
a case and be given the work suited to his
build, just as the medico treats several persons
according to their different complaints. I was aston-
ished on one occasion to read in the Melbourne Argus
a column telling a schoolboy how to train for a foot-
race. The fatal mistake in handling schoolboys is to
train them at all. They do not require training, in
the sense that athletes understand the term. They
merely need practice under the careful eye of an
experienced sports master. If a young athlete has
no “athletic father” to guide him, so to speak, he
is invariably at the mercy of a lot of mugs, or he
may catch the eye of an artful old dodger who finds
him useful as a “try-out horse.” If he be left alone
to follow his own inclinations on the track he often
gets carried away by his feelings at the time, and
overdoes the work. It is so easy to over-do one’s
training when one feels in the real pink of condition.
At that stage the advice of the wise-head is most
essential. Take, for instance, the different types of
runners. Dad Wheatley, a thin slab of a ped. and a
great natural runner to boot, was defeated in his
day by Dr. Harvey Sutton, a stout athlete of quite
13st. The pair met in an Australian championship
over half- and one-mile flat. Sutton won one race
from the back and t’other from the front. Sutton
required work to get right. Wheatley just pottered
about. If Wheatley and Sutton had prepared on
rule-of-thumb lines, Sutton would have won, no
doubt, but Wheatley would have broken down. The
success of Yank athletes in all branches of athletics
is that competent coaches direct operations and each
trainee is a special ease. Lots of theories have been
advanced regarding the ways and means of getting
into form, but nothing outside the principle of run-
ning for runners, swimming for swimmers, boxing for
boxers is any good. Men like Geo. Hackenschmidt
lift an odd weight or two for arm work, but wrestle
’emselves into nick. For any training in the world
walking will fit in admirably. It is the one exercise
that meets every case.
* ■* * *
Ward Prentice’s scoop of five wickets for
VO against N. Sydney on Saturday established his -
claim to be styled an all-rounder. Years ago he was
one of Ma State’s finest wicket-keepers; later, he
specialised in batting; nowadays he reaps his harvest
with the ball. Prentice will fill Carter's place in
the Anniversary Day patriotic match at Sydney this
week-end. In the cold weather, Prentice is addicted
to Rugby, and tripped away with the “Wallabies”
in 1908-9.
* * # *
A youngster with an abnormal appetite
for wickets in Sydney premiership cricket is F.
Dodds, of Paddington 111., who in a recent match
against Petersham IV. took 14 for 16 runs!
* * * #
Eighteen-year-old Down, who plays for
Carlton, is coming on and looks like turning into
the kind of '"■>ckcter Vic. has been limiting for. On
Saturday against ’Roy he amassed 166 in three hours,
and was going strong when stumps were drawn. He
smote 15 fours and gave no chances. He is not the
accomplished performer that Trumper was at 18, but
he has a fine variety of strokes to make good with.
There is a young bowler coming on, too, in the next
paddock—Hood, of Melbourne. He got four St.
Hilda wickets on Saturday—all clean bowled.
* *• * * "
“Over” : Cricketer Charley Lawrence, who
died the other day, came to this country in 1862
as a member of Stephenson's first All England XT
and stayed here as a cricket coach ; later he was a
railway employee, and finally, when 60 years old,
again took on the job of coach, this time to the
M.C.C. Charley was the only white man in the
alio, team which went to the Bull country in 1868.
The team was made up of the Binghi cricketing
talent in the Glenelg, Oasterton and Coleraine dis-
tricts, and contained at least two notable performers
—Mullagli and Cuzin. Mullagh was an all-round
cricketer, Ids chief defect being typical Binghi lazi-
ness. He reduced the making of boundary hits to
a fine art, chiefly, it is said, because he did not have
to run for them. During the tour Lawrence had his
hands full. Although Binghi was sent to bed early
every night to save him from Bull hospitality, he
mostly escaped by the window' and the nearest water-
pipe, and Charley w'as kept busy rounding him up
or disentangling him from trouble. Tw'O black
brothers—Sundown and Jim Crow —had to be inva-
lided home, and King Cole died during the tour.
Fanny Durack’s habit of .annexing cham-
pionships in the water is a complaint that only old
age seems likely to cure. In the 150yds. State
Championship, decided at Sydney Municipal Baths on
Saturday, Fanny remained with the field for 50yds.;
then, ringing up “full speed ahead,” she turned the
event into a procession. She finished in lmin. 56sec.,
and the next lady knocked at the door ssec. after-
wards.
* * * *
Walter Bethel, who fathers the scheme
which recently utilised the school vacation to push
Sydney, kids through the elementary stages of swim-
ming, is a lively stalwart of 53. For more than 20
years he has been a figure in Sydney swimming. As
adviser and instructor he helped the wonderful
Barney Kieran towards the top. Harold Hardwick,
Oscar Dickman and a host of others who assisted in
the recent scheme reckon that 1000 youngsters have
been added to the army of swimmers.

This is an English co. which had a bad
time 12 or 15 years back and has been rather
nervous about Australia ever since. And the
nervousness increased prior to 1910 when
the Federal Labor party talked so valiantly
that the co. decided to look totvards S.
America. It set up an establishment at
Buenos Aires; but, though it no doubt had
visions once of shifting the bulk of its
interests that way, it is probably more in-
dined than ever to put up with Australia.
The Australian devil has a habit of lashing
his tail in the face of the capitalist and
roaring that it proposes to eat him. The
foreign devil, who hasn’t, yet got the capital-
ist in his power, more cunningly hides his
tail for a while and doesn’t mention any-
thing about his appetite.
The co. was particularly nervous about
the Federal land tax. There was a lot of
talk about bursting up large estates, and
the co. seems to have got the idea that big
stations would have to be cut up and sold
at a pound or so an acre. As a matter of
fact, the Land Tax didn’t reduce the price
of farms, largely, no doubt, because the farm
mostly isn’t taxable. Further, as land within
the agricultural area has been cut up, a
keener demand has set in for grazing country
outside; and that must have suited the
A.M.L. & F., which has always been heavily
interested in the dry country—a fact that
explains the bumpiness of the profits column.
However, taking it all round, though the
co. has suffered now and then a slap in the
eye, it has done remarkably well. Its worst
time was in 1902-3-4, and the table opens
with one of the only two blanks in the dividend
record. For the rest of its life of 5G years
it has more often paid 20 p.c. than anything
else. Its great advantage, apart from the
size of its reserve, which is within a few
pounds of the capital, is that by far the
greater part of its borrowed money is long-
term debenture stock, £1,000,000 carrying 4
p.c. and £lOO,OOO 3 p.c. Any well-managed
co. could make money by lending money on
pastoral securities in Australia so long as
it wasn’t liable to have to suddenly repay
the principal. No co., no matter how well
managed, could escape smash, except by a
miracle, if it lent on that kind of security
money which was liable to be called for in
a time, say, of drought.
In 1914-15 the co. had a dry time, and
last year’s profits look smaller than usual.
But the year’s record was better than it
seems on a mere comparison of profits. First,
the co.’s securities are better. Assets, apart
from advances and properties, were
£1,058,482 as at June, 1915; 12 months
later, by getting in advances, etc., the more
or less "liquid assets had been increased to
£1,379.137, and liabilities, apart from the
debentures, had been reduced from £408,660
to £394,926. Considering the nature and
extent of the assets, the floating debt is
small; and, though there will be the same
old ups and downs, the co.’s prospects con-
tinue rosy. Profits will, .of course, be cut
into by war taxes, for a few years. Last
year (and this,partly explains the shrinkage
in profits) income taxes in England and
elsewhere totalled £46,183 ; in 1915, £34,953 ;
in happy, ignorant 1910 they were only
£7534.
* * * *
There has been talk about a thermo-
electric co. that proposes to treat wolfram
ore from or in N. Queensland. It is high
time Australia did produce some tungsten-
steel products. At present, if steel of that kind
or ferro-tungsten is required, it has to be
imported from Scandinavia ; and a striking
feature is that J. C. Moulden, an Australian,
is consulting engineer to one of the biggest
of these Scandinavian thermo-electric cos.
Australia has the brains and the material,
and yet it doesn’t do the work. What’s
wrong? Is capital too tired, or labor too
hungry and uncertain?
* * * *
The Speck’s latest notion is to put a
hydro-electric power-station in the King
River Gorge, about the most picturesque bit
of wild scenery on the West Coast. The King
has cut through the coast range a canyon
several miles long and in places over 1000
feet deep, all boiling water and cliffs of
black rock, with trees and bushes and
creepers growing in every crack and cranny.
But now it is reckoned that there is a good
site for damming back the water from a
watershed of over 200 square miles, with a
rainfall of anything over- 100 inches, and
that something like 20,000 (some say 40,000)
horse-power can be got there to run the
electrolytic process for treating the Her-
cules-Rosebery ores. 8o the gorge is likely
to be tamed.
Alexander Macdonald seems to have got
hold of something extra in the Kitchener
molybdenite mine, North Queensland. At
70ft. a drive has been put 130 ft. in ore; and
throughout all the development workings
“the bodv has averaged sft., with values
varying from 2 p.c. to 8 p.c. In no case has
a blank been encountered.” A battery and
flotation plant has been installed. The start
wasn’t much of a success, nevertheless in a
brief run 400 tons put through gave six tons
18cwt. concentrates, 93 p.c. pure, and it is
alleged a clean tailing has now been achieved-

Within a garden’s green
Near melbourne town,
An elderly man was
Foully struck down
Upon the gravel walk
Where he’d fell with a thud,
Outstretched he lay
Bespattered with blood.
For robbery his grim
And treacherous foe
Had waited and watched
To strike the blow.
Within the doleful morgue
A mother and son
Mourned by the slab of
The murdered one.

“OThimerty’s” fragment of history :
The most interesting part of the diary of “Bobby”
Knopwood, first chaplain in Vandemonia, who came
with Collins in 1803, is that which relates the stay
of the ship Calcutta at Rio de Janeiro. She had
been knocked about in a storm, and the expedition
was held up for three weeks while she refitted. Knop-
wood didn’t mind. He made many friends ashore,
especially a young lady boarder, Antonia Januaria
by name, who was being instructed at the Convent
de Ajuda. Her only regret was that he was a heretic.
Knopwood’s . entry, for Sunday, July 10, states
that he attended Mass in the morning, and then
visited “the charming Antonia.” He dined at the
hotel, and saw 4000 negroes dance in the afternoon.
In the evening he went to the theatre to see a play
called “The Poor Soldier.” The last Rio entry in
his diary is mournful: “Visited the convent for the
last time. Saw Antonia at five, and we took leave
of one another with regret. Vale;”- Knopwood held
the chaplaincy for 20 years and winked most of the
time. He was succeeded by William P>edford, and
then the winking stopped. Bedford’s heaviest guns were
turned against the highest officials; and as a result
of the bombardment Governor Arthur issued an order
that the officials should marry the women who were
the mothers of their children or take the sack. Chap-
lain Bedford was very busy for a while reading the
marriage service for men who cursed him all the
Way from “dearly beloved” to the benediction.

Encouraged by the fact that many resi-
dents in the northern-coastal towns use
sponges washed in from the Reef, the culti-
vation of the sea-plant on scientific lines is
being considered. It is estimated that
sheepswool sponges, which take four years to
mature, would yield £ll6 per acre, and that
the cost of planting, harvesting and market-
ing is £4; and the small reef sponges, in
spite of their lower market value, give an
even better return, because they mature m
12 or 14 months.

IN BANANA LAND
“Outback”: Though Clermont has had
many floods those of 1870 and December,
1916, stand in a class by themselves, so
far as modern times go. But Binghi remem-
bers another. Andrew Small, one of the
oldest residents of the Peak Downs, .told me
that he gave a hand to build the Clermont
Hotel, the first groggery in the township. This
was started in October, 1862 ; part of it is
now the kitchen of the Bank of N. S. Wales.
When Andrew was assisting to put in a
wall plate, an abo. remarked, “White pfeller
plurry fool,” and showed Small and John
Winters, owner of the pub, a flood-mark on
a tree on Sandy Creek, where he had seen
the water when a boy. The levels were run
out by Small and Winters, and the pub was
feet under flood mark. In the last flood it
was the terrific cross-currents which did the
damage. The depth of water was not ter-
rific ; at any part of the flat there was
never more than 12ft.
According to his own admission Queens-
land Government Printer Gumming has
broken out in an unexpected place by de-
veloping a fiery enthusiasm in the cause of
the Y.M.C.A. This is the manner of his
circular appeal for £21,000 for the Associa-
tion’s funds: —
The best fellows that ever drew the breath of life
have gone from this country to belt blazes out of the
bully of the Central Empires. When a soldier gets
time to think he gets a bit homesick. He would be
having a rotten time if it were not for a good .pal
that is standing to him day and night. I am talking
about the Y.M.C.A. It seems a bit strange for my
name to be mixed up with the Y.M.C.A., but this
institution has broken down all my prejudices, and
to-day I take my hat off to it as the finest thing in
God’s earth.
Why it should be a bit strange for Gumming
to be mixed up with the Y.M.C.A. is left to
conjecture. But if he can rake in £21,000
the Y.M.C.A. will have quite excusably
become mixed up with Gumming.
“McNorth” : The latest report of the Chief
Protector of Aborigines contains a casual
statement to the effect that there has been
an outbreak of whooping-cough at Morn-
'ington Island. Mornington is a remote
speck in the Gulf of Carpentaria, and was
the only remaining terra incognita in the
North until a year or two ago, when a
mission station was established there. Since
then introduced disease is making ravages
amongst the natives. Under existing condi-
tions the fate of the islanders is as certain
as the setting of the sun. They are doomed.
But no one bothers. The heads of Depart-
ments seem to enthusiastically pursue a
policy of letting things slide and allow mis-
sionaries (some of them Germans, by the
way) to work on whatever lines .they
choose. The result is disastrous. The
Torres Strait Islands are the only places
where any good has been done, and the man
chiefly responsible for their progress, W. M.
Lee-Bryce, P.M., was unthinking enough to
die the other day.
Bananaland is again (hie) to the fore.
Under the amended financial agreement
between Commonwealth and States it is pro-
vided that, while South Aus. is to be con-
tent with £2,660,000 of loan money, and
Victoria must not exceed £1,552,000, Queens-
land is to have (hie) £2,910,000. True.
£400,000 of this is for flood-relief purposes;
hut under the old agreement Bananaland
claimed the same proud pre-eminence. North-
ern Labor is still on a financial jamboree,
and simply will not be denied its beer money.
Passed out at Gympie a few days ago,
William G. King, a Bananalander who had
done more than his share of roaming. From
Queensland scrubs he went to Sydney, thence
to the Big Smoke, and, later, with the 7th
Hussars to South Africa. His next move
was into the Bechuanaland police, which
patrolled the country towards Bulawayo.
In ’9B he casually called in on Bananaland
again, only to amble Boerwards once more
on the outbreak of the war. A spell of
mining followed, and, finally, lie lapsed into
the uneventful pleasantness of Queensland
fruit-farming. He missed the half-century
by three years.
PROPRIETY.
MISS JONES : “I hope there’s no one looking, Mr. Brown.”
THE AMOROUS MR. BROWN : “Not a soul.”
MISS JONES : “Because you ore exceedingly rude if there is."
Jacob Stumm, the Lillev Liberal M.H.R.,
put his case lucidly and pithily at a recent
recruiting conference, and it is good enough
to go anywhere. Stumm has? been 60 years a
Queenslander, having arrived as a mere
baby. His father was naturalised. Out of
10 members of the Stumm family born in
Queensland seven are doing their bit in
camp or field and one has been killed in
action. The mother of the young Stumms
came of a Scottish family, a fact that did
not prevent her son being challenged when
he recorded his Australian vote in the
recent referendum.
“Itan” : Bill Hamilton has no time for go-
slowers. Not long ago it was evident that
the stall’ opening up a State coal-mine for
him was afflicted with lazy itch. He sent
for- the foreman, who admitted it. Hamil-
ton sacked the lot. Then he put another
man to captain the job, and told him what
would he his fate if the loafing element got
on top again. The foreman said he under-
stood. The next thing the Minister heard
was that the foreman the first day had four
fights and won them, and that everything
was! now going' swimmingly.
“F. 8.” : A truly wise man was Archbishop
Dunne, just deceased, I well remember
how, when he was parish priest of Too-
woomba. he devoted himself heart and soul
to settling people on the land. The good
“soggarth” used to maintain that while
church, convent and schools were all healthy
for folk; it was the home that made a church
and a nation. There must be many scores
of families on the Downs to-day who owe
their homesteads to the aid of the good old
cleric.
“Coolibah” on Strawberry’s toilet: —
Queensland Agricultural Department should dis-
seminate a little knowledge on the subject of cattle
dipping. Most of us who are breeding stock within
the old tick-infested coastal areas have acquired a
great deal of knowledge by the old and expensive
method of paying for our mistakes; but the recent
holocaust at Chinchilla, where 400 head went up
in smoke after being over-dipped, and the more
recent occurrence in the Nanango district, where
22G forward stores were destroyed, seem to suggest
that cattle-owners on the verge of the infested area,
and further out, have much to learn. I have no
particulars concerning the Chinchilla case, but it is
safe to say that the trouble was brought apout by
giving the same stock two dippings within, say, two
or three days. At Nanango the bullocks were dipped,
driven 35 miles in two days, dipped again at the
conclusion of the journey, and a few days later were
dying in great agony. The hides of beasts dying
in this way are useless, being as hard as deal and
cracked and fissured right across in great open seams.
The poor brutes usually lose their sight about the
second day after their maltreatment. Dipping
cattle, even in a solution of the ordinary strength
required for tick destruction, produces a degree of
cutaneous irritation that leaves the hide in a con-
dition unfitting it to withstand the effects of a
further immersion. This condition of sensitiveness
is at its height on the second or third day, and
any further application of the poisonous fluid about
that time will always be attended with very bad
results. Years ago, when stockowners were gaining
their knowledge by experimenting with dipping, ino-
culation, etc., a station near Bundaberg killed 80
cows by this method. I lost a whole - platoon of
milkers in the same way, but managed to nurse a
few back to active service at the bucket by simply
washing them in crude castor-oil. Curiously, two or
three immersions in the dip within a few hours does
not appear to be attended with any bad results. An
old bull of mine, who is a noted gate and rail
artist, goes through the dip on dipping day with
every mob of cows that he sees approaching the
yards. He thus frequently gets three or four baths
in as many hours without in any way impairing his
ability as a hurdler.
Ashes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Come near to me, O Rose of all the W orld!
Thy satin lip, like flow’ret half uncurled,
Press close and closer yet to this grim mouth.
Mayhap that crimson bud will quench the
drouth,
The yearning and the wild ecstatic pain,
The agony of loss, the longing vain
For her who nevermore will kiss again!
They call thee Chalice of Delight, ’tis said.
Canst help me to forget? For she is dead!
Dead are the laughing eyes, the dancing feet.;
Cold is the heart which once to mine could
beat.
For me alone Love woke within her eyes.
Each day with you a new Love wakes and dies.
Sold are your kisses, and your vows are lies.
O woman, can thy soft hands’ flutt’ring touch
Bring heartsease to a fool who loved too
much ?
Above her head the drifting rose-leaves fall,
Hiding her earth-bed with a scented pall.
Above our heads bloom other roses red,
Shedding their fragrance o’er our silken bed.
Press close, hot lips! I would forget my dead.
QUEENSLAND.
E.S.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, January 20, 1917.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
This afternoon Premier and Mrs. Ryan
boarded the Lucinda and entertained dele-
gates to the A.W.U. and (take this lot
slowly) A.M.I.E.U. conference on the river.
Rain did its best to spoil the outing, but
nobody complained of a little thing like
that in view of the set-off.
Brigadier-General Lee, District Com-
mandant, and Mrs. Lee will soon leave this
State to take up their residence in X. S.
Wales, where the old Light Horseman will
succeed Brigadier-General Ramaeiotti as
Commandant. Such a popular couple can
have only one farewell —R.I.P. No, no, my
dear; it means Return If Possible.
George Woolnough, the late editor of the
Brisbane Telegraph, farewelled the staff
that lie is bequeathing to Dr. Fred Ward
at a dinner last Tuesday night. A photo-
graph of the bequest, autographed by the
items, was handed to the departing chief by
.1. R. H. Lewis, who for many years has
swung the sub.’s blue pencil with firmness
in his hand and remorse in his heart.
The other day Bernard Canniffe, a well-
known accountant, dragged two small boys
from the Brisbane River and returned them,
damp but otherwise undamaged, to their
agitated parents. No, thanks; much
obliged, but he wouldn’t mention his name.
However, somebody had recognised him—no
man can do a good deed in this world and
escape detection —and then there was the
fuss that the rescuer had hoped to avoid.
Sister T. J. Dunne, who is well known
in and around Nerang, has been made
matron of a war hospital at the British
Deccan, Poona (India). Her nursing staff
is 50 strong.
Another Queensland nurse who has been
distinguishing herself is Sister Constance
Keys, who has been awarded a Royal Mili-
tary Cross of the second order. She left
Australia in September, 1914, with the first
lot of Bananalanders, and on the voyage to
Egypt attended to some of the wounded
from the Emden. Her next lot of patients
were Anzacs. After that she came home on
a transport carrying wounded, returned to
Egypt again, and was made a sub-matron.
Young Sylvester Browne, who got his
commission a short time ago in the Irish
Guards, and is now with his regiment in the
trenches, is the youngest son of the late
Sylvester—the big fellow who amused him-
self by capturing bushrangers in the long
ago. The remains of the old stockyard on
Dillalah station are still standing, close to
the spot where he performed his last feat of
the kind.
E. S. Emerson hasn’t fotgotten the way
to turn out pretty bird-songk. You will re-
member those pleasant fanqi,es he embodied
(accompanied by Norman Lindsay decora-
tions) in “An Australian Bird Calendar.’
Round about his cottage at Zillmere num-
bers of the beautiful blood hloney-eaters were
visitors in the Spring. These sprites “‘Milky
White” introduced in a lyrip; and, to a set-
ting by S. 11. Pittman 250 girls of the
Kangaroo Point school warbled it recently.
’Twas an animated, tuneful performance.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

STUDEBAKER WINS AGAIN!
THis time it is the Australian Speed Records, made on Christmas and Boxing Days-
Half mile in 21| seconds
Mile in 44 seconds
attaining' a speed of nearly 85 miles per Hour.
The winning car was driven by Alec Jewell on Southport Beach, Queensland.
It is a stock Studebaker “6” chassis, the only addition being a crown wheel
and pinion made in the C.C.M. workshop to raise the gear ratio. Racing
parts, including aluminium pistons, were ordered from America for the occa-
sion but failed to arrive. Locally-manufactured aluminium pistons proved
unequal to the strain, so the sturdy car broke the records on her own parts,
which makes the performance the more remarkable. No shock absorbers
were fitted ; this alone makes the record almost unique, as it is doubtful whether,
in any race, a car has ever attained the speed of 85 miles without them.

fine cotton threads. Fibre and rubber alternate so that, in the complete
cord, no two strands touch.
Woven canvas, the tyre reinforcement of all Tyres
hut Palmers, lacks “life.” Itsetsup the internal

The cords run transversely across the tread in two
layers. They form a reinforcement which
restrains the air pressure evenly and without heat.
palmer Cord Tyres are made in all sizes for Heavy and Light Cars, Cyclecars, Motor Cyles, Auto-
Wheels and Aeroplanes.

“Y.E.”: Jack Loudon had leanings to-
ward the stage, although he never got as
far as dramatising any of his own works.
Others, however, have seen the possibilities.
Recently in London one of his shorter
stories was dramatised under the title “A
Fine Night’s Work” and produced with un-
qualified success. London, by the way, gave
a film company a big scoop three days before
his death by allowing it to take a picture of
his home life, revealing charming glimpses
into the character of the hale, big-hearted
fellow of God’s great outdoors.
“Up Stage”: This is fame! Homeward
bound the other night I introduced Bert
Bailey to a commercial, who, although in-
different to the theatre, complimented the
actor upon his success as Dad in the "Sign
of the Cross.” Reminds me. Years back,
while playing a Sydney season, Bland Holt
wired his mechanist in Melbourne to send
over the scenery of “White Heather.” As
they were loading it at the wharf a dear old
gentleman with a profusion of whiskers and
brass buttons inquired the destination. “Syd-
ney, to Bland Holt,” was the answer. “Dear
me! And how much is there?” the ancient
asked. “Fifty tons.” “Dear me, remark-
able!” He was still more surprised when
informed that nearly half as much again was
used in some productions. “Well, well, you
astonish me! I thought they could carry
enough under their arms for their concerts.
Fancy a Band of Hope using all those
screens!”
“Hohoho”: The obvious explanation of
the pathetic series of foreign imported
dramas in Australia is given -by Clayton
Hamilton, a well-known American critic.
These crook melodramas or cranky farces
are not even representative of the foreign
country from which they come; they are
tried before an unrepresentative audience, and
by the verdict of that audience they become
successes or failures in the U.S.A. That
foreign audience wdiich settles our dramatic
taste for us is the Broadway audience;
and the Broadway is “a region in
which real people do not live, and
cannot live, because it is lighted only by
electric lamps instead of by the sun and
moon and stars. America is not made of
such people, and neither is New York ; but
our theatre, for the most part, is deliberately
edited for that infinitesimal proportion of
our general population that patronises flashy
lobster-palaces and noisy cabarets.” As
the cultured people who patronise concerts
and art exhibitions have long given up going
to the Broadway theatres, the destiny of
the entire theatre in America (and, of course,
its theatrical suburb called Australia) is
determined and controlled by the success or
failure of the plays that are thrown before
the flashy lobster-eaters. The U.S. press
takes its cue from tliis Broadway mob, and
our Australian managers dutifully follow the
noisy crowd. Come to think of it, there is
a flashy-lobster flavor about most of this
foreign nroduet. Perhaps they would prove
palatable to Australians if they were given
away with a cray.
“S. Snell” : In response to the irresistible
force behind the law of buying in the cheap-
est market and selling in the dearest, I can
see the whole of the moving picture business
going over to China. Digest this from an
interview with a happy manager running a
film business in the valley of the Pe-hi-ho:
“We have a Chinese Charlie Chaplin. Ilis
name is Chau, and he is a wonderful acrobat.
He is the only Chinese slap stick comedian
we have ever got them to stand for. He
gets £2 10s. a month. It is so much money
that other actors refuse to believe him.”
Think of the cinematographic possibilities
of a country where you can get a Charlie
Chaplin for 12s. Gd. a week! There is in-
deed <a theatrical managers’ Celestial Empire.
They go to China when they die.
“Norbell” : Gagging often saves the situa-
tion. Years back, when Dampier put on
“Monte Cristo” at the Alex., Melbourne, the
cue went in vain the first night for the “big”
entrance of the millionaire Count. An
agonised wail came from the prompt corner:
“Keep it going, the old man’s round at the
front!” Without the flicker of an eyelid
the late Charles Field took centre and waded
in : “As I came up the long avenue of janes
but now I saw an aghd man, with arms
crossed on his breast, pacing sadly to and
fro in the moonlight. Surely, I thought”—
and so on by the yard. Presently the reas-
suring voice of the prompter whispered:
“Cut it short.- He’s ready now!” Field
gracefully brought his impromptu speech to
an end with a melodramatic flourish, and
got a round of applause from a crowded
house. Then the Count of Monte Cristo
entered, and everything proceeded swim-
mingly. I asked Field afterwards how he
did it. He put it down to years of experi-
ence in the penny gaffs of the East End of
London. There, he said, they talked over
the plot of the new thriller in the morning
and found their own dialogue at night.
“8.J.”: General Smith-Dorrien’s cam-
paign in favor of stage purity and neck-to-
ankle costumes for revue girls has fallen
fiat. One of the warrior’s latest yelps was to
the effect that a scene in “Air. Wu,” remem-
bered here as a rather dull Chowdrama, was
“absolutely immoral.” A London Sunday
paper sent a reply-paid wire to 11. AI. Ver-
non. the author, asking him to say something.
“Why should I be the only person in Eng-
land to pay any attention to an old gentle-
man with a bee in his bonnet?” was Vernon’s
answering wire, me old gentleman hasn’t
been taken too seriouslv as a reformer since,
at Aldershot a few years back, he started a
strenuous campaign against the degrading
cigarette, which was sapping the moral fibre
of the British army, or words to that effect.

Government Notices.
COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 20th January, 1917.
Volunteers for the Manufacture of
Munitions in Great Britain.
THE Government of the Commonwealth has decided !
to send further men to England, with a view |
of assisting the Empire in the production of Muni-
tions.
The men will he chosen front those engaged in
the following trades: Jig and Tool Draftsmen and
Toolmakers, Toolsetters, Fitters (general,, erecting,
bench), Turners, Coppersmiths and Shipworkers.
Those engaged in the following shipworking trades
will be considered: Boilermakers, Platers, Riveters,
Angle Smiths, Caulkers, Drillers, Boilermakers’
Assistants, Shipwrights, Joiners, Oxy-acetylene
Cutters, Plumbers and Blacksmiths.
Passages will be provided to and from England m
transports or such other vessels as may be determined
by the Minister for Defence. Allowances’ of Five
pounds ( £5) will be made to each man for expenses
on the voyage each way. Such men as are married
or are the sole support of mother or family will
be granted in addition an allowance as follows: For
the first eight weeks after embarkation, at the rate
of Twenty-five shillings (255.) per week and Two
shillings and sixpence (2s. 6d.) per week for each
child under the age of sixteen years; and thereafter
Twenty shillings (205.) per week and two shillings !
(25.) per week for each child under the age of
sixteen years, until the date of their landing on their
return to Australia; these allowances are conditional
on their families being and remaining resident in
Australia. A subsistence allowance of ss. per day
is being paid by the Board of Trade, from day of
landing in England till day of commencing duty.
Railway expenses incurred _in travelling from
countrv districts to capital cities or other distant
centres will be refunded when such journeys are
undertaken at tlie direction of State Munitions Com-
mittees for interview purposes only, but no other
out of pocket expenses will be allowed to applicants
prior to embarkation.
To cover the risks of the voyage the provisions of
the Commonwealth Seamen’s Compensation Act will
be applied by Government to volunteers accepted
under this scheme for munitions Work in England.
Passports will be provided free to accepted appli-
cants.
Selected applicants will be required to undergo a
medical examination before final acceptance.
The men selected are to agree that they will accept
duty wherever placed, and will diligently render
every nossible service in pursuance of the purpose
for which they are to be sent; they will be subject
in England to the direction and instructions of the
Ministry of Munitions or officer authorised by the
Australian Government.
The conditions under which the men will work
and the rates of wages to be paid in Great Britain
will be those prevailing in districts where and by
the firms or Government Departments by whom they
'are employed; pay to date from the day of. com-
mencing duty. Subject to their work and conduct in
England being satisfactory and to suitable vacancies
existing, the selected applicants will receive favor-
able consideration when appointments are being made
for tlie Australian Arsenal.
Applications for enrolment must contain tlie follow-
ing particulars, viz.: Name (in full) and address,
age, birthplace, nationality, whether married or
single, dependents (if any), present occupation, a
short statement of apprenticeship or training and
subsequent experience, a specific statement as .to
where engaged* and the class of work done .during
the past three years, the class of work desired in
England (indicating the-trades or sections of trades
in order of preference), the earliest date on which
the applicant is prepared to embark, together with a
declaration signed by the applicant that he is in
good health and capable of undertaking the work
applied for. Applications must be accompanied by
certified copies of testimonials as to skill and fitness
as a tradesman. Not more than three such testi-
monials are required, but they must he from em-
ployers or responsible persons in the trade. . Two
personal references as to character should be given.
Applications should be lodged as early as possible
with the Secretary, State Munitions Committee,
Harots Buildings, Wynyard Square, Sydney, New
South Wales; Collins House,- Collins Street, Mel-
bourne, Victoria; State Munitions (Advisory) Com-
mittee, William Street, Brisbane, Queensland ; State
Munitions Committee, Tourist Bureau, King William
Street, Adelaide, South Australia; care of Govern-
ment Analyst, Wellington Street, Perth, Western
Australia; Railway Commissioner’s Office, Hobart,
Tasmania, from whom printed forms of application
nav be obtained. ,
AH applications previously sent m are void, and
skilled men desirous of volunteering for munition
work in Great Britain, who previously applied but
were not selected, are requested to submit fresh
applications.
T. TRUMBLE,
Acting Secretary.

Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 11th January, 1917.
Tenders for the Supply of Dynamos,
Switchboard, Motors, Balances and
Control Gear for Power Plant for
Flinders Naval Base.
AMENDED NOT!CE.
TENDERS will be received, subject to the condi-
lions of tendering until 12 noon on Monday, 2nd
April, 1917. for the supply of dynamos, switchboard,
motors, balancers and control gear for Power Plant
for Flinders Naval Base. Delivery to be f.o.r.
Flinders Naval Base.
Tender forms, specifications and full particulars
may be obtained at the Office of the Director of
Navy Contracts, Navy Office, Melbourne ; and at the
Naval Staff Offices, Brisbane; Edgecliff (Sydney) :
Birkenhead (S.A.) : Fremantle and Hobart.
Tenders to be endorsed “Tenders for .... for Power
Plant, Flinders Naval Base,” on top left-hand corner
of envelope, and addressed to the Director of Navy
Contracts, Navy Office, Melbourne ; or to the Director
of Navy Contracts, c/o one of the above-mentioned
addresses, as the case may , be.
The lowest or any tender not necessarily accepted.
.T. A. JENSEN,
Minister of State for the Navy.
