UNIQUE PROCESS FOR
RETAINING AND
REGAINING BEAUTY.
By Mlle. Charlotte Sirier.
Does your skin chap or roughen easily, or become
unduly red or blotchy? Let me tell you a quick and
easy way to overcome the trouble and keep your com-
plexion beautifully white, smooth and soft. Just get
some ordinary inercolised wax at the chemist’s, and
use a little before retiring as you would use cold
cream. The wax, through some peculiar action, flecks
oft the rough, discolored or blemished skin. The
worn-out cuticle comes off just like dandruff on a
diseased scalp, only in almost invisible particles. Mer-
eolised wax simply hastens Nature’s work, which is
the rational and proper way to attain a perfect com-
plexion, so much sought after but very seldom seen.
The process is perfectly simple and quite harmless.
** # *
It is astounding the number of women who suffer
from unsightly growths of hair on the face, and it
will come as a piece of good news to know that
there is a simple substance known as powdered
pheminol which will remove it immediately- and per-
manently. Mix a small quantity into a thin paste
with a little water, and apply to the objectionable
growths. In two minutes all trace of the hair will
have entirely vanished, and your skin will be as soft
and smooth as a child’s.
The hair should be allowed to breathe, and the
gieasy film around each strand must be removed with
a mild non-alkaline shampoo. Soaps should be
tabooed. The very best solution for the purpose can
be made by dissolving a teaspoonful of stallax
granules in a cup of hot water. It stimulates the
scalp to healthy action, and at the same time leaves
the hair in that soft, fluffy condition so much
admired. Any chemist can supply you with an
original packet of stallax, sufficient to make 25 or
30 shampoos.
* >1: * :!=
One need not resort to the very questionable ex-
pedient of hair-dye in order not to have grey hair.
The grey hair can easily be changed back to a
natural color in a few days’ time merely by the
application of a simple, old-fashioned and perfectly
harmless home-made lotion. Procure from ' your
chemist two ounces of tammalite concentrate, and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply this to
the hair a few times with a small sponge and you
Will soon have the pleasure of seeing your grey hair
gradually darkening to the desired shade. The lotion
If pleasant, not sticky or greasy, and de-js not injure
the hair in any way. [2

The Inky Way.
The two Launceston dailies held out when
the Hobart pair put up their price to three-
ha’pence, expecting that the paper market
would tumble presently ; but as it hasn’t, and
judging by the price of pulp isn’t soon going
to, the Examiner and Telegraph have now
moved up alongside the Mercury and Post.
* * * *
“S.T.S.” : Why all this delight in calling
“of” by the artificial name of “o’ ” in poem
and drama? It is supposed to reflect a
colloquial way of speaking; but how far it
is from doing that can be seen by the fact
that two people out of three—women especi-
ally—don’t recognise it at all, but pronounce
it (for example) “Peg Oh My Heart.” The
letter “o” simply 'doesn’t represent the sound
made by “of” with the last letter dropped ;
and, in the few cases where the “f” really
wants dropping, one has to write “kinder”
and “sorter” to give the effect. But really
there are very few such cases. It is an
artificial literary trick, and very properly
is not found with genuine sentiment that
reflects the world as it is. The worst ex-
ample I have-struck is “glory o’ England,”
which nobody ever said naturally, or indeed
could say without checking in the middle.
* * * *
“Beacon for Breakfast” is a Brisbane
Daily Mail misprint. A light meal, cer-
tainly. <-
* * # *
“Fx”: Another illustration of the petty
jealousy of daily journalism. The Age
quoted from London Daily Chronicle con-
cerning the heroic work of Florence Sandes
in Servia. But it said never a word about
Florence being a sister of Johnny, now of
Sydney and for years on the Argus, whose
verses, written for the hated rival paper, have
appeared in book form in Melbourne. It
wasn’t in the nature of the Age to give its
stolen news a local interest by mentioning
brother John.
* * * *
Three years after his arrival in Queens-
land in 1861, Chas. Hardie Buzacott estab-
lished in washed-out Clermont its first news-
paper-—the Peak Downs Telegram, of
which the last very vigorous editor was Joe
Legina, subsequently a member of the
Bananaland Assembly. Buzacott is one of
the grand old men of journalism in Aus-
tralia. It is years since he left his four-
score mark behind. As he emerged from the
seventies the comps on the Brisbane Daily
Mail, to which he was then attached as
consulting-editor, went out on strike in
sympathy with the tram men and the coal
men and all the rest of them. To preserve
the copyright of the paper some matter had
to be set each day, so the old man stood at
the case and picked up a' few sticks of type, to
be dropped into an opened forme, from which
a page was printed for distribution. He
retired from the Courier on a liberal pen-
sion, which he forfeited when he threw in
with the Mail.
* * 7T *
“T.D.” : Commissioner of Police Urquhart,
of Queensland, has dabbled in the ink. He
has published a volume of verse, a number
of stories, sundry sketches (pretty good
ones), and a collection of legends of the
blacks of the North. A slash across the
thigh and a spear-hole in his system handed
him by the aborigines impaired his police
efficiency for a term, but did not discourage
him in his pursuit of the Muse. More than
once he overtook her, but there does not
appear to have been any undue familiarity.
‘“Van Eck”; The verses of “Den” have
taken a further leap into popularity with
pros, earning a crust in vaudeville. A few
weeks ago Clement May. billing himself as
“The Sentimental Bloke,” set out to recite
some of the poet’s verses, but was nipped
in the bud by the copyright act. The latest
exponent is Gwen Lewis, of the Passing
Show (Sydney). This artist, however, gives
only fleeting impressions of “Ginger Mick,”
probably on account of the vaudeville rights
of the verses being held by George Edwardes
of the Fuller circuit. Edwardes was the
first to recite “Den’s” verses. Some ten
years ago. when “The Sentimental Bloke”
was striving for any other recognition than
The Bulletin gave him, Edwardes was do-
ing well with “ ’Urry,” one of the gems pub-
lished in “Den’s” first book, “Backblock
Ballads.” When he trekked to Fogland the
young Australian took “ ’Urry” along with
him and introduced it to English audiences.
On his return to this country he still clung
to “Den” and is now making hay with his
more up-to-date stuff.
MY BOOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No man writes a book without meaning something,
though he may not have the faculty of writing con-
sequentially, and of expressing his meaning.— Addison.
It is complete—the magnum opus finished.
My book is writ; its joys shall be my own—•
These none shall share. Delight is undiminished
In that I revel selfish and alone.
Each line, as written, stands for anxious thinking;
No pencilled sign was casually made.
At times my very soul with fear was shrinking;
Again, I’d write a line and pause dismayed.
Here should be tears—l wrote this line in sorrow—
Here deeper grief, for much was put at stake ;
Oft have I wished each day was its to-morrow,
Oft slept with Fear —-with Care to mate, awake.
At length the end ; 1 wrote in desperation
To simplify the tangle in my skein,
And finished with a powerful situation —
Finale —in my very happiest vein.
No linotypist's inky paws shall fumble
The pages of MY BOOK. No printer scoff
At my poor penmanship ; no reader grumble,
And wish I had been, timely, taken off.
The sacred pages only I shall study ;
And I for Recollection’s joy alone
When I would have the reds of Life more ruddy,
And realise a pleasure shared by none.
I’ll read again the writing, in the double-book
I made; ten thousand was the limit, and one horse
I hadn’t laid (for the cognoscenti knew him, and he
had no chance, they said ; where the quick were under
saddle, he was listed with the dead). But he can-
tered home the winner of the second big event, and
I'motored home “a skinner” —wherewithal I am con-
tent.
N.S.W.
JOHN DRAYTON.

Naturally a Healthy Mother
should feed her own child, and no cause, save inability,

mothers who, though willing, cannot wholly or even
partially, nurse their children. Either they have no
milk for them, or it is poor in quality and deficient
in sustenance.
The Question then arises
what food should be given to Baby. Carefully consider
this highly important point. Remember, that the
right food, given at the proper .age will lay a sure
foundation for future health and happiness. Ordinary
cow’s milk is totally unsuitable as a food for young
infants; it is acid, contains indigestible curd and is
frequently swarming with dangerous germs, especially
in summer time. Farinaceous Foods must not be given, as
a child under seven months of age cannot digest starch.
A Satisfactory Solution

provided by using the ‘Allenburys’ Foods. Prepared
as directed, these pure Foods closely resemble healthy
mother’s milk in composition, nutritive value and
digestibility. They are the outcome of prolonged
scientific investigation, carried out with all the resources
of wide manufacturing facilities and experience.
Decide to use

ailments which folllow the use of unsuitable food will

health and strength.

Creation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THUS it was at eventide.
All my soul was open wide,
And the windows of my heart
Summer’s fingers held apart.
Over trees a music fell
As of some far wind-stirred bell
Tossing in a belfry high,
Built in depths of moonlit sky.
But no wind the near air stirred.
Lightly as a spoken word
Breathed within a lover’s ear
Dropt rose-flakes my casement near ;
And a white moth’s dusty flight
Made faint sound across the night.
Shadowed faces came and went,
Shadow eyes above me bent —-
Ghostly robe and veil and zone —-
Harvest of old dreamings sown
In white hours when bird and wing
Filled the fallowed field of spring.
Scarce I breathed in that still hour,
Strange enchantment rich with power ;
With white hands my longing prest
Lightly to her silken breast.
Suddenly the silence thrilled!
Magic light my spirit filled,
And the forms my dreamings wrought
In full nets of truth were caught.
Worlds were open to my ken,
Thronged with life and breathing men ;
Music from a woman’s throat
Edged with laughter seemed to float,
And strange flutter of strange wings
Winnowed thrones of gods and kings,
While all woes that were and are
Wailed aloud to sun and star.
Blood that all the years had bled,
Tears that all the sins had shed,
Raining wildly over me
Held me in an agony;
But through all there seemed to float
Laughter from a woman’s throat.
Gardens gay with bloom and bird,
Dusts a Pharaoh’s chariot stirred,
Lonely streams whose dappled shores
Heard the plash of pilgrim oars
Ere a royal secret hid
’Neath the oldest pyramid—
These in vision fair and fine
For a holy hour were mine.
Suns of oldest deserts set
By the jewelled minaret;
And the cave-man lean and white
Stared soul-startled through the night.
But through all there seemed to float
Laughter of a woman’s throat.
Some white oread, wet with dew,
Heard the pipes the shepherd blew
On the hillside’s thymv steep
Where the long flocks loved to creep ;
And a little altar sinoKe
Made blue shadows near the oak.
Sun-browned gods, from worship free,
Talked with maids on Ossa’s knee.
Bearded kings with furrowed brow
Taught the oxen team to plough ;
And a sudden clash of shields
Rang across the Spartan fields,
Where a wail of death arose
From a town beset with foes.
There the long-ships near the shore
Moved to wind and slave-strained oar
Bearing plunder of the south —-
Slimmer slaves of redder mouth
And the robes that queens desire,
Purple from the looms of Tyre.
These in vision clear and fine
For a mystic hour were mine;
But through all there seemed to float
Laughter from one woman’s throat!
Horror on the death-choked air!
Pits of darkness and despair
Shudder ’neath the charnel lights,
Where the red worm writhes and bites
And the living dead strive on .
When all things but pain are gone ;
And an awful purpose heaves
Through the slime that sobs and grieves,
As if mammoth forms unborn
Strove to reach the bitter morn
When a hope, in hope’s despite,
Yet might win them air and light
That their stagnant lips might stain
The red mouth of faith again....
Yet o’er all there seemed to float
Laughter of a woman’s throat.
Shadow on the cloistered ways.
Here no idle footfall strays,
But with hood about his ears
Talks, the schoolman with his peers.
Monkish books are stored within ;
Restless quills must strive to win
From the ways the sages trod
All that is of man and God,
Following the labored line
Of a song of loves and wine,
Lest within the script should hide
Earth’s last wonder open-eyed.
Salmon from the flowing stream.
Tonsored heads have caught the gleam
Of a sun that redly sets
Tinging learning with regrets
For the open life and free,
Heedless of philosophy ;
For through all there seemed to float
Laughter of a woman’s throat.
All the things that I had dreamed
In that hour about me streamed,
Clear as in the well of youth
Gleams the jewel light of truth.
I the dream-creator there
Viewed my world through finer air ;
Strange, aloof from that I wrought.
Saw the long, white lanes of thought
Even as God may look to see
Ages fainting at his knee. . . .
Yet through all there seemed to float
Laughter from a woman’s throat.
God whose dream in early spring
Taught the youngest bird to sing,
Filled a garden full of bloom
For the first sweet bridal room.
Winged the planets in their flight—
Must he see as I this night?
Must lie hear o’er all things float
Laughter from a woman’s throat
As the last and perfect thing
Of his vast imagining?
Hear its silver throbbing call
Piercing sweetness through the gall,
And its clear insistence still
Mastering all thought and will?
Must he hear,? —-Ah, God and I,
Dreaming till all dreams run dry,
Faint before the perfect thing
Of our own imagining, .. .
Living woman that I wrote,
By the laughter in your throat,
By the dreams that come and die,
Did God make you or did If
N.s.w.
DAVID McKEE WEIGHT.
Medical Attention.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The back door of the skillion was thrust
violently outward, and a short, globu-
lar person with wrinkled face and
pathetic whiskers burst into view and stum-
bled in awkward haste to the slab stables on
the bank of the waterhole at the rear of the
house.
He ran with short, jerky steps. The slack
of his shirt caught the air and filled like a
balloon ; behind him, like the tail of a Chinese
kite, swung the loose ends of his braces.
The sorrel mare, dozing uneasily upon the
dirt and litter of the stable floor, heard the
approaching footsteps and rose clumsily to
her ancient feet in a sudden wild hope that it
was already time for the midday feed. She
was an old, old animal, but held absurdly to
the remnants of an optimistic youth. With
her rose a swirling cloud of flies'that hummed
discordantly about the head of the little man
as be threw down the stable bar and grabbed
at a headstall.
The mare submitted to the bit like one in a
dream. Only a cynical drooping of the lower
lip denoted the passing of still another illu-
sion. Protesting feebly, she was dragged out-
side into the glaring sunlight and the re-
mainder of the harness hastily flung across
her flea-bitten frame. As she was backed
between the shafts of the buggy a woman’s
voice, calling from the door of the bouse,
brought her master’s head round with a jerk.
“Can’t you ’urry?” it shrilled. “She’s
getting wus; and every minute tells in this
here. And don’t fetch the wrong man by mis-
take, like you done larst time but one. You
’ear me?”
The little man bit savagely at a knot in
the rope reins and swore beneath his breath.
“Orright. Come up ’ere, Blossum ; git over,
darn yer.” Then, as the voice still persisted :
“Orright. Ain’t I told yer? Orright!" lie
shouted the words in a childish passion.
4 Hamn that woman,” lie added sullenly,
pausing to. wipe away the perspiration that
streaked his forehead. “Damn that woman!
Thinks she owns the place. A man might ’s
well be dead!” -
Still muttering, he climbed into his seat,
grabbed the whip and smote the okl mare on
the rump. “Gittup, you, Blossum— Uit /”
As he rounded a corner of the house the
voice floated out to meet him on a sea of
words.
“If you ain't quick, ’er blood be on your
’ead, and so I tell yer. And see you bring
him—drunk or sober. ’Arf a doctor’s better’n
none. And his name is Garret; and your
missus bespoke ’im larse Chewsday fort-
night. I seen the letter myself. And don’t
fergit a bottle of whisky from Doolan’s. A
time like this you never know. Better write
it down. The doctor and a bottle of whisky.
A bottle of whisky and the. ...”
“Oh, damn that woman!” Mr. Stubbs
slashed. savagely at the swarm of insects
about his head. “Damn ! Damn that woman !
You, Blossum— Gittup!”
The old mare shambled along in a smother
of dust, with head well down. The driver
sat hunched forward upon his seat. Every
turn of the wheels gave out a slurring whine
that was almost human in its protest.
Doolan’s pub was the first of the dozen
untidy houses that went to make up the
settlement of Coon’s Gap. Nearly a score
of summers had so twisted and warped its
worm-eaten timbers that it might be said to
bang together only by courtesy. A disrepu-
table, irreclaimable ruin, it- tottered insanely
upon the verge of dissolution.
Beneath the verandah, behind a blind of
ragged hessian, half a dozen men sprawled
in the sweltering heat. Two of them were
playing euchre with a greasy pack of cards,
and the rest looked on with sullen interest.
The door of the bar was wide open; whether
to admit the flies and dust or to let out the
over-night odor of stale beer and tobacco
only the Devil —or Doolan—knew. The odor
was a fat, full-flavored one, and boasted
openly. The men seemed not to notice it.
These things are, after all. purely a matter
of education.
Doolan sat on the mud floor of the veran-
dah with his back against* the wall. He was
wondering how long it would be before the
crowd would be drunk enough to make it
safe to work off some of the sour beer lie had
bought, for a mere song, as salvage from a
burnt-out confrere at Buckety Flat. Sud-
denly he cocked an ear like a terrier.
“In Hiven’s name,” he said, “what’s that
row ?”
“I don’t hear nuttin’,” growled one of the
card-players. “I make it same as larse time.
I don’t hear nuttin’.”
Doolan’s calculations had made him irrit-
able. “How the Hell can you expect to.
anyway, yapping like a lot of blasted dawgs?
Listen- there now.”
This time there was no gainsaving the ex-
traordinary noise that came at irregular in-
tervals. Even Doolan’s untrained ear recog-
nised something weirdly distressing in this
latest dislocation of the peace. While they
still _ speculated profanely as to its origin
the infernal clatter ceased abruptly; there
was a moment’s silence; then the' hessian
blind was hastily torn aside, and a short
globular apparition, with dejected whiskers
and dirty, fly-speckled countenance, precipi-
tated itself into their midst. “A bottle of
whisky AND the doctor. The doctor AND a
.... Oh. damn that woman—-what am I
saying? Where can I find Dr. Garret?”
“Good Lord!” ejaculated Doolan. “It’s
little Stubbs. What’s the matter now?”
“The missus is crook. I gotter fetch the
doctor back ’ome. Where’s Dr. Garret hang
out?” 6
One of the company rose unsteadily to his
feet. He was a large man and insufficiently

IN summer the skin is made tender by
excessive perspiration ; and the badness
imprisoned in the tissue, owing to the
sluggishness of the pores, is drawn out in
the form of pimples, rashes and heat swellings.
The strong air of the country or the seaside
does not always suit the skin of the town
dweller, while a change of water frequently
induces a burning irritation and rash that is
even more discomforting than sunburnt and
peeling arms and face, because this irritation,
provokes rubbing and scratching, which opens
the way for eczema or some poisoned sore.
The best check to these skin troubles is the
regular use of Zam=Buk, which is a pure skin balm.
For removing that tingling irritation known as
Summer Itch, or Prickly Heat, Zam-Buk is invaluable.
Zam-Buk is also highly recommended for taking the sting and smarting
out of insect bites, for it not only soothes pain, but counteracts any
poison that may have been introduced into the system.
Zam-Buk has achieved a world-wide reputation for purity and
effectiveness.

clad. His speech lacked coherency. “’s my
name.” He blinked solemnly upon the trav-
eller. “’s my name, li’le man. Wha’s
marrer?”
Mi\ Stubbs regarded the flabby, unshaven
face before him with open disfavor. It was
obvious that the doctor was very drunk
indeed. Mr. Stubbs, unversed in diplomacy,
said as much.
The accused spluttered indignantly.
“Drunk? Me drunk? Why, you little
runt” He shook a hairy fist in the other's
face.
Doolan interposed coldly. “Of course,
you’re drunk. You always are drunk, if it
comes to that.” He turned to Stubbs. “What
d’ye want with ’im, anyway?”
• “Ter fetch him back ’ome with me; soon’s
the ole mare’s had a feed. • I tell you he’s
engaged all proper and reg’lar. The missus
bespoke ’im munts ago. He’s gotter come.”
“Wha’s her name?” inquired the doctor,
with dignity.
“Stubbs. Missus Henery Stubbs; out
Dry-pool way.”
“Condition?”
“Why, dammit”
“One min’, my fren’.” The doctor fumbled
inside bis vest and produced a ragged note-
book. “Thish ’s mosh irreg’lar. Lesli be
calm. Now, wattle you bet I’m not ri’, eh?”
He fumbled the leaves hazily. “Sinclair’s
cow. Drench, one shill’n’. Miss Jones,
rheumatism; profeshn’l services, 2s. 6d.
Thash not it. Here you are. Missus Stubbs,
expectin’. Now when”—the doctor wagged
a finger at Doolan—“when d’you suppose she
fixed it?”
“How the blazes do I know?” exploded
the publican. “And what's that got to do
with it, anyway?”
“Not for nuzzer fortnight,” said the doctor
with triumph. “There’sh no argument.
Lesli has some’in’ to drink.”
“Are you coming, or are you not?”
squeaked Mr. Stubbs, hysterically.
“No, I’m not. There’sh such a thing as
profeshn’l etiquette, ain’t there?”
“Profeshn’l etiquette be jiggered,” snapped
Doolan. “You make, me tired. Make no
error; you'll go.”
“You’re only a silly layman,” retorted the
doctor. “And I tell you I won’t.”
“Then you can go and sponge for board
somewhere else,” said the publican. “You
owe me two-pound-ten for grub and beer, and
you gotter earn it. Or out you go; now
then.”
“Well, then, gimme a drink, and I will.”
The doctor lapsed into sullen grumbling.
“But ish most unprofeshn’l; I take these
igcnTmOn to witnesh ish most unprofes-
sional.”
“It’s all right,” said Doolan, reassuringly,
to the dubious Stubbs. “His top-piece is
clear enough, though you mightn’t think it.
Matter of fact, lie’s better half-shickered
than most of them sort is sober. He fixed
Hogan’s poddy a treat larse night.”
A scant half-hour afterwards the old mare
was harnessed afresh for the homeward
trip. Dr. Garret hoisted himself unsteadily
over the ivlieel and fell into the back seat,
where he went to sleep.
“Well, s’long!” said Doolan, as the trav-
ellers moved off. “Hope it’s all right with
the missus. And say, when he puts ’is bill
in, you might pay it to me. I’ll see it’s all
right. And, fer Gawd’s sake, grease them
wheels o’ yourn.”
For ten minutes, perhaps, Mr. Stubbs
drove steadily onward. Then, suddenly, he
jerked the mare to a standstill with a vio-
lence which brought his passenger’s head
over the rail of the seat in angry protest.
“Wassermarrer—driving like that?”
“Damn that woman!” Mr. Stubbs’s voice
rose in a wail of anguish. “I kep’ thinkin’
all the time that there was sutliin’ else. I
fergot the whisky.”
“Goo’ thing, whisky. Snlen’id thing.” The
doctor subsided drowsily again. “W’at’ll we
do about it?”
Mr. Stubbs stared hopelessly in front of
him. Suddenly he appeared to make up his
mind.
“Look here, if I walk back fer it will you
mind the mare? If I don’t get it I’ll ’ear
about it fer a week.”
“All ri’. All ri’. Don’t be long.” The
doctor’s voice trailed off into sleepy silence.
Mr. Stubbs looked doubtfully about him
for a minute, then climbed out of his seat
and trudged painfully back. The old mare’s
head sank lower and lower in the blazing sun.
The doctor’s inoutli was wide open, and the
ceaseless drone of the flies was punctuated
by an occasional defiant snore. Presently,
zig-zagging through the air, came an object
which rapidly resolved itself into a swarm of
bees. Now it was overhead. A dozen bees,
separating themselves from the humming
cloud, circled for a second about the mare’s
hindquarters; then, moved by goodness
knows what phase of insect curiosity, effected
a lodgment upon the ragged wisp of tail.
At once things began to happen. Startled, the
- mare backed a step and shook herself
roughly. Instantly half a hundred red-hot
darning-needles pierced her ancient hide, and,
mad with pain and fear, she bolted. It was
a brief display. A hundred yards along a
wheel struck a gum-stump, the mare
swung half-round and pitched sideways
across the shafts, which went with a
snap. For a second she lay, kicking furi-
ously ; then she tore herself loose frpm the
wreckage and disappeared, in a scatter of
dust and bees. After that there was silence
once more.
Mr. Stubbs, plodding wearily back with a
bottle of overproof whiskv under his arm,
rubbed bis eyes in bewilderment as he neared
the legitimate end of his tramp. Muttering
to himself, he surveyed the barren landscape
in dazed fashion till his eye suddenly took
in an ominous heap near a patch of saplings.
Shrieking a malediction he broke into a
run. A belated bee. furious at missing
active service in the recent engagement, met
him as he charged and planted his sting
with precision. But Mr. Stubbs hardly
noticed it. With bulging eyes he surveyed
the untidy wreckage before him. Then he
remembered the doctor and dived frantically
at a pair of legs that protruded from under
the pile.
He was a small man, but despair lent
him strength, and the remainder of the
doctor’s body came out into the open with
a rush. To Mr. Stubbs’s surprise the man
was uninjured. In his relief he shook the
disreputable figure with a violence that did
credit to his outraged feelings.
“What have you bin and done to my
trap?’’ he shouted. “What have you bin
and done to my trap?”
The doctor opened his eyes and closed
them again wearily.
“Go away, li’le man,” he murmured. “By
gosh, what a storm! Go away, li’le man."
“Storm! On a day like this! Talk
sense.”
“Washn’t there a storm?” demanded the
doctor. “Mean to tell me there washn’t a
storm, li’le man?”
“Oh, what’s the good of arguin’?” Mr.
Stubbs mopped his streaming face with the
sleeve of his shirt. “Come on, then —we
gotter walk if it takes us a fortnight. Lord
knows what’s going to be the end of this; I
don’t.”
“My fren’, I’m a sick man. What'll you
For once Mr. Stubbs had a flash of in-
spiration.
“Will you come if I give you a drink,
then, damn yer?”
“My fren’, your diagnos’s correct.
Couldn’t have hit it better myself.” The
doctor’s good nature recovered itself even
as his hand grasped the bottle. “Li’le man,
you’re a sport. Now let’s get a wriggle
on.”
Arm in arm (it was the doctor’s sugges-
tion) the untidy pair took up the burden
of their task. The doctor’s air was one of
grave professional concern, slightly marred,
it must be confessed, by sudden, frequent
lapses into his former attitude of indecision.
At such times Mr. Stubbs, to whom the
whole business was in the nature of, a
dreadful nightmare, would threaten and
cajole, to no apparent purpose. Only by
instant resort to liquid argument could
he induce the diabolical creature at bis
side to forego his loudly-proclaimed inten-
tion of returning to Coon’s Gap. Indeed,
with every step of the way it became in-
creasingly evident that the doctor’s endur-
ance of his plighted word was, in some
mysterious manner, based upon the contents
of the bottle under his arm.
It was after dark when they reached the
end of the journey. The Voice met them
at the door with a shrill volley of abuse.
“What have you bin up to? Cornin’ ’ome
like this here, at this time of night. You
can thank your stars; Stubbs, you ain’t .
branded Cain tins night. Only fer me being
on hand....and fighting, too —my goodness,
look at the man’s nose.”
“Lemme see my patient,” demanded the
doctor. “Damme, I didn’t come here to
listen to this! What’s his nose to me? I
shay, lemme see the patient.”
“Oh, you can come in, if you like,” said
the woman, wearily. “The baby come an
hour ago, but there’s no ’arm in ’aving a
look at ’er, I s’pose. It’s not me will foot
the bill. And small thanks to either of you
she’s all right. Call yourself a father, do
you, Stubbs?”
As the doctor disappeared into the dimly-
lit room beyond, Mr. Stubbs, with whom
the limit of endurance had long since
passed, retired raving into the background.
On the, still night air the words of his fer-
vent and oft-repeated petition lost nothing
of emphasis:
“Damn that woman!"
The hills and rocks caught up the sound
and sent it echoing between the trees till
it died away in a blasphemous murmur. It
was the requiem of the little man’s final
repudiation of human benevolence. Then
silence resumed her sway.
Two hours later the midsummer moon rose
resplendently over the tree-tops, tinging the
landscape with a mellow radiance. It laid
ghostly fingers upon the little house and
shone white upon a scrap of pape.r fastened
crookedly upon a wall of the skillion : —-

To Dr. James Garret.

a yearling heifer out of that dun cow of yours, if
you’ll deliver it at Doolan’s for me.
Now it crept on across the barren ridges
of the plain. Beneath its genial touch the-
shadows broke away and vanished into sil-
very nothingness. All except one heavy blot
that resolved itself into the figure of a man
plodding unsteadily over the rough ground
in the direction of Coon’s Gap. He was a
large man and insufficiently clothed, but
he sang as he went, and waved an object
that glistened seductively in the moonlight.
BERNARD CRONIN.
The Ballad of the Skeleton.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I AM the nude that has never a blush,
For shames and tremors I’m all too
bare;
The dfooping lashes, the sudden flush,
And the breath a-flutter with passion’s
flare—
I have dropped all these for the steady stare
And the grin at a joke that never will pall.
For the attitude brazenly debonair,
And I have the best of it, after all.
The heart’s commotion, the blood’s hot rush,
And the dimpled'cloak that I used to wear —
The rich flesh glowingly warm and lush —
It is dust to dust. It is grave-worm’s fare.
And I ask myself: Do I really care?
The worms are welcome to keep their haul,
Death’s sombre menace is mostly scare,
And I have the best of it, after all.
In the crowded earth, in the graveyard’s
crush,
I have shed such trifles as lips and hair.
I have shed my follies and freaks and gush,
And much that my vanity thought was fair;
But I’ve gained a sense of the humorous
there,
And a chaste, white body none craves to
maul,
•Intense sincerity, candor rare,
And I have the best of it, after all.
*
Envoy.
Sister, laugh if you wish....and dare:
Your flesh is a rose. . . .and the roses fall;
I unto you am as wheat to tare,
And I have the best of it, after all.
VICTORIA.
SYD C.
